THE RED PAGE.
Voices from the Basket.
AY that ninety percent, of offered verse
finds its way to the waste-basket, or
is shown a way —what is it like?
Well, most of sit is flat, uninteresting
-the ' rhymed commonplace of uninterest-
ig people—neither bad nor good, and con-
iquently damned utterly. Very bad verse
i sbmetimes interesting by reason of its
adness —the worse it is, the more human it
i : it does n’t conform with the rules of
Lterature, but it has a heart and a pulse,
fluent, feeble mediocrity—that is the thing
xecrable ; for the writers have lost Nature
without gaining Art. The “loyal poetry”
lere recently ■ printed was not without
ts peculiar virtue: it was earnest, for the
nost part; it was felt. When a man feels,
t is odds on his attainment of some measure
jf expression; to feel is to write, given
practice enough. To-be-sure, there are
minds which can never appreciate the gold-
smith’s work of. Taste : subtle shades are
beyond their vision, subtle harmonies beyond
their ears. ' That matters little if there be
sufficient Taste to refrain from the more
obvious blunders. Byron survives, and
good judges praise him, though he penned
probably not one line which a high
canon would hold faultless. He sur-
vives in virtue of his passion, his force ;
and no “ lapidary style ” can supplant
these, or compensate for their absence from
verse. The body is always more than the
raiment. What you have to remember is
that to fully and fitly express a round
emotion in square words is an impossible
thing. There is an eternal conflict between
Feeling and Style. You want to find the
exact poise which will gain most Style while
sacrificing least Feeling ; and the balance
varies. Style is the preservative ; but
only Feeling is worth lyrical preservation.
(Thought, unless sublimated to Feeling,
belongs to Prose.) So the verse-architect’s
problem is very like that which confronts the
naval architect : you have to gain speed,
but. without losing cargo-capacity ; and the
theoretical line of grace is continually being
, subordinated to practical utility.
Here, mow, is a piece of verse which would
be basketed with regret, even from the
merely literary point of view : from the
human point of view it was preserved for
this occasion of print. It comes from that
storehouse of never-to-be-written romance,
Rookwood CN.S. W.) Benevolent Asylum.
He might be Scottish ; and then again he
mightn't. Bat there is no doubt about the
Scot in George Reid, one of whose admirers
presents a cherished copy of his “Poem.”
The ardent young Cobdenite of the seventies
produced several Freetrade, economical
“ poems ” which earned fugacious fame in
the newspapers; but the “Lines addressed to
New South Wales on the opening of the First
Australian International Exhibition ” appear
to be the only ones embalmed for pamphlet
perpetuity. Printed in Syd. Evening News
I Sept. 17, 79, they were reprinted by
ibbs, Shallard, and circulated by the author
whose prefatory note declares that he “ is
lite aware that he runs risk of
lyerse criticism in publishing a * maiden
Fort ’ of this kind.”
The “lines” are rather better than Fitz-
bbon’s, and not quite so good as Parkes s.
hero are some 220 limping Alexandrines,
imposed with labour and sweat—like these .
enial hostess of a world-invited throng,
a thou the only goddess of my humble song,
or in a presence bright and beautiful as thine,
one can lament the absence of a classic shrine.
nd fraught with happy fates was the eventful
hour, . ,
hat saw thy virgin form embraced by lari tain s
hy sunny plains—so long the paradise of brutes,
Gil now be bless’d with Cultivation’s choicest
fruits ; .
.nd where the horrid fires of cannibals had
glow’d, ... , i
erchance become of Christ-like love the calm
abode.
Enter Cobden—
jet all who seek to make a manly people great,
$y in; ermeddling bribes and solemn acts of State,
n thy unpamper’d youth and buoyant powers be-
hold,
That health of commonwealths is neither bought
nor sold !
Iklas ! the space that shields thee from all foreign
harms,
Elides from the outer world thy most attractive
charms ;
indeed in Paris but a very short time back,
Some of thy daughters were disowned because not
black! : 'L'-
Long lines mean easy rhymes ; but this
versified speech is not made without diffi-
culty, and by the time he reaches the Expo-
sition the author is tiring.
Here, massive motors whirl a wilderness of
wheels—
There, wondrous trifles to the gentler taste
appeal. ■ \< :
It ’s near enough. The “ lines ’’—solid,
shrewd, sensible, unimaginative, like the
author and his nation —close with King
Charles’s head
This harmony of force was early understood
By that first ’mong modern princes— Albkrt the
■ Good’; . ,
Bright harbinger of the restored, primeval plan,
That seeks the freedom yet the brotherhood of
man ! v . k
And the basket is still overflowing. “ The
rest would fill a vohim.lv, but it s neither
here or then.”
THE BOOKFELLOW.
THE WALERMAN'S TREE.
I The Chorus :
Chill on his face the death mist was stealing,
L : mp were the hauls lyin > helpless an l cold,
The haze o’er his eyes came slowly revealing
We soon would carry him over the Wold.
The Walerman :
Bend over and listen and hold the word law :
Gather a dozen boys, strong, tall and bold,
Let six mare;i behind and six march before,
And keep even step as I go o’er the wold.
And get the old lorry and harness the bays ;
Two you can do, but the four would be bold :
And step it out briskly—but keep me two days—
Tnen step strongly out as 1 go o’er the wold.
And polish the harness all sheeny and black,
Give the hosses a clip and shine ’em like gold
All hairy and shaggy !—with them on the track
I’d look a rare fright as I went o’er the wold.
You must paint up the lorry, fairy and span—
(She ’ll make a good show when she comes to b
sold)
For years yet to come they ’ll tell yarns o the mar
Who went with four bays his last way o’er tht
wold.
A sawn board coffin so the worms won’t come thro
The ironbark joints till I canker to mould,
It’s an old man’s right and an honest man’s d ue—
Is a right tight shell as he goes o’er the wold.
Then take my old duck gun and clean out the
grime
And fire a half-charge as I go in the mould,
And step it right briskly and firmly to time—
But you ’ll fire her once as you start o’er the
I wold!
Bind small branches o’ the wattle in blossom—
Your father’s a Waler tho’ ninety years old !
Bind to the bays so in stepping they ’ll toss ’em
And they ’ll be my plumes as I go o’er the wold.
And stick in my hat a twig o’ the blossom,
(The band’s nearly broken—you ’ll see it will
hold) ‘ v'
Toss’t in with me; it’s the skin of a ’possum
Brought down by my gun as I went o’er the
wold.
Remember your dad was a boy in his prime—
Sing “John Brown’s Body” right airily and
bold: , „
And you’ll make ’em keep right I—why, bless me,
the time
Will be just the thing as we pass o’er the wold.
And grease those dry boxes and stop the loud
Else “ John Brown’s Body ” would fall flat as it
rolled :
And don’t let me bog in the mud o’ the creek,
But take me right airily over the wold.
I don’t want the parson to pilot me thro’,
Nor suavely stretch his gloved paw for my gold;
When my Jean named ye did they sprinkle their
dew ?
Eh ? We won’t have the parson over the wold.
And plant a good wattle seed over my grave,
Take a big hatful o’ the creek’s rot and mould ;
It will give her a growth right airy and brave,
And show you the place where we lie o’er the
wold.
Wrap me a blanket, a blanket around me ;
Oh I am tired boys ! so tired and cold ;
I think ... I see your old mother before me
Beckoning me near wherg she lies o’er the wold.
The Chorus.
Wran him a long sheet, a winding sheet round him;
Coffin him in—the Walerman hoary and old:
Best at the last: ah! long rest where they ’ll ground
him,
Down deep in the hole where he ’ll lie o’er the wold.
There the dingoes howl at the dawn of the day,
When the shivering dogs skulk the winter's cold ;
There the crows seek shade as a rest by the way ;
But there’s rest for the Walermanon the wold.
William Robert Campbell.
Admire the truth of it, the strength of it,
the stern, tramping rhythm of it—its rugged
majesty, as of some scarred, weather-beaten
visage of the people, the toilers, grown grand
in conflict. That is a piece of verse to be
treasured beyond many of the prettily-
pranked artificialities of our literary popin-
jays. Why 1 Because it holds the writer’s
virile Force : it is muscular and massive ; if
rough-hewn, it is hewn from the rock.
But there is reason to question the origi-
nality : more probably it was re-written from
an old model—some clanking chant of the
iron “ early days.” For here is what looks
like another variant of the original—soma-
' what trimmer and tidier (as becomes the
nautical sphere), and less impressive—
THE SAILOR LAID LOW.
I ’ve grown old sailing over the ocean ; •
My blood’s growing chilly and dry ;
So good-bye to the sea’s rough commotion !
For to-morrow I’m going to die.
Roll me up in the old spare gaff-topsail!
Place a shot at my head and my toe !
Then sink me deep down in blue water
Where the swift tides may over me flow.
Gfet me twenty-five jolly good fellows
In their own boats to form a long row,
And let every manjack strain his bellows
Singing this poor old shellback’s laid low.
Bring along a large case of blue ruin,
That my mourners may never feel dry ;
Bring a cask of Joe Harley’s last brewing,
And give me a good drink ere I die.
After death I am going to Heaven,
To the land where there’s rivers of rum ;
There my wings will be sixes by seven
And the winds through my tail-feathers hum.
I go straight to that sweet land of beauty
Where there never was any hard graft—
Where Tobacco and Rum’s free from duty,
And the same tucker’s for’ard and aft.
So, farewell to this blooming old hooker,
To the captain, the mates, and. the crew !
To the cook, which his name’s Billy Booker,
And the poor little cabin boy, 'too !
Roll me up in the old spare gaff-topsail l
For I know that I have got my call;
My wing and tail feathers are sprouting—
I am off, so good-bye to you all!
I Possibly some reader can give a clue to the
historic source of both versions ? .
Come to the bush man’s rhyme of to-day.
The songs that are sung in the huts and on
the camps have little to do with Literature
with an L ; but they are often racy, often
graphic, and always close to the aboriginal
emotions. “ Songs”—“sung”—for the first
thing that the typical bushman does with a
piece of verse is to set it to some tune slum-
bering in his brain, and drone it through his
nose for the benefit of listening mates or
gum-trees. His song corresponds with pic-
ture-writing : it is the Word audible instead of
the Word visible. He has to utter in order
to fully comprehend and feel. By the
typical bushman is not meant the up-to-date
shearer with his latest magazines, or the
digger with his civilised interest in f/ie Bul-
letin; but the Bush-grown, Bush-ro*ted pro-
duct, the nomad tethered in the limits of the
cattle-track, the shepherd stagnant among
out-station sheep, or the man hidden all his
days among the gullies and the ranges, in a
world bounded on the one side by the re-
mote township, on the other by the great Aus-
tralian silence. After all that has been saidand.
written, we know little more of thin type of
bushman than the fact that he chants “The
Wild Colonial Boy ” —a ballad whose verses
must by this time stretch on to infinity,
since it is a kind of boa-constrictor ballad,
which swallows, and digests, and incorpor-
ates all small-fry verses which come within
the ken of its adepts. Here, from the
basket-mass, is a typical piece to be sung by
this typical bushman.
(THE SQUATER AN: KANGAROO.)
(A Muse.)
A s a squater took a ride one-day across his run
some kangaroo s espied that were basking in the
sun
as in the tall grass contented like they lay
The squater took a ride across and to them did
say
Begone from here you kangaroos quickly with
sp6ed
and a warning i will make to you for eating all
my feed
when again i come this way i will bring dogs an
gun
Then for it mu ’ll dearly pay if i find you on this
run
Then up there sprang a kaingaroo who to the
squatter came
his color was a reddish hue with gigantic frame
he bounded through the greenwood while com-
rades looked on
then before the squater stood this champion'
Those words unto the squater thus this kangaroo
did say
you say that you will slaughter us when next you
come this way
and us to destroy you will bring dogs an gun
But your threats i defy i will not leave this run
Both the dogs an the squater are coward to the
base'
our weaker ones to slaughter they are galnt on
the chase. .
on this run i was bred an here to will 1 die
when perchance some piece of led will give me
my death cry
Three of your greyhounds already i have slain
and yonder near those sandy mounds they are
bleaching on the plain
here alone do i stand get down from your mare
and battle with me hand to hand aproach me if
you dare
No no did the squater say that i will never do
for you have proved this day a noble kangaroo
you an your mates may roam at will an never
more know strife
for a kangaroo ill never kill you are safe from
dogs or rife
The old kangaroo is living still be seen often in
the bends , .
and with his mates he roams at will amongs ms
wooly friends
nothing for to greave them i will close this muse
So peace we leave them an give them our
adues.
David Harford.
There is romance in that, if you could
hear it chanted more on less hiccupically
from the Boulia pub-verandah on the night
of the races. The pieces which follow are
less characteristic, more sophisticated by
“the coast.”
BUSHMAN IN TOWN.
A bushman stepped into a pub
not to have a bust
but to see the barmaid
if she did give trust
the barmaid give a surly look
and sulky she said
have you not heard the news
that poor trust is dead
trust is dead the bushman said
when did the poor man die
it is hard luck for you must know
that lam so dry ~ ’
yo’ 11 get no drink the barmaid said
with a sulky frown
why don’t, you do a bit of work
and not loaf round the town
the bushman who was full of fun
no longer could resist
and soon a scoie of sovereigns
filled his sunburnt fists
then the barmaid smiled and said
you took me by surprise
I’ll take back what I said
I do appolijize
appolijize the bushman said
who was full of pranks
as the barmaid filled a glass
the bushman said no thanks
the barmaid almost stupified
stood there in dismay
and as the bushman left the beer
he smiled and said good day.
That is good “bush,” but not the best.
Another is from an older hand, and twanging
rather English than Australian ; yet it is
“The Song of the Open Road” from all
Australian point of view.
TRAMPS LIFE.
I am an Australian Working man
This country for to roain
All 1 got is a swag and a Billy Can'
And a gum tree is my home
I tramped across the Mountians blue
My Bundle on my back
And a days work I cannot get to do
Till I get to the Stations Back
I have to camp in frost and snow
In a wild be wild red place
And every where that I will go
line looked on in disgrace
So ail folks pitty the poor tramp
While his weary life he’ll spend
The Moon that Shine is his parler lamp
And the Brite sun his best friend
Its when I wake, line feeling old
As by a log I’me lyeing
I am almost perrished with the cold
Till the welcome sun will shine
Its then I rise my humble head
My quivering body shake
As I will leave my lonely bed
And no breakfast have to take
Ive seldom got enough to eat
Bad cloths, I have to wear
And the weary roads I dayle beat
And no Body seem to care
I call to the farmers dwelling
' Some tucker for to ask
In my pocket theres not a shilling
So it is a terriable task
Some times I am refused right strate
And ordered from the door
And as I pass out through the gate
Around me the dogs do roar
Some times the farmer ask of me
What sort of work I seek
And hard enough Oh he will be
To offer five Bob a week
Oh this I think is very Cruel
And you might all plainly see
I think that I would be a fool
To scab my country
The road .is wide through many a hill
My feet and back are sore
And with hardship myself I’ll Kill
Before the wages I will lower
I’ll Tramp to the North of Queensland
And die beside a log
Before I will turn my hand
And be a rich maus dog.
That lias not the Australian emotion : as a
matter of fact, there are very few Austra-
lian-born swagmen. The local youth has a
nest, or cuts a niche ; but in poverty he is
stationary, when he travels it is for profit.
“He knows a better game than swagging ”
—even if it be “spieling. 1 '
Embarrassment of riches ! —and space is
filling. But this heart-felt “poem” must
have place.
THE DEATH OF WABGHOPE.
He fell at the head of his Columb
While cheering on his men,
his brave deeds would fill a volumb
but its neither here or then
He knew when, he got the order
oh that Cold and Chilly morn
er’d he’d Cross the foe mens border \
a corpse he would be borne
But I will do my duty
I am here But to obey
then follow me my beauties
we’l attack at dawn of day -»
But he fell at the first volly
of that memorial fight
this hero who always was jolly
Who was always so cheerful and Bright
He fell mortaly woundend
Pei reed through with shot and shell
Brave m m I expected it sooner
for it don’t blame me lads Farewell
Those Brave men of Tartan and kilt
who fight fiercest when there blood as been spilt
with heads bowed low and a tightness of chest
they laid ther Comander away to rest,
and as they stood round that new made grave
this question often was asked
and the pipes seemed to say as sweetly they played
revenge sweet revenge to the last,
ther foe men outlined on the crest of the hill
instinctive each man raised his eye
W ith sorrow and grief each Breast was filled
you could read revenge in each eye.
As they moved away with a quick firm tread
from the grave of there comrade they loved
With knit brows and a look that said
no good for the foes on the hill above.
For they have carried the sway on many a day
their courage there only sheild
when foes ran away in fear and dismay
from the Charge of the icy cold steel.
Thomas Summersgill.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Return.
Beloved, the summer is with us,
The breezes blow soft from the sea ;
The roses, are scenting my garden,
And blooming for thee.
The swallows are waiting to greet thee,
So come ere they fly with the rain !
And let us forget with the summer
And love once again.
Beloved, the summer is dying,
The roses have fall’n in the night,
And out in my garden the petals
Lie withered and white.
So haste thy return, my belov’d one
(The breezes blow chill from the sea),
And bid, with the last of the swallows,
Adieu unto me.
“ Dear heart, I am here in thy garden !
Fast, fast at thy call have I sped
To share in thy joy of the summer ! ”
But summer was dead !
8. A.
ALICE GRANT ROSMAN.
Ballade of Constancy.
One moment wait upon the bridge
That spans the rippling clear lagoon,
While slowly o’er the purple ridge
Climbs steadily the waning moon.
Ah. love ! it seems but yester-eve
Love came upon us suddenly—
An idle hour our hearts to weave—■
An hour of idle ecstasy.
Nor Devil nor Divinity
Must serve to keep us two apart;
Nor Time nor all Eternity
Should heal that dear delicious smart.
Ah, love ! alas for constancy !
Another heart’s beneath vour feet,
Whilst I fulfil the comedy
And call another maiden sweet.
N.S.W.
H.B.S.
Two Graves at Port Davey.
Ever moaning, moaning sadly,
As I sit within the bay,
I can hear the ocean, madly
Lifting high its stormful spray.
Out beyond the rocky pillars, lo! I hear it far
away.
Oh, I never heard it moaning
With such sad and bitter wail,
As it were some spirit groaning,
Fragments of some piteous tale
Of the tempest-tossed and dying, wringing hands
without avail.
Weirdest scene of desolation,
Wildest hills and shrieking sea !
None hath here his avocation,
Not a bird and scarce a tree,
But two graves are on the sandbank, comrades in
obscurity.
Wreckage from old Ocean’s slaughter,
Stranded here for unknown years;
Name and fame are writ in water,
Though a piece of pine appears
With some faded letters on it, which I scarce
cna read for tears.
Fifty years or more, if rightly
I the fallen fragment read,
Here they lay ; while for them, nightly,
Ever since, above their head,
Sigh the breezes, and the Ocean sings a requiem
for the dead.
All the night our barque seemed rocking,
As if moved with speechless grief,
Or with mimic music mocking
Hoarser tones upon the reef,
Art and Nature here combining, singing for the
soul’s relief,
O’er these outcasts from the sainted
Who in God’s own acre rest,
Tn a broken tin some painted
Amaranthine flowers were pressed
By some horny-handed seaman, whom my heart
devoutly blessed.
These were faded, but unfading
Was his deed before my eyes,
Prompted by that grace pervading
Hearts that God doth canonise,
For the willing cup of water to the Master in
disguise.
So I slept until the morrow,
Loving more this lonely spot,
And in dreams forgot my sorrow
Since the dead were not forgot
Even here, in this wild graveyard, where the
mourner cometh not.
TAS.
J. BUFTON, G.F.S.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Mnoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
■SI, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of\\d. postage, the subscription
to Tub Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease of in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage —lJrt. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of Tub Bulletin is no w sent to Queensland.

publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that toe
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-draum —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under ivhich
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 29, 1901.
How Britain is Getting Left.
While the unburied ghost of the Fiscal
Issue stalks abroad in Australian politics the
question of the alleged decadence or other-
wise of Britain under its Freetrade policy is
a subject of endless and rather weary con-
troversy. Wild and woolly Foreign-traders
from N.S.W. allege that the magnificent and
unparalleled prosperity of Britain is a solid
proof of the truth of the tariff principles
which they never practised in their own
State. Many Protectionists, on the other
hand, hold that the great and obvious slump
of Britain is a conspicuous evidence that
Freetrade is a lost faith and a dead super-
stition. But many considerations go to
make up the case either way, and many of
the politicians interested are crude and
whirling persons who don’t know their own
subject, and who, for want of study, have to
invent their frantic evidences as they go
along. Moreover, it is generally twenty
years or more after a nation really begins to
lose ground before the process is unmistak-
ably apparent. It is necessary to stand off
at the distance of a decade or so before
things can be viewed at the proper angle, so
to speak.
• • * *
Yet within the last year or so some start-
ling things have happened. Britain; for
years and years the money-lender of the
earth, has actually been borrowing money in
the United States with which to carry on
the war in South Africa. Several foreign
loans, of the kind which used to be issued in
London as a matter of course, have been lately
floated in New York, which is seriously dis-
puting with London the billet of the world’s
financial centre. New South Wales has
even lately been invited to place its foreign
loans in New York, with a promise of better
terms than London can give. Something
like 30 years ago the United States, for the
first time, seriously challenged Britain’s
supremacy as a manufacturing state ; and it
has since then so utterly left its rival
behind that in 1896 (no later returns
are available) its output of manufactured
goods was worth £1,980,000,000 per annum
(equal to, roughly speaking, £29 worth
for every inhabitant) against Britain’s
£876,000,000 (equal to about £22 worth per
inhabitant). In fact, as a manufacturing
country the United States now 7 beats the whole,
British empire badly. Last year, for the first
time in history, the United States beat Great
Britain and Ireland as an exporter of its
own produce to foreign markets ; its exports
of its own goods increased by £40,500,000 in
one year, against the United Kingdom’s
increase of barely £27,000,000, and
the supremacy which the United King-
dom held against all-comers for gene-
rations has disappeared at last. Still
more recently two other noteworthy
events have been recorded. For Britain to
own foreign railways has long been a matter
of course, but now one of the London city
railways has passed into American hands,
and at least one other is likely to pass
shortly, which is a strange up-ending of the
old order of things. And the United
States, after for many years leaving
Britain the unquestioned mistress of the
seas, now calmly announces a determination
to make the Atlantic an American lake. As
a preliminary step the fourth largest ship-
owning concern in Britain has been bought
up bodily by American owners, together with
a controlling interest in another very large
British shipping concern. A third huge
British shipping co. has been bought up
bodily by Germans, who now own, in the
Hamburg-Amerika line and the Norddeut-
scher-Lloyd, the two largest shipping con-
cerns in the world, even the smaller of them
being far ahead of any single British enter-
prise of its kind.
• * • *
There was a time when the Briton held
control of the sea through his superior apti-
tude for a seafaring life, and in those days any
serious attempt at foreign competition might
reasonably be denounced as absurd. But
things have changed in later years, and the
proposal to make the Atlantic an American
lake is less ridiculous than it seems.
Out of the 244,135 seamen who manned
Britain’s ships at the end of 1899, 33,805
were Asiatics (Statistical Abstract of the
United Kingdom for 1899, page 179).
Between 1896 and 1899 the Asiatic sailors in
British ships increased by 3806, while the
white sailors diminished by 1710 (same
authority and same page). Of the white
sailors, an immense but unknowm proportion
are Scandinavians, Germans, Dutchmen,
Greeks, Italians, &c. And any foreign
nation, with the needful money and commer-
cial energy, can buy ships and hire all these
people to man them quite as easily as
Britain can. Commercial supremacy on the
sea may now go any day to the nation with
most commercial energy and most money.
And the United States kept its word so
emphatically wben it undertook to deprive
Britain of its manufacturing supremacy that
it would be rash to regard its present under-
taking as impossible.
* * * *
Even before the Germans and Americans
bought up two of Britain’s great steam
fleets, some curious things were happening
in the old land. The number of vessels
belonging to the United Kingdom decreased
from 23,662 in 1885 to 20,196 in 1899 (Statis-
tical Abstract of the United Kingdom
for 1899, page 175), the fall being con-
tinuous all along—though as the vessels are
larger, and the total tonnage increased
materially, this is of less importance than
might appear. The point that is of real im-
portance is that Britain is no longer keeping
its hold on the trade of its own ports as it
used to do. This is the total tonnage of
British and foreign vessels engaged in trade
with foreign countries and British possessions,
which entered and cleared at British ports
in 1896 and 1899 (Statistical Abstract of
the United Kingdom for 1899, page 164) :
British British F , Foreign

From 1885 (where the figures of the Statisti-
cal Abstract commence) to the end of 1895.
Britain kept securing a larger and larger
share of the trade of its own ports, but
since then the movement has been all the
other way. The last year of the four (1899)
was also the worst. In that year the
British shipping entering and clearing from
the United Kingdom for foreign and colonial
ports increased by 1,432,261 tons ; the
foreign shipping increased by no less than
5,386,660 tons—in other words, during 1899,
foreigners annexed nearly 80 per cent, of
the new shipping trade in British ports. In
1898 the position was even more remarkable.
The British shipping engaged in the foreign
and colonial trade whicli entered and left
United Kingdom ports decreased by 209,817
tons, while the foreign shipping increased
by 974,920 tons.
• * • •
The coastal trade of Britain is still almost
wholly in British hands, and there .the
foreigner is only a.n atom- —not much of an
atom either. But even there the order of
things has changed a little since 1896. Wit-
ness the following from the Statistical
Abstract of the United Kingdom for 1899,

• • * »
Another point of interest to those who
study the alleged decadence of Britain is its
wonderful mass of imports as compared with
its very much smaller exports. About this
question a vast amount of misconception
exists. It is not necessarily a good sign nor
a bad sign that exports should exceed im-
ports ; nor is it necessarily a good or bad
sign that imports should exceed exports.
The situation may be summed up in these
six propositions :
(1) If exports exceed imports it may be a good sign, sig-
nifying that a country is very rich and is investing its
surplus money abroad.
(2) If exports exceed imports it may be a bad sign, sig-
nifying that a country owes much money abroad, and the
surplus exports are required to pay interest.
(3) If exports very largely exceed imports it may be a
better sign, signifying that the country can not only spare
enough surplus exports to pay interest on its foreign
debts, but also something over with which to pay off the
debts themselves.
And on the other hand :
(4) A surplus of imports may be a bad sign, signifying
that a country is buying more than it can pay for, and is
heaping up a great foreign debt. (This was the case in
Australia in earlier days, when it was piling up its monu-
mental mass of outside liabilities.)
(5) A surplus of imports may be a good sign, signi-
fying that a country has much money owing to it abroad,
and that the interest is coming in in the shape of goods.
(6) A larger surplus of imports may be a bad sign again,
signifying that the same country is not only getting back
its interest in goods and eating that up, but that it is
also getting back some of the principal of its loans in
goods, and is eating them up—eating its past accumula-
tions, in fact, and becoming poor again.
» » * *
And this is the curious record of the
United Kingdom in recent years (Statisti-
cal Abstract of the United Kingdom for
1899, page 49, except as regards the figures
for 1900, which are from the Royal Statis-
tical Journal) :
Exports Surplus
Imports. (including of
Re-Exports). Imports.
1885 .. £370,967,955 £271,474,308 £99,493,647
1890 .. 420,691,997 328,252,118 92,439,879
1895 .. 416,689,658 285,832,407 130,857,251
1899 .. 485,035,583 329,534,658 155,500,925
1900 .. 523,634,000 354,550,000 169,084,000
This stupendous increase of nearly £77,000,000
in the surplus of imports since 1890 can’t be
accounted for by the increased earnings of
British shipping, which has only begun since
then to feel the stress of foreign competi-
tion. It can’t be accounted for on the
assumption that Britain has, in nine years,
lent about 1200 million pounds extra to
foreign countries, and is getting in all
this extra interest in consequence, for such
a sum is wildly ridiculous. The increased
gold influx from South Africa, which is the
only unusual streak of luck Britain has had
in recent years, only accounts for about
£9,000,000, or one-seventh of the whole in-
crease. The only visible explanation is that
France, Germany, and the United States
(principally the two latter), and some other
prosperous States that used to be heavily
mortgaged to Britain, are paying off their
debts, and thatßritain, to that extent; is eating
up its past accumulations and becoming
poorer. And the spectacle of American
financiers taking up'British loans, and reach-
ing out for Britain’s position as loan-monger
to other nations, and intruding in British
railway ownership, and utterly wiping out
Britain’s manufacturing supremacy, and of
Americans and Germans and other nations
seizing upon an increasing share of the trade
of British ports, lends color to this view of
the problem.
* * * *
Another notable point is that Britain’s
exports of manufactured goods are falling
away. Between 1890 and 1899 its exports
only increased by about £1,282,000. But
its export of coal increased by £4,000,000 ;
of herrings, by over £900,000 ; of raw hides,
by over £250,000; and so on down the
list of raw materials ; while its sales of
manufactured goods are diminishing. On the
other hand, its imports of manufactured goods
are increasing enormously; being already
dependent on foreign States for its food it is
also learning to be increasingly dependent on
them for its manufactures. The situation is
a curious one. The United Kingdom hope-
lessly lost its once commanding position as
an agricultural State a good many years ago.
About 1870 or 1875 the United States left it
behind as a manufacturing State, and be-
tween 1870 and 1880 Germany began to pro-
gress so much more rapidly in this line of
industry than Britain that it became only a
matter of time when it would also get in
front. About 1880 Britain lost its position
as the wealthiest country on earth, the
United States taking the lead. But still it
remained the world’s greatest sea-carrier;
the greatest commercial State on this planet;
the centre of the world’s finance ; the State
which had the biggest mortgage on the out-
side globe. Now, the United States and
Germany both propose to fight seriously
for commercial supremacy on the sea.
The United States is beginning to bid
for the billet of the world’s financier and
money-lender ; there are signs that Britain’s
mortgage on the globe is diminishing a little
—the United States alone is reported,
within the last five years, to have paid oft'
£200,000,000 of its liabilities to its old-time
creditor ; and last year, for the first time in
an immense period of history, Britain
dropped into second place as an exporter of
its own products.
» * • *
These things, all added together, may
mean the actual beginning of a decline, or
they may simply mean that Britain is still
progressing but is no longer putting up the
best rate of progress. The latter theory may
mean actual decline later on or again it may
not. There is generally an interval ot a good
many years after a State ceases to e
definitely first before it becomes definitely
second or third —a hazy period when lis
difficult to say how far the matters in which
it still leads counterbalance those in which
it has : fallen to second or third or
fourth place. Britain is in that hazy intei-
mediate stage now. The situation is only
definite enough to make it certain that the
present isn’t a good time to talk of the
United Kingdom as an overwhelming proof
of the advantages of any fiscal policy. In its
old Protectionist days Britain was so far
ahead of every other country in everything
(with the possible exception of agriculture,
and it held a high position even in that
respect) that there was no unpleasant haze
whatsoever.
“This is a Young Country.”
“ Yet it is evident to all who give themselves the
trouble to think'that, in a young and comparatively un-
developed country like this, a certain expenditure out of
permanent loans for public works is not only prudent but
necessary.” — Premier See, of N.S. W., at Graftonr ~
The Bulletin has sometimes had an idea that
it would be a healthy tiling if it could be arranged
that every writer and politician should write or
speak with a half-ton weight suspended over his
head, and whenever he states that this is a young
country the weight should fall automatically and
obliterate him. It is an unwholesome thing for
any land to eternally pose as a young country,
just as it is for any man to pose as a Boy
.Politician. The habit of being a young country is
doing Australia immense harm. It creates an im-
pression, somehow, that Dan O’Connor invented
Australia, and that alone is a disaster. The general
conviction here is that wages should be higher and
hours shorter than in other lands because this is a
young country —which is a ridiculous theory, for
the age of the country has no relation to the case ;
that taxes should be lower than elsewhere because
this is a young country ; that it is quite safe to
borrow an awful amount of money, because this is
a young country ; also (in some quarters) that it
is absurd to think of promoting manufactures
here because, this is a young country, and a young
country should be content to peg away at the
worst-paid of all the world’s occupations—that of
agriculture; and (in some other quarters) that
Australia’s ports should be opened freely to
Asiatics, Africans, Polynesians, and all such,
because this is a young country and needs popula-
tion. Further, the writer has heard proposals for
selling to foreign syndicates, mines, tramways,
great blocks of land, and almost all our other
assets, defended, at one time and another, on the
ground that this is a young country.
* • • •
Yet, according to the politician’s acceptation
of the term, Australia is really no younger than
any other country. That is where the abject
silliness of the whole tale comes in. The politician
mostly holds forth about Australia’s extreme
youth as an excuse for the towering Public Debt,
and his theory is something like this : “ Australia
is a young country which had all its railways,
telegraphs, telephones, roads, bridges, public
schools, sewerage systems, water supply systems,
gaols, lighthouses, docks, and other public work*
to build ; therefore, its situation is quite excep-
tional, and it has an exceptional debt as a matter
of course.” And his audience, as a rule, sees the
point quite clearly. Yet, during the century in
which Australia built all its railways, telegraphs
and telephones, every other country in the world
had to build all its railways, telegraphs and tele-
phones also. In these directions all the world is
just as young as Australia, and entitled to
exactly the same commiseration. In the same
century almost every other country in the world
had to build its public schools just as Australia
had, for the supply of schools before that time was
pitiably small. Also most countries have built
the bulk of their roads within the last 100 years,
exactly as Australia had to do, because most of the
roads existing previous to then were wholly use-
less according to modern ideas. Scotland, 150
years ago, had only tracks. Austria was a land
without a road for 30 years after Australia was
first settled. Seven-eighths of the roads of France
were made since 1835. All the roads of Portugal
have been made since 1840. Italy only began to
make roads long after Australia had started. In
all these countries what was called a road till
quite recently was only a streak of soil with foot
marks on it. It was practically the same with
Germany, Russia, Canada, the United States—
almost the whole world in fact. Australia, in this
matter, is just as young a country as nearly every
other country, and it would be ridiculous to have
the whole world apologising for its extreme youth.
♦ * * *
Similarly, every other country has had to build
its bridges within the last 100 years—just like
Australia. Bridges were very scarce a century
ago, and most of those that existed have worn out
since then, and been rebuilt. Every other land,
just like Australia, has had to build its post-offices,
gaols, &c., during the life-time of this country,
because the old ones wore out or became obsolete.
In fact, as regards post offices, most of the world
was almost as ill-provided as Australia 100 years
ago. Almost every costly water-supply system in
the world has been constructed within the last 70
years; almost every sewerage system within the
last 50 or 60 years.
* ♦ * *
In fact, when Australia started, the world started
also, as far as concerns almost- all the costly
essentials of modern civilisation. When Aus-
tralia commenced as a white man’s country, it and
the rest of the globe were equally guiltless of
railways, telegraphs, telephones, street tramways,
modern sewage systems, modern water-supply
systems, modern schools, prisons, lunatic asy-
lums, and gas and electric-lighting systems; arid
the rest of the world was almost as badly off as
Australia for roads, bridges, public libraries, post-
offices, lighthouses, for docks and harbours that
would accommodate modern shipping, and for
almost everything else which makes up the loan
bill of this great community. Civilisation, as we
understand it, is quite a recent thing, and in a
civilised sense all the white man’s world is about
equally new.
* * * *
But the world didn’t begin level bv any means.
Europe started with an incredible load'of debt,
burdened with huge armies, and ruined by 20
years of war. Australia started with no armies to
maintain, no war debt, and with what is even yet,
in proportion to population, the grandest asset on
earth in the shape of its public lands —an asset
winch, depleted, as it- is, still brings in nearly
£4,000,000 a year. And the result, when this
young country is compared with others quite as
young so far as modern civilisation is concerned,
is not flattering. Australia, despi ie its gold, its
land revenues, and its freedom from war debts and
war losses, owes .practically every copper of the

value of its railways, tramways, telegraphs, tele-
phones, water supply and sewerage systems, and
most of its road and bridge and harbour and light-
house expenditure, and a lot of other moneys
besides. Denmark, with none of Australia’s ad-
vantages, built all these public works out of re-
venue and paid off the cost of a disastrous
war as well. Sweden and Norway paid
a small part of the cost of their State
railways, and practically all the cost of
their other public works out of revenue. Prussia
- did likewise; so, in a less degree, did Holland,
Belgium, and Austria. In fact, it is necessary to
hunt round among the disreputable scalawag
kingdoms of Europe to find one which parallels
Australia, and even they have the excuse that
they never enjoyed Australia’s land and gold
revenues, that wars and the necessity for main-
taining large armies weighed them down, and
that they began the new era of civilisation loaded
with old war burdens. But among them all there
isn’t one which laments regularly about being a
young country as an excuse for its debts, though
as regards the matters which make debts in Aus-
tralia they are mostly just about the same age as
this country—some of them younger.
* * * •
So it is hereby moved that Australian politi-
cians cease offering the weary remark that this is
a young country as a full and satisfactory explana-
tion of everything. It is silly and it sounds
abject. New South Wales, at all events, as
the oldest of the States, is just as old, for
all the intents and purposes of Premier See’s
observation, as any other average place, and
it has had chances which no civilised State outside
Australia possessed. Its troubles have nothing to
do with its alleged youth. They mostly arise
from the fact that for over 100 years its people
paid no direct taxation, and comparatively little
indirect taxation, and explained, as a sufficient
reason for this course of action, that N. S. W. was
a young country. And they made up the shortage
by floating huge loans, and if anyone expressed a
doubt as to the wisdom of this course the state-
ment that N.S.W. was a young country settled
everything. And now that the revenue is wholly
insufficient to meet the interest on these past
follies, the average politician’s remedy and balm
and explanation consist of the ancient and moss-
grown remark that N.S.W. is a young country,
and that irrelevant observation covers a multitude
of sins.
Father Carey’s Chickens.
Father Carey’s chickens, like Mother Carey’s
chickens, are the emblem of just one thing—Bad
Luck, wherever you sight ’em.
• * * *
Father Carey’s chickens are the returned war-
riors, the returned “Bushmen,” the glorious
soldiers who went out to tread down the liberties
oc the little Dutch-African republics—“for fun.”
“ For the Empire,” the toadying, tuft-hunting
wire-pullers said ; but the men’s troubles about
“the Empire”! They nearly all went to the
war for a change, for sport, for what they could
make out of it. They had their sport ; they’ve
“ made their bit,” and many of them have spent
it in the private soldier’s usual way ; and now
they are back on Father Carey’s hands. Father
Carey wants to turn them over to the taxpayer to
provide for. That is so like the Jingo—he’s
always generous— at other people's expense.
» * * *
Father Carey is boss director of Sydney Daily
Telegraph —which kept the Protectionist Lyne
Govt, in office despite its own Foreign-trade prin-
ciples ; but not, as it protests, because nine or
ten Govt, billets were distributed, or might have
been distributed, among its directors and staff.
Father Carey is boss-director of the Daily Tele-
graph—which is boss-director of the little politi-
cal junta of wire-pulling nobodies who declare
who’s who in theN.S. W. Foreign-trade party, and
try to ram their pets down electors’ throats
by force of the Daily Telegraph. And
Father Carey’s paper is grieved because no-
thing is done for his chickens. The See
Govt, won’t create billets regardless of expense
for incompetent chickens to fill ; it won’t even
give obviously incompetent chickens the existing
billets which competent men are waiting to fill.
In other words, it won’t rob the taxpayer—not
this time, not wholesale. And Father Carey is
grieved. The Telegraph weeps over his chickens
in leading articles. It wants a little board
appointed—another little board ! —with powers of
patronage for Father Carey’s chickens, and (of
course !) d—n the expense. Possibly the Tele-
graph knows two or three nice little men waiting
to draw salaries on that nice little board ? Good
Old Father Carey ?
* * * •
The Bulletin enters an emphatic “No!” to
the proposal. Let the Jingoes provide their own
billets, and put their hands in their own pockets.
These returned soldiers have done nothing for
Australia but waste Australian money. For that
they are not specially to blame : they had their
chance of “sport,” and they took it. But Aus-
tralia owes them nothing: the conditions of
agreement were clearly stated beforehand, and
the conditions are fulfilled. While they have been
away thousands of other Australians, just as good
or better, have stuck to hard graft without any
“change,” and they deserve all the consideration
that is going.
* * * •
Heroism 1 The Bulletin swears there is as
much heroism, and more, shown every week on
the mining fields of Australia—and in a dozen
other avocations. The man who faces a lighted
fuse to take his fallen mate out of a shaft earns the
Victoria Cross a dozen times over; for he has no
esprit-de-corps to stimulate him, no hope of reward,
no bait of “glory.” And a hundred similar cases
suggest themselves—met and conquered every
week by the soldiers of industry, the soldiers
of whom alone Australia has reason to be proud.
* * « •
The Telegraph pleads that Father Carey’s
pets are unfit for honest graft in competition with
others. That is the Jingo curse come home to
roost. Many of these men left their wives and
families, left occupations where they were of use
to the country ; and now they are unfit to go back
to their places in the industrial machine. They
have become the typical military drone who lives
on the sweat of the laborer. And the country is
to support them!
* * * *
Let Father Carey and the Telegraph and
the Jingoes support them. They are respon-
sible. Send Father Carey’s hat round again;
boom the Jingo drum again ; let the
Telegraph howl again for subscriptions and
advertise subscribers in bigger space, than ever.
Why should Father Carey’s chickens be made a
permanent burden on the taxpayer ? There is
only one reaspn—because your typical Jingo is
warlike only as long as the responsibility doesn’t
come home to his own life, or his own pocket. He
has the front of a lion, the tail of a dingo.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Libelling the Crow-Eater Army.
Adelaide, the Holy City where church-spires
stick up the spikes on the uneven porcupine,
had a final spasm of desperate'Jingoism when the
Duke was nearly due. It apparently went out
looking for a small person to hit in honor of the
Empire, and it fell upon C. W. Chandler, editor
of tiie Fuel Lance, who was already in gaol for
libel, and who now gets six months’ mope gaol and
is fined £5O for reflecting on the 'morals of one
of the contingents. For Reflecting on the Morals
of Soldiers ! !!! Chandler’s paper alleged that
when this contingent was camped at Adelaide
24 females were discovered one night in the
tents. Chandler afterwards stated that he didn’t
see the article before it appeared in print, but.
that point may pass ; as editor it was his duty to
know what appeared in his- own paper. Two
officers in charge at the camp swore indig-
nantly that there were no women found
in, or turned out of, the camp on the night
in question. A third and superior officer
admitted that females might have got into the
camp without the knowledge of the officers, the
fence being reasonably accessible,' so, as far as
concerned the presence of women it was freely
admitted that Chandler's tale might be partly
true. Chandler’s paper didn’t specify any in-
dividual soldier as an offender, so it didn’t libel
any person in particular, but the State’s legal
authorities, in their frenzy of Jingoism, went for
Chandler for libelling at least 24 persons un-
known out of a large body, just as they might go
for him for saying there were over 3000 illegitimate
births in S. A. within the last ten years, and thus
libelling about (5000 unspecified men and women
out of a large community, or for libelling the
human race generally by saying,, as the
preacher does every Sunday, that most of
it is going on the broad road to perdition.
And in its loathsomely servile desire to
get a conviction it put a whole lot of distinctly
false issues before the jurymen, and appealed to
their meanest instincts of illogical grovel. The
Crown’s representative told them that “it was
unbearable that soldiers who had since fallen in
the defence of their country should be falsely
charged with having committed the abomi-
nations referred to on the eve of their depar-
ture from their native land.” The “ abomina-
tions” are matters which happen daily in
S.A., as shown by the illegitimacy record
and other evidences. They were just plain,
ordinary immorality, and the Government which
made such a fuss thinks so little of plain, ordinary
immorality, when the parties are of the age to be
responsible for their own sins, and no side issues
are involved, that it doesn’t even treat it as an
offence punishable by 2s. fid. fine. A. Government
which goes on like that has no right io strike such
attitudes and howl about “abominations.” The
prosecution defined S.A. as the “ native land ” of
the maligned soldiers, so by its own showing
the statement that they died in defence
of their country (which is presumably
their native land) was an attempt to
inflame the minds of the jury on a false issue ; the
Boers never threatened South Australia, and
nobody died in its defence. The prosecution also
shrieked about the unbearableness of such a false
accusation against the dead, but it didn’t make
the least attempt to prove that the charge was
directed against any of the dead soldiers. No
soldiers in particular were specified, and the State
Law Department simply alleged on its own unsup-
ported say-so, that those who were meant in-
cluded some of the men who are now dead.
It was the kind of tombstone claptrap that was
calculated to make a jury boil over with maudlin
sentiment and run amuck, but there wasn’t a
shred of evidence behind it. When anyone says
(and such things are occasionally said) that the
illegitimacy record of S.A. for the past ten years
is a disgrace to the community, he reflects on the
dead in exactly the same way as Chandler’s
paper did, for a good part of the community has
died in the last ten years. Also the prosecution
just took it for granted, without a vestige of proof,
and apparently hustled the jury into doing like-
wise, that the morals of the cohentingent were so
beautiful, and so much better than the S.A.
average, that the charge of having women in the
camp was a blight upon its character. In the
city of churches and brothels that Crown Law
functionary got up, with the hair of a strange
animal on his head, to profess that the mere sus-
picion that a S.A. soldier might, be just a very
ordinary human being, and not more than three
times as moral as David, the Psalmist, was an out-
rage, an unbelievable horror, a foul aspersion
and an obvious, glaring, shocking lie—also an
“abomination.” Chandler’s yarn was foolish
and clumsy and not too clean, but it was a lot
more respectable than the yarn by which the un-
speakably mean and revoltingly hypocritical
Crown got him six months.

A Minimum Wage for Teachers.
The teaching profession of New South Wales has
lately made its voice heard in a small way asking
where its minimum-wage comes in. It can only
do it in a small way, for though the pick-and-
shovel employe may besiege the Government
offices and the papers with his. grievances, the
teacher who did so would incur the high displea-
sure of that awful potentate— the Department.
The average wage of the Cornstalk teacher in 1896
was £ll7 4s. per annum. By 18 )9 it had sunk to
£ll5 4s. per annum (these figures can be verified
by anyone who cares to divide the amount
spent on teachers’ salaries by the number of
teachers, as given in Coghlan’s “Statistical
Register”). The See Government has gained
much kudos by fixing a minimum wage of 7s. a
day for pick-and-shovel men and the like in
Government employ. That is at the rate of about
£lO9 a year. The average wage of the Cornstalk
teacher is not much higher than that, and the
minimum doesn’t come within gunshot of it. And
the teacher has to spend years of study to get
there. He commences about the age of 15 as a
pupil teacher, and remains in that grade four
years. He goes up for a stiff examination, and if
he passes, and if he be among the first 25 that
pass; he is sent for a year to the training-college.
At the end of that time he gets an appointment at
a magnificent £96 a year. After a year he rises to
£lO4 ; in another year to £ll3 ; in another year to
£l2O. He has now been working for the Depart-
ment eight years—the first four for a practically
invisible wage. Then he is sent out into the
country at £136 a year, and he may remain at that
figure for eight or ten years—in fact, there is no
guarantee at all that he will get off it. He may
be a 3A man, or a 28, or a 2A, but whether he has
toiled to qualify for the highest degree, or con-
tented himself with the lowest one, he gets the same
salary, and freezes at the same point. The
teachers- make the very modest suggestion that
the 3A man (even he has to be a person of much
knowledge and some ability) should stick fast at
£l2O ; that the 2B man should increase by annual
increments to £136, and the 2A man by annual
increments to £l7l but this simple idea of vary-
ing the salary according to ability, and encourag-
ing the teacher to qualify for the higher grades, is
beyond the department’s comprehension. The
only way to secure further advancement (unless
the teacher has “influence”) is to pass a further
exam, and get a still higher certificate. But the
teacher can only go up for this final exam, by per-
mission of the Department, and a man who has
studied continuously till he could pass with ease
may be refused permission till he is old, if the
Department feels that way. If he gets permission
and passes, he remains where he is till he is further
examined by the boss inspector, and gets his cer-
tificate confirmed, and if lie is in the far-back and
inaccessible regions of the State he may wait and
moulder for years till the great boss inspector, who
alone can conduct this final exam., comes his
way- The teacher is growing pretty old by this
time on his £136 a year. His great chance lies in
getting appointed to a school. Then he is paid
according to the class of the school, and class
depends on size. If he is a man of high attain-
ments he may be appointed at a good salary to a
large school in a prosperous mining town, and
then the mines may burst up, and the population
depart, and as the attendance diminishes the
teacher’s salary goes down with a run. And a
very inferior man appointed to control a small
school at small pay may find his salary go up
through an influx of population till he gets a far
higher wage than the man who ranks above him.
And, curiously enough, the men who fail in the
first examination, and never qualify for the train-
ing College course at all, often get better appoint-
ments than the ones who qualify with high
honors. And why all this muddle should be
Heaven only knows. It is tolerably oertain the
Department doesn’t.

Tv/o Samples of Sydney “ Telegraph.”
This is from Sydney Daily Telegraph's leading
article of 21st June, 1901
For years past the Protectionist body has been deceiv-
ing the country with false cries as to the state of its
finances, AND DECLARING IN THE TEETH OF ALL
EVIDENCE THAT UNDER A FREETRADE SYSTEM
OF TAXATION IT COULD NOT POSSIBLY PAY ITS
W AY. Yet, since they have been in t)lHce, they have to
admit that the State income has been largely in excess of
the normal expenditure.
You observe that it was absolutely false to state
that N.S.W. didn’t and couldn’t pay its way with
the revenue supplied by Reid’s ' tariff—which
came in force on Ist January, 1890, and is still in
force. And this —ye gods ! —is from Sydney
Daily Telegraph of 20th June, 1901—just one
day earlier: —
There is always in the finances of New South Wales
something to be .shelved, and the result is that the de-
ficiency, instead of diminishing, increases. Take the
acknowledged deficiencies from December 31, 1890. In
the following statement, the totals down to June, 1899,
are from the. Auditor-General’s report; that for June,
1900, is from Sir William Lyne’s accounts (not yet vouched
for by the Auditor-General); and that for June, 1901,
is based on Mr. Waddell’s own statement of the posi-
tion :
* In the half-year ended June 30, 1895, credit was taken
for £341,320, which should rightly have come off the
original deficiency, In 1898-9, credit was taken impro-
perly for £150,000, which was added to deficiency in 1899-
1900. ** Surplus.
Excluding the exceptional half-year in 1895, the average
new deficiency created in the 10 years has been £274,000
per annum.
In all these years there has in reality been only one
surplus—in 1897-B—and that was questionable, owing to
the charges made to “ short-dated loans.”
Thus it appears on the Daily Telegraph’s own
showing that :
(1) The Protectionists were telling the absolute truth
when they said N.S.W. couldn’t pay its way under the
Reid Tariff, and the untruth is in the Telegraph’s
leading article of next day, wherein it eats its own words
and its own figures.
(2) The Telegraph, in its anxiety to bolster up the
Calico Jimmy Government r>f G. Reid, told shameful in-
accuracies about his alleged surpluses. It now admits
that the two biggest shortages were in 1891 (mostly
under Parkes), and in 1896, under Reid.
(3) The Telegraph now admits that Reid only pro-
fessed to square the accounts for the half-vear to 30th
June, 1895, by improperly annexing £341,320 of loan
moneys as revenue, and by not paying the half-year’s
charge (£75,000) in reduction of deficit.
(4) It now admits that the only surplus which was pro-
duced under the Reid tariff (a miserable £8060) was a
dubious affair created by charging much ordinary 7 ex-
penditure to short-dated loans. '
(5) It practically admits that its years of yelling about
the “ Dibbs deficit,” and its constant comparisons be-
tween the thriftless Dibbs and the economical and solvent
Reid were a mean slander on its old enemv Dibbs. The
three years in which N.S.W. worked wholly under Dibbs’s
estimates and Dibbs’s financial system left a deficit of
£629,325 (£209 t 775 per annum). Then follow four com-
plete years under Reid : deficit, £787,353, or £196,838
per annum, with the additional information that t 311,320
was improperly taken into revenue from loans, that
£150,000 was improperly taken credit for, that a half-
year’s sinking fund wasn’t paid, and that the deficit
would have been much larger but for the charging of
ordinary expenses to Reid’s new “short-dated loan”
device. Which, all put together, make Reid so much
worse than Dibbs that there is no possible comparison
between the two. Even the Telegraph doesn’t charge
Dibbs with any cooking of the accounts.
This is a good deal for the Foreign-trade organ to
convict itself of in two days. Its own evidence is
to the effect that it meanly hounded down a com-
paratively thrifty Premier with shrieks of “Dibbs’s
deficit,” and systematically bolstered up a worse
one by assuring the public that Reid’s financial
doings were all right; that it wasn’t telling the
truth when it assured the country that the revenue
under Reid’s tariff was quite sufficient; and that
it let N.S.W. go further and further on the road
to rum, and pile up a bigger and bigger deficit,
not only without saying a word of serious
warning, but while assuring the people that those
who uttered words of warning were quite un-
worthy of credit. Also, it is hollering to the
people to. put the old Reid crowd of figure-fakers
(they are that by its own showing) back into office.
In fact, it stands off and throws mud at itself as a
journal that is wholly unfit to be trusted when the
country s interests and the importers’ • interests
come in conflict.

Premier See’s Doctrine of Cowardice.
Over a year ago, when the clear atmosphere of-
Austraha was rent with the cries of the Jingo
about the “sacrifices” he was prepared to make
for the Empire, and the sky was darkened with
i up-thrown bell-topper, this paper
asked him flatly whether his sacrifices would go
the length of paying the War Bill, or whether he
only meant to have the excitement of seeing the
contingents come and go (he didn’t go himself, as
a rule), the banquets, the “glory,” the titles, &c.,
and leave the coming generation to pay as usual.
And it tried to explain to him, without giving
needless offence, that if he took the latter course
he would be a coward, a good ideal of a liar, and
a very abject and erawlsome fraud. So far as
New South Wales is concerned, Premier John
See supplied the answer about a week ago in these
word :—•
The extraordinary expenditure consequent on the
outbreak of the plague, and the cost involved
in sending the contingents to South Africa and
China will amount to about £320,000. That will he
provided for, and provided for out of. revenue. It
was never contemplated that the whole of the expendi-
ture in connection with these matters should fall upon
one single year. It would be just as reasonable to expect
the British Government to charge the whole cost of the
South African war against one year as to expect us to pro-
vide for these extraordinary expenditures out of one
years revenue. The contention is* of course, absolutely
a isurd, and would not be maintained by any fair-minded,
reasonable man.
Ihe Bulletin tries to be reasonably fair-minded,
and it takes this opportunity to remark (again
without any desire to give offence) that the
above statement is a deliberate and childish per-
version of the case. Also that the Jingo who
blattered so much about the “sacrifices” he was
going to make for the empire is proving himself
just the same mean and sneakful fraud that was
expected. In the first place, consider the two
war-bills:—
War Bill
Population. War Bill. per
~ , Inhabitant.
United Kingdom .. 41,454,217 £153,317,000 £3 13 11
New South Wales .. 1,362,232 320,000 4 9
The fair-minded and reasonable John See holds
that it would be just as reasonable to expect
Britain to pay its whole war bill (£3 13s. lid.
per inhabitant) out of revenue in one year as to
expect N.S.W. to pay its whole war bill (4s. 9d.
per inhabitant) out of revenue in one year. Surely
the useless and unornamental John must mistake
N.S.W, for a lunatic asylum! And as a matter
of fact Britain has paid, or is paying, the follow-
ing sums out of revenue each year, by the simple,
courageous process of levying extra taxation :
War charges paid
by Britain out of Amount per
extra taxation. inhabitant.
1900- .. .. £10,866,000 .. ss. 3d.
1901- .. .. 15,883,000 .. 7s. Bd.
These amounts don’t nearly represent all the war
charges that Britain lias paid out of revenue—it
has paid over £18,000,000 more, and its total war
charges paid out of revenue in the last three
years have been at the average rate of Bs. 4d. per
inhabitant per annum. The above are only the
amounts which it has paid by extra taxation.
On the other hand, Premier See hasn’t paid one
penny of N.S.W’s war expenses (which are only
4s. 9d. per inhabitant in all) out of new taxation
or old taxation, or out of revenue in any shape.
Nor has he provided for any of them. ' And, as
usual, he is moaning in the ditch (for it really
amounts to that) that the people of N.S.W. are
such a poor, miserable, hopeless lot that they
can’t be expected to face their responsibilities as
other States do —that the Briton in his own
country may be a gritty person, who doesn’t mind
an extra tax of 7s. Bd. per inhabitant in one year
to meet an extra trouble, and who doesn’t lose
heart even when the income-tax goes up to Is. 2d.
in the £, but the degenerate Briton at this end
can’t be expected to face a much smaller liability
in two years, and so on. The Bulletin says once
more that the Australian people, though they
may be a rather tired and indifferent race, have
courage enough to meet their responsibilities, and
it is only the cowardice of their rulers which
makes the trouble. The people may be too
tired to hunt for honest rulers, but if an
honest ruler turned up the cash support of the
people would not be wanting. Meanwhile, it
seems, when judged by the bill, that the “aid”
N.S.W. gave to the empire, and the “sacrifices”
it made for the empire, weren’t anything like large
enough to justify one-tenth of the shrieking,
guzzling and flag-flapping that were indulged in
oyer them. Also, that the large home-staying
Jingo who talked so much about these “sacri-
fices” and got so drunk on Mafeking Day, is
proving just the same melancholy public botch
that might have been expected, for he not
only hasn’t paid-up his alleged “sacrifices,” but
he holds that no fair minded man could expect
him to do so. And N.S.W. is still anxious to
know how the See Government is going to find
£350,000 or £lOO,OOO for Old-Age Pensions next
year and in every succeeding year, when it is so
utterly absurd to expect it to find £320,000 for
war and plague ex.’s this year.
On a burning N.S.W; Civil Service question :
Editor Bulletin,—The re-grading of the N.S.W.
Public Service has at last been gazetted with
but 12 days to spare. Another fortnight and
the votes covering the increments would have
lapsed. 3956 officers have been dealt with, and to
1274 have been granted increases (of £lO, mostly). The
Board’s conclusion that the other 2682 are not worthy
of such a magnificent increment as 16s. Bd. a month wiil
not materially raise them in the opinion of the 2682. A
significant feature of this re-grading is that the Board
seems to have dealt with each officer in the service and
not his office. It has been heretofore the Board’s boast
that each man is paid according to the value of the work
performed. Thus, if the P.M at Boolaroo or the Latids
Agent at Oow Mountain is a genius worth, say, £3OO a year,
and the work he can do in his out-of-the-wav corner is
only worth £2OO a year, £2OO is all that he can get, hut
the Board will see that he is transferred to a post where
his fine abilities will have full plav. This ingenious
theory blocked many appeals, the appellants being in
some cases greatly flattered at the Board’s implied recog-
nition that they were underpaid. But time has
gone by and no serious attempt seems to have been made
to satisfactorily establish these unfortunates. In the
present re-grading many instances can be cited where
officers have been placed in positions at a less salary than
that paid to their predecessors, which shows that the
Board considers other circumstances than the merit of an
office, irrespective of the holder. Public servants are
everywhere anxious for a definite reply to the query : Is
the billet or the billetee-graded-? Considering that the
Board is continually remodelling its own regulations, and
has not made up its mind how to run the service, it pro-
bably could not answer the question itself. The correct
reply seems to be that the Beard-grades the office or the
officer, just as it pleases, always provided that it hits on
the method by which the occupier is best “ done in.”
Blank Cover.

The Bulletin's tip for the N.S.W. State
election is : Vote for no candidate who is pledged
to either Lee or See. Don’t touch either Lee or
See with a long pole. Don’t be found dead in the
same street with either Lee or See. Vote for
Labor candidates, or independent candidates if
they seem to be really independent and have a
decent political and personal record. If there is
neither a Labour nor an Independent candidate
available, don’t vote at all. Lee is a part of the
old Reid Government and has the old Reid
gang around him. See and his crowd are the old
Lyne gang. Reid was a political leprosy, a curse,
a public disgrace ; in any decent community, after
his atrocious faking of the public accounts, and
his public bowl-out by the Auditor-General, in-
stead of being leader of the Federal Opposition
he would no longer have been politically tolerated.
Lyne followed in his footsteps; the best thing
that can be said for Lyne is that he was only a
follower. And now these two awful sets of
exposed individuals are exposing each other.
Even the brightest and readiest intellects
often require a spur to assist them to special bril-
liancy, at a crisis. D.C.L. whisky provides a spur
that will, if required, induce a gallop without
necessitating putting the intellect into the stable
to reduce swelling of the head. It encourages
effort at the smallest cost to the vitality, and the
possibilities of dire after-results, which lurk in so
many other brands, are unknown to D.C.L. —The
Whisky of the Wise.*

“The Favorite,” at Sydney Lyceum, is
likely to have a long run, as the good characters
are even stupider than usual, and there is also a
considerable amount of horse. The behaviour of
the equine characters on the opening night was
certainly curious; the horses that had merely to
trot on the stage with a peaceful carriage behind
them got excited and pawed at the tops of the
trees. The horse, however, that was hocussed in the
stable-scene took his pill in a Christian spirit, and
later on another horse won a great race with calm-
ness, appearing on the stage immediately after-
wards without a sign of hard running about him.
There is a good deal of poisoning and drugging in
the drama, and every now and then the villain
offers someone a glass which another person
immediately dashes to the ground in an obviously
intentional manner. The good people, however,
continue to accept things from the bad man in a
trustful spirit, the latter later on varying his
wickedness by conveying the poison in more solid
diet. It is a relief to the audience when the
aconitine chocolates given to the small boy are not
dashed to the ground but merely confiscated—so
many things have been more or less dashed by
this time that any change of method is acceptable.
As a set-off to these casual methods of murder the
villain concocts an elaborate scheme for destroying
the heroine in a picturesque manner in a cistern
on the top of a roof. The tank obligingly illumin-
ates and shows her floundering in the water like
an imprisoned gold-fish. She is rescued by the kind
policeman, aided by the larrikin boy who afterwards
turns out to be her own son. The audience is pre-
Eared to find this child of good birth owing to his
abit of praying to the stars and rebuking his pal
for sin.
* • * m
Miss Eugenie Duggan, as the heroine,
seems a nice girl, but she has a curious fashion of
going out to drive in evening dress, and it is re-
markable that some of the ladies on the racecourse
also appear in this light costuming, though judging
by the heavy overcoats worn by the men onlookers
it is meant to be a cold day. H. O. Willard dis-
guises well as the villain who frequently leaves
his gentlemanly address to train thieves in a well-
staged London den, while Temple Harrison makes
a favorable impression as the bad big boy with the
“ good ’eart,” who is finally reformed by the good
small boy, who turns out to be a lost heir. On Satur-
day night Miss Kathleen Duggan irrupted on the
racecourse, and sang something rather sorrowfully,
while fat jockey-boys behind her capered about in
a fearful manner. There is only one girl in a hun-
dred that can afford to dress in jockey costume,
and the Lyceum does not seem to have caught
that one as yet.
