The concluding nights of “Florodora”
are advertised, after which the “ Casino Girl ”
will arise at Sydney’s largest theatre. “ Floro-
dora” takes its departure thickly crusted with
the new songs and fresh items that have grown
upon it in its latter days, and it goes away taking
tne unsolved problem of its success along with it.
Many people nave asked many other people why
they so greatly admire “Florodora,” and the
other people have mostly waved their hands in a
vague, flapping way, and said there was a some-
thing about it, you know—a kind of an atmo-
sphere in a manner of speaking—a feeling that
couldn’t be defined, but which somehow enveloped
you, as it might be, and other hazy remarks.
«* * *
At Sydney Criterion the “ Milestones of
Life ” will to stand up like irregular teeth
along the beaten road of existence after Friday.
Saturday is to see the beginning of a revival of
“ The Power and the Glory.” Sydney has already
seen this piece, but looking back vaguely into a
memory where innumerable melodramas skurry
after each other like wet, disgusted ghosts, writer
can’t recollect for certain whether it is the melo-
drama where the good girl has her head put under
a steam hammer, or the one where she is left on
the rails for the train to go over her, or the one
where she is only suspected of something she
didn’t do and consequently has to make a pre-
carious living at plain sewing in an attic on a wet
night, or another one. As all melodramas are
pretty much the same melodrama, the chances are
that it isn’t another one.
** * *
“In the Soup ” progresses well at Sydney
Palace, and offers an interesting contrast in some
respects to “Nurse,” lately at the Royal, exemplify-
ing the man’s and the woman’s method of comedy-
writing, so far as the softer sex has progressed in
that art. Every now and then man rises in a body
and declares that women can’t do this or that,
upon which woman rises and does the thing she
couldn’t do—and frequently does it well. It
was alleged, f’rinstance, that the feminine mind
was not shaped for comedy-writing, whereupon
Clo Graves wrote “A Mother of Three,” achiev-
ing something like a success. But her prentice
hand is visible in “Nurse.” “Nurse” has better
lines than “ In the Soup,” and, as far as dialogue
goes, there is newness in much of its humor; prob-
ably it would read much better. ButthePalaceplay,
besides the advantage of better all-round acting,
shows the strength of the more experienced man
mind. All the available humor is squeezed out of
every situation, and every incident leads up to
something else. The third act, where all the men
want divorces because their respective wives went
to sleep over a dinner-table in the presence of
members of the opposite sex, is, of course, wildly
impossible, but a wild impossibility is of no im-
Eortance in the last act, when the mind has
ecome drugged with the improbabilities of two
previous acts. The art of comedy-writing is partly
the art of being humorous and partly the art of
being improbable probably, and of filling three
acts with possible impossibilities. And the person
who can only be improbable in an improbable
manner had better abandon comedy. It isn’t his
line of usefulness. Nature intended him to sell
stale fish to people with bad colds, or to follow
some of the cruder walks of deception.
** # »
Sydney Empire is brightened this week by
Charles Fanning, a comedian that Sydney has
probably seen before. Herr Pagel continues
getting through useless feats of strength, his per-
formance being occasionally of a painful charac-
ter. Something like this happens : Pagel lifts his
wife, who sits, with unavoidable want of grace, on
a heavy, round weight, which is raised higher and
higher by one hand, while the other gropes about
to catch hold of the other weight, which is sup-
posed to be lifted at the same time. The right hand
holding weight and lady is half raised, when she
slips and comes down on the stage. Another try,
and then there is another heavy fall—this time
right down on the boards. The lady jumps up
smiling, to prove (which is impossible) that she is
not at all hurt and rather enjoys being flung about
in this manner. Again she is lifted, when a sym-
pathetic galleryite calls out, “ Say, call this part
over ! ” But the feat is gone through. Herr
Pagel’s companion in misfortune retires to the
wings and Pagel rests, waits sadly for his next
feat, his ill-used muscles quivering painfully, his
melancholy face expressive of nothing but physi-
cal distress. The struggle for a crust 1
** * *
Snazelle’s show at the Queen’s Hall,
Sydney, draws excellent houses to the last, and
the audience amuses itself between whiles by cal-
culating what particular means is taken to pro-
duce each separate sound supposed to occur in the
pictures. The swishing of the sea round the
torpedo suggests the hissing of a gigantic sausage
in a frying-pan, but the biff when one tar hits
another is still unplaceable. Snazelle’s reci-
tation of the “Handy Man” is well done, and
as he mentions each good quality of that useful
gentleman—such as his love for children or for
work—an illustrated exemplification of the same
is immediately thrown upon the canvas. But
when Snazelle remarks that the handy man’s
heart is the heart of a lion that animal’s vital
organ doesn’t appear on the sheet, and this over-
sight is unlike Snazelle, who is generally so careful
about detail. Snazelle has added new pictures to
his “Navy” series, and his ships and funerals
will remain at their present address till Satur-
day. After that they open at Brisbane Opera
House on July 8.
** * *
There is a plethora of the child-artist and
comedian at Sydney Tivoli just now. Fanny Powers
and the diminutive Eric are really clever, but a
little kid goes a long way at a variety show, and
both the abovementioned kids are very small,
indeed. Will Daly, who used to sing in
a fine voice and make such horrible faces, is back
again. He doesn’t make such faces now, and is
considerably improved. The Brothers De Wynne
do some startling things in the acrobatic line;
most startling is where the light brother stands on
his head on the head of the heavy brother,
and the heavy brother wanders up and downstairs
with the light brother thus inverted. The Musi-
cal Tramp and the songful slavey and her little
bit of orange peel seem to be growing in popu-
larity.
** * *
Up to now, Melbourne has outlived the
season of Chinese opera at the Democratic Club
(of all places !), partly because there are no top
notes or choruses in the musical scheme, but
chiefly for the reason that admission, as a rule, is
limited to celestial Believers. In the original
Italian sense of the word, it is not opera that hap-
pens at the dingy Demo. Club room. The Chinese
have no art of vocalism, and only a limited system
of musical instrumental tune. The entertainment
aforesaid is drama, with an interminable orchestral
accompaniment and frequent interspersions of sing-
song, the difference between the sound of the
spoken dialogue and the incidental warble being
too subtle for European criticism. All the same,
the orchestra of five, who sit at the back of the
stage in plain Australian clothes and soft felt hats,
appear to punish their one-string fiddle, flageolet,
tom-toms, etc., with malice aforethought. When
the talk diverges into chant or ballad strains the
singer—every vocal effort is a solo, seemingly—
suits his wail to the accompaniment. He always
knows what noise is coming next. Nobody con-
cerned in the production ever needs prompting.
As an example of Chinese memory-culture the
show at the Demo, is at least respectable.
* * * *
The play had been moving along in a loose-
ointed, narrative way for about 20 nights of
hours each before The Bulletin was in a position
to solve the mystery of its weirdness, and sym-
pathise with the patient aliens who confronted
the trouble with their hats on. The audience, by
the way, expresses reverence for the drama of its
forefathers by brooding under the shade of its
“ cadey.” No Chinese theatrical classic is less
than a few thousand years old, therefore one pays
homage to the ancient literature by sitting hat-
headed in all parts of the house. Even the com-
bined stage-manager and scene-snifter, a casual
bloke in a fifty shilling sac-suit, who turns a chair
round, or introduces a pair of curtains on a brass
rod to signify, new and original scenery what time
the persistent play is still progressing favorably—
even this matter-of-fact interloper wears his mark
of respect on top of him. Probably the scene-
shifter and the orchestrians can follow the dialogue
more closely than their average compatriot in
front. It is spoken in Mandarin language,
which often soars above the education of
a low-down Loo from the fan-tan shop,
and would be impossible to the mummers if
they had not had stage experience in the country
where the shilling tea comes from. Chinese stage
experience teaches the tricks of female make-up
to perfection. The men cast for women parts are
quite convincing. The unhappy wife who got
sentenced to be chopped into 1000 pieces on the
uncorroborated statement of her step-mother-in-
law (vide 20th section of the play) was the fac-
simile of the typical charmer one finds on a
Eaper fan, except that the she-male face was
etter painted. The evil step-mother, with the
little black beauty-spots (representing moles) upon
his countenance, was every inch a woman, like-
wise the servant gal of the period. The beauty
spots of the European powered-wig period
were probably suggested by the bogus mole of
Chow drama. Also it appears that the betrayed
spinster in a Bland Holt fiction can show an
ancient precedent for letting down her back-hair.
It is the first law of mourning in Chinese female
life. Loose hair spells Woe even as the long
beard on the man signifies the authority of sexa-
genarianism. Chinese drama is unsullied by
argument re old-age pensions. The man of
years takes out a license to grow chin hair,
and becomes a domestic mandarin on the
strength of his constitution. He is one of
the Fittest because he hasn’t died earlier. Never-
theless the bad old girl with the black sticking-
plaster dotted o’er her countenance offers a
travelling assassin 500 dollars to remove the
bearded parent, and the deed would be done
easily enough but for the low comedian, who
listens outside the door when the crime is being
ordered, and ultimately slays the hired villain.
There is not a real door for the comic hero to listen
against. He just comes to the front of the stage,
wipes one hand across the other, like letter H in
the deaf-and-dumb alphabet, to signify his
presence at a keyhole, then puts one hand behind
nis ear and eavesdrops in an animated manner.
There is a certain pathos in the simplicity of this
make-believe.
• • * *
Chinese drama is, in some of its phases,
alleesame as Xmas panto., though its intentions are
strictly honorable and serious. Instead of a fairy
queen, a man walks upon the stage and alleges that
he is a heavenly person, who intends to punish the
guilty and straighten things out. The archangel
then instructs an evil spirit—a frog goblin of the
most advanced panto, manufacture —to go and
seize the evil step-mother’s only son, and take him
to the hell reserved for first offenders. Next time
the doomed young man takes the floor the goblin
humps him on his back by friendly arrangement,
as though he were lifting a sack of coals, and bears
him off. At 11.30 p.m. or later the moral lesson,
when it is a moral lesson, comes to an end. Some-
times it is a giddy lesson, with a four-poster
Chinese bedstead in the last act, but, anyhow, the
end arrives before midnight.

Arnold is prospering in the golden West
with his nimble narrative of Smith, the man
who Left Home. Crowded houses at Perth, and
every prospect of the same old luck at Kalgoorlie
and elsewhere. Smith’s departure from the do-
mestic roost will begin to explain itself in Mel-
bourne on Aug. 3rd, when Arnold and co. make
their first re-appearance at the Princess’s. Mean-
while, an amateur opera crowd is going to open
Musgrove’s theatre for a short season.
** # *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the prima-donna,
or the tenor, or both, will continue to die melo-
diously every evening until further notice. True
love has a horribly bad time in grand opera. The
couple who duet with their faces close together in
the first act are invariably marked for earthly
separation before the band ceases to play. If they
don’t both perish, the sole survivor is left in-
sensible on the boards, a silent witness to the
foolishness of intense affection among the
higher singing classes. The baritone and basso
live to drag out a miserable repentance in almost
every case, and the contralto, or mezzo, usually
gets a chance to attend the funerals of her late
friends. But the soprano and the young man she
has been walking out with are as lilies of the field
waiting to be cut down and cast into the oven. It
is opera—but it is not fair dinkum. Which
remark would look more impressive in Italian
language.
** * *
The sad story of “ Ernani,” likewise the
hysterical tale of “Lucia” and the tombstone
tragedy of “Aida,” were each performed last
week, and an honest public certif. of merit was
awarded to the company every time. Melbourne
has been a cheerless city of cold, damp feet and
catarrh (various kinds of catarrh, and all of them
unmusical) for the past few weeks. Moreover the
local daily and weekly press notices of these Mel-
bourne record ventures in Italian opera have (with
exceptions) been about as cold and damp as
technical phraseology could make them. All the
same, a winter-stricken community rolls up to the
big house in large numbers and applauds with un-
common warmth. One night there was a change in
the “ Aida ” cast. Signora Sapio appeared as the
captive lady, and stood some three inches taller
and less massive than estimable Signora Bene-
detto in the same character. The story felt the
benefit of the change. The new heroine sang as
effectively as the first one, and her acting pleased.
A new Ethiopian father was introduced on this
occasion. Bozzoli played the part more vigorously
than was expected, and his bright young baritone
voice gave it an improved musical aspect. Ihe
chorus is getting ready for “ Cavalleria,’ 1 ag-
liacci ” and other things, but a chorus’s capacity
for study needs a lot of judicious shaking up. ahe
two short operas will probably be out this week.
** * *
“ His Natural Life,” with a Friday lapse
into “ Hamlet,” is filling the bill at Melbourne
Bijou. The present Dampierian treatment of
Marcus Clarke’s book inclines more to cheap
frivolity than some of the previous versions, al-
though Gabbett and the triangles are frequently
on the surface, and the dramatic hash has a strong
savor of blud. Sarah Rex and Rev. Meekin
gambol together for the amusement of the loud,
unthinking cherubs who sit up aloft and chew pea-
nuts. Whereas the real original Meekin of Clarke’s
story ought to have been foolish enough for
any low comedian’s purpose, the inane shepherd
whom Sarah tickles under the chin seems a poor
joke all the time, and when Maurice Frere is
dragged into the funny business for the sake of a
gallery laugh, Meekin amounts to a dramatic sin.
One rather redeeming circumstance in “His
Natural Life ” is the quick, decisive climax which
brings the curtain down. John Rex administers a
fatal whack to Frere’s skull with his iron bracelets,
and the convicts who ate the Moocher in the
wilderness are able to gloat over their oppressor’s
corpse before they get hanged. Alfred Dampier,
as of yore, plays the troubled alcoholic chaplain,
and sacrifices many precious drops of whisky to
save his martyr brother Rufus. Alfred Rolfe is a
respectable Rufus, and the venerable E. Holloway
resumes his entertaining character of Gabbett, the
cannibal. Some of the remaining performers
deserve to be mentioned in terms of severe reproof,
but take it all together, “His Natural Life” is a
well set-up show.
# * *
At the Royal all goes well with the
gorgeous scenery and human fittings of “In
London Town.” The blind man has abated
nothing of his badness, and the credulity of Baker,
the millionaire, is as fresh as before. Also Albert
Norman continues to get pushed off the roof with
great success, after the sightless sinner aforesaid
nas received a violent inducement to fall down-
stairs. Some of the stairs had been removed
by the lodgers for firewood purposes, but these
§aps in the structure don’t appear to make any
ifference to the falling body. The audience can
hear blind Norrie bumping on his way, and they
seem glad to think that he probably lies dead on the
bottom stair. “In London Town” is full of
movement. In the second act Bland Holt and
Brown—Brown of the sore voice and capacious
smile—go through a form of harlequinade. Bland
chases Brown through a series of pantomimic
obstacles, but the fugitive ultimately escapes by
falling into a gigantic flour-bin. This happens in
the home of a struggling factory-girl, who stores
her kitchen flour by the half-ton.
*• * *
At the Opera House an alleged barn dance,
by the Dartos, is the latest attraction. Melbourne
has exhausted all its surprise concerning those
tour-de-force artists, and their recent efforts to
revive enthusiasm have been painfully fruitless.
The barn dance, as performed on Saturday,
was the saddest of endeavours. It merely
gave one to suppose that the dancing
ability of the Dartos begins and ends
in acrobatics. Finally the gentleman Dartos
picked up the lady Dartos and carried her off the
stage with her head downwards and her shapely
little legs extended like the arms of an opera
singer. But even this desperate novelty failed to
excite the audience. The pair of French Gartos
will appear until further notice.

concerts at the Town Hall has been cut off.

The Cobar Chesney mine, Cobar, have lately
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany for a pair of powerful winding engines to haul from
2000 feet. These engines are rapidly approaching com-
pletion, and will be delivered about first of next month.
The same firm of engineers have also supplied the Occi-
dental mine, Cobar, with a handsome pair of winding
engines, as well as a boiler. This machinery was delivered
in very quick time and should give great satisfaction.*

♦ * * *
The Mount Bulga Copper Co. has purchased
from N. Guthridge, Ltd., Agents for the Rand Drill Co.
of New York, a14x14 x 22 Rand Compressor. This has
already been sent up to the mine, and will be working very
shortly. This mine is likely to come into prominence. It
is under the same management as the North Mount Lyell.
The Mount Bulga Copper Co. has disclosed a lode between
well-defined walls 32ft. wide, and various bulk parcels of
ore have given an average of 7 per cent, copper. The
co. is now sinking a main shaft to strike the lode at
200 ft. or 100 ft. lower than the present deepest level. Ore
has been found for one mile along the same strike within
the co.’s boundary.

All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Yanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Ooolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

From a Dunedin correspondent:—M.L. dredg-
ing still languishes, owing to mismanagement. The slump
is intensified on account of abnormally high rivers,
which drive down silt and at the same time prevent
dredges from bottoming. The best returns are usually
got in the early winter, when frost sets in and snow is
held on the ranges. Consequently river falls and dredges
get on to their best ground. So far this winter has
been very mild, and the only perceptible “frost” is on
scrip lists. At time of writing the Molyneux and Ka-
warau Rivers are varying from 20 to 30ft. above normal,
and all dredging is at a standstill. Some dredges
are being washed away, and it is only with great
difficulty that dredges are held to moorings ; some have
actually sunk. Unless rivers fall and permit new dredges
to get going and brighten things up, there will be a
bigger slump than ever.

The Wild Cat Column.
In view of the fact that the finances of New
South Wales are growing more and more chaotic, and
that State has not only the biggest deficit in Australasia,
but one which more than equals all the other deficits put
together, The Bulletin desires to offer its particular plan
for getting rid of the trouble. Which is this:
(1) First of all, face the ghastly situation. Issue suffi-
cient 3 per cent, stock to put the Civil Service Superan-
nuation Fund and Police Superannuation Fund on an
absolutely solvent footing this will require about
£2,000,000. (The Civil Service Superannuation Fund is
insolvent because various Governments took the civil
servants’ money—their own contributions —for wrong-
ful purposes, and the State must settle this up.)
Issue sufficient 3 per cent, stock to pay off the
shortage in the Martin Place and Centennial Park
suspense accounts (about £528,000) ; to pay the war and
plague and celebration expenses (about £473,628); put all
of these and the present year’s deficit, if any, and the un-
paid year’s interest on the Sydney land resumptions into
the deficit also, and cover them by the issue of 3 per cent,
stock. Then transfer £500,000 of the ordinary public debt
to the deficit, as a counterbalance to the £500,000 or so of
each year’s expenditure which is left unpaid under the
Cash Basis Fraud.
(2) This will show the deficit at probably about
£5,700,000, which is just what it really is.
(3) Having repaid (in Government securities, anyhow)
every penny that has been borrowed from the Trust
Funds, let it be enacted that all the Trust Funds shall
hereafter be invested in Govt, securities (of N.S.W. or
other States) only, and that none of them shall be bor-
rowed for revenue purposes or other temporary expedi-
ents. Place them in charge of a committee, including
the Auditor-General and certain other carefully-selected
officials, who shall be wholly responsible for their invest-
ment, and put them wholly out of the Treasurer’s reach.
(4) Any moneys realised hereafter by the sale of Cen-
tennial Park or Martin Place building sites to be paid
over to the Auditor-General, and applied towards the re-
demption of the Treasury Bills issued to cover the
deficiency.
(5) Any surplus that may remain when the Civil Ser-
vice Superannuation fund finally' closes up many years
hence to I e similarly applied.
(6) All surpluses (there hasn’t been a genuine surplus
in N.S W. for 15 years or more, but there may be one
some da.y) to be similarly applied.
(7) The present pa.vment of £150,000 a year out of
revenue towards the deficit to be abolished —the money
will all be wanted for old age pensions, &c. But the land-
tax to be doubled, and the whole proceeds of the land-tax
(which would then be about £500,000 a year) to be paid
directly to the Auditor-General, and used in the redemp-
tion of the Treasury Bills issued against the deficit as
above-mentioned, none of the land-tax revenue going into
the hands of the Treasurer at all, or being included in the
revenue in anv shape or form. This process to continue
till the existing deficit, and any further deficits, are
wholly extinguished. As this process will leave the
State with insufficient revenue, the income-tax must be
largely increased, probably to Is. 3d. in the £, and new
taxes imposed. .
Tiie Bulletin recommends this, or some similar device,
as the only' means of shifting the incubus. No amount of
indignant inquiry as to how the great shortage arose, or
denunciation of its authors, or throwing out the Govern-
ments which did the sin, will pay a penny of it. The
deficit has been indignantly denounced and lectured upon
and held up to obloquy for 16 dreadful years, but it has
grown bigger all the time. Everything has been done to
it except the one essential —it hasn’t been paid not a,
penny of it. The only thing that can shift it is MONEY.
Let us get MONEY and shift it bodily.

The earnings are at the rate of £5 12s. lid. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the beginning of
the half-year (capital,’£ls2,o4o ; reserves, £26,958 ; total,
£178,998). The rate is good enough, yet as compared
with May, 1900, there is a very material falling away.
Judging by the State revenue returns, Tasmania’s recent
little boom is pegging out, and this is probably one of
the results. Business has apparently been about sta-
tionary during the half-year. Deposits have decreased
by about £4OO ; note circulation by about £2OO ; profits,
as shown above, are £4lO less. The cash reserve stands
at £99,299, which is £23,000 less than it was six months
ago, and represents about 19 per cent, on all liabilities to
the public—not a very large amount, but probably suffi-
cient for all ordinary purposes. The National Bank of
Tasmania is a small concern of good repute, but its re-
serves are not very strong 1 , and though it is doing much
better than it did four years ago its progress is not very
rapid even now. An addition of barely £4OOO to the re-
serves in six years can hardly be called fast travelling.
* * • *
Some figures and facts concerning Maoriland
State Life Insurance Department:
The life insurance fund now stands at £3,139,957 ; and
there is also a tontine fund of £20,826, and investment
fluctuation reserve of £36,297. So the Maoriland Govern-
ment Life Department still holds its place as the second
largest of Australasian life assurance institutions, only
the A.M.P. being in front of it. It also continues to keep
its place as the most cheaply-managed of Australasian
life offices, the A.M.P. only excepted. During the last
nine years its results in this direction have been as
follows :
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1892 .. £l5 7 7 per cent. 1897 .. £l4 17 2 per cent.
1893 .. 14 19 0 „ 1898 .. 14 6 9 „
1894 .. 15 1 7 „ 1899 .. 14 2 11 „
1895 .. 13 1 5 ~ 1900 .. 14 9 11 „
1896 .. 14 18 7 „ I
The last year hasn’t been quite so good as the one imme-
diately preceeding, but still the Maoriland State Depart-
ment'holds the highest place, bar one, for economy of
management.
The fall in interest rates still continues. Last
year the Maoriland State Life Insurance Department
earned £4 9s. 3d. per cent, on its average funds, which
compares with some previous interest earnings as follows:
1892 .. £5 6 2 per cent. 1897 .. £4 16 7 per cent.
1893 ..5 5 2 „ 1898 .. 4 14 0 „
1894 .. 5 6 0 „ 1899 .. 4 11 1 »
1895 .. 5 2 2 „ 1900 .. 4 9 3 „
1896 .. 4 19 0 „
The interest earnings are thus Is. lOd. per cent, less than
they were in 1899, as compared with the A.M.P.’s drop of
2s. 3d. per cent. The Maoriland State Department has
always been at a disadvantage in the matter of interest
earnings as compared with most Australian offices, for it
keeps a much larger proportion of its funds in Govern-
ment and municipal securities, and a smaller proportion
in mortgages, and thus, to some extent, sacrifices interest
for the sake of security. But on the other hand it hasn’t
been struck by the slump as the Australian offices have.
For instance, the Insurance and Banking Record, in 1891
and in 1900, showed the interest earnings of the leading
Australasian life offices as follows :
Interest earned per cent, on Average Funds.
1891. 1900. Decrease.
Victoria .. .. ..£8 0 2 £4 3 7 £3 16 7
Aus. Widows’ Fund .. 6 3 10 4 8 10 1 15 0
National Mutual .. .. 6 3 10 4 15 10 1 8 0
Temperance and General.. 60 10 462 1 14 8
Colonial Mutual .. .. 6 0 10 3 18 0 2 2 10
Mutual Life Association ..600 490 1 11 0
A.M.P 5 19 2 4 11 0 182
Australian Alliance .. 5 18 10 3 10 10 2 8 0
Maoriland Government ..58 10 493 0 19 7
The Maoriland Government Life Office’s assets stood the
shock of bad times better than all the others. In 1891 its
interest earnings were the poorest of the lot, and it was
the last in a list of nine. In 1900 it stood third on the
list of nine, only the National Mutual and A.M.P. being
ahead of it, while six offices that were far ahead of it in
4891 are now behind it—the Alliance, Colonial Mutual,
and Victoria so far behind as to be practically out of
sight.
The Government institution is also at a serious
disadvantage in the matter of expenses, for its business
is confined to Maoriland, where direct taxation —the only
kind that strikes a life office—is almost twice as heavy as
the Australian average. Its expenses also form a heavier
per centage by reason of the lowness of its premiums,
which are about the most moderate in Australasia.
Under all these conditions it can fairly claim to be the
most successful of Australasian life offices, even if it didn’t
throw in (as it does) a State guarantee for the payment of
all policies. Its assets look distinctly better than the
average. Out of over £3,100,000 of assets, foreclosed pro-
perties stand for less than £5600. Less than half the
money is invested in mortgages, and the balance is mostly
loans on policies and State securities. Overdue interest
is almost nil, and these are all features which speak well
for the soundness of the investments.
* * * *
The Liverpool and London and Globe Insur-
ance Co., which does a large fire business and a certain
amount of life business at this end of the globe, shows
these results in its life and annuity department:
Premiums
including Added to
considera- Interest, Total Life and
tion for &c. Income. Expenses. Annuity
Annuities. Funds.
1895 .. £551,206 £185,318 £736,524 £28,816 £251,578
1896 .. 381,203 190,928 572,131 29,249 147,246
1897 .. 396,848 194,136 590,984 30,673 182,944
1898 . 425,567 197,909 623,476 30,831 30,464
1899 462,484 200,232 662,716 30,992 133,642
1900 .. 430,989 198,550 629,539 31,517 117,727
The total life and annuity funds of the Liverpool and
London and Globe are now £5,472,078 (life fund,
£3,778,121; and annuity fund, £1,693,957). In addition
to these funds there is a paid-up capital of £245,640 ; a
general reserve fund of £1,300,000; investment fluctua-
tion fund of £208,003 ; and undivided profits amounting
to £1,076,999, so the reserves available for the protei tion
of policy-holders may be set down at over £2,800,000, in
addition to the assurance fund which, in itself, is sufficient
to cover all liabilities under policies. This means an
enormous amount of security, and it is the only point on
which a first-class proprietary life office can claim to be
better than a mutual one. The mutual office never sets
itself to accumulate a general reserve, apart from its
assurance fund and reserve for depreciation of securities.
It is difficult to know why it shouldn’t, but it doesn’t.
Unfortunately the Australasian proprietary life offices
now in active business are not at all like the L. and L.
and G. in this respect.
The Liverpool and London and Globe doesn’t
push much for new life business, and its progress
isn’t rapid. Through not hustling for much new business
its expenses are very small —£5 os. Id. per cent, on total
income, a rate that even the A.M.P. can’t approach. In-
terest earned is about £3 13s. per cent, on the average
amount of the assurance and annuity fund for the year,
which is good enough according to British rates, though
far below the Australian average. The L. and L. and
G. is a most undeniably safe concern for assurers, and its
bonuses are remarkably good, though it doesn’t get
alongside the A.M.P. in this respect. Still it would be a
great deal more joyful to be the heir of a large original
shareholder in the L. and L. and G. than to be insured in
it. The dividend for last year was 90 per cent.

If the stories current ’concerning the merits of
that Sydney ship-broking dispute are anything like true,
what a pity, in the public interest, that the matter was
not ventilated in the law-courts, instead of being squared
privately. , « *
Wyalong (N.S.W.) municipal council has had
enough of law-fighting with the local mines over the right
basis for taxation ; but the Broken Hill Council, all but
two Laborites, keeps the lawyers fed. Last year it spent
£lOOO without getting the main question touched at all ;
this year it has put £5OOO down on the estimates—at
least half of its total revenue 1 Why doesn’t the State
Parliament pass a law defining the position ? The pre-
sent uncertainty has lasted for years.
» * * *
This year, so far, 16 men have been killed in
Broken Hill mines—proportionately a far greater number
than during ’OO. It may be only a coincidence that since
the beginning of this year only one mining inspector has
been employed instead of two; and even that one has
had no previous experience of big workings. And yet, in
view of the lead-slump and consequent economies, State
supervision in the working’ miners’ interests has been
particularly desirable lately.
At their wits’ ends to make exes, at present
metal-prices, the Broken Hill co.’s are arranging to ask
for reduced freights on ore products over the S.A. rail-
ways. While they are about it they might ask for a yet
bigger reduction from the Silverton Tram Co., which still
makes its 40 or 50 per cent, a year.
* * *
If a buyer can be got for the Broken Hill Junc-
tion in England, it will most likely go that way; the
directors don’t yet see any other plan for netting the
necessary capital to re-open the mine. The likeliest
buyers are people interested in zinc-extraction.
* * * »
To inquire into the Broken Hill South mining
disaster Crown-Prosecutor C. J. Wade has been appointed
a Royal Commissioner. And Barrier miners remember
that Judge Murray, sent on an almost precisely similar
errand three years ago, reported that the N.S.W. mining
regulations were thoroughly rotten and insufficient to
protect the workmen in any mine (Judge Murray’s
italics). That was three years ago, and the same regula-
tions still remain ! But the inquiry did something—it
stopped grumbling for a while. By the way, it is just
about three years since the last N.S.W. general election.
* * » *
To help along the smelter, a complete and up-to-
date concentrating plant is being put up at Lloyd’s
copper-mine, Burraga, out from Bathurst. Broken Hill
experience is being made use of.
* * • *
Mt. Boppy: Some 20 miles from Cobar, now
running a 40-head stamp mill which came from Gally-
mont mine, one of the failures of the Anglo-Australian
Co. There is every reason to think that the co. has at
last found a good mine in Mt. Boppy. Three shafts sunk
on the lode, extending over an area of 1000 ft. Levels put
out at the 100 and 200 ft. Lode (quartz) runs from 6 to
14ft. thick, averaging about 10ft; gold fine, but free mill-
ing. Returns not given. Gold contents of ore believed
to be about 14dwt. per ton, and understood that half-an-
oz. is got by amalgamation and cyaniding, balance being
in the slimes, of which the ore makes about 25 per cent.,
and for the treatment of which filter-pressing plant has
been decided on.
* * * »
The Commonwealth : A mine eight miles from
Wellington, N.S.W. Said to be a promising concern. 200
tons sent to Dapto and averaged loz. 15dwt. of gold with
an excellent percentage of silver. A few tons sent to
Cockle Creek averaged loz. 17dwt. gold, and 22ozs.
silver. Said of it, the greater the depth the greater the
richness, but then that is said of all N.S.W. mines. Let
us hope it is true of the Commonwealth.
* * * *
North Cobar, N.S.W. : The prospecting shaft
is down 300 ft. It is being sunk much further to the
west than any previous prospecting work, as it is under-
stood that the last run of ore worked in Great Cobar
copper-mine is pitching away in the direction of this
prospecting shaft. Occasional seams of quartz, stained
with copper and iron, are met' with, but no opening-out
will be done till the shaft is down 300 ft. The North
Cobar is a big gamble, but it may be well worth the
trouble.
* * * *
Mt. Drysdale, N.S.W. : Mill shut down for
want of water, and although there is said to be plenty
ore that would pay for local treatment, it will not stand
heavy haulage and smelting charges. Main shaft going
down another 100 ft. Mine now much better equipped,
and it is intended to cut down the main shaft, making it
a decent size.
* * * *
North Bushy Hill, N.S.W. “ The shares which
a fortnight ago were forced up to the fictitious value of
over 205., were yesterday quitted at 4s.”—which is an un-
usually forcible phrase for Sydney D.T’s mining columns.
A well-known mining man, just returned from the
locality, says certain Bushy Hill shows are the latest
exhibitions of N.S.W. mining folly, and give an example
of the ignorance of some people working there. They
asked his opinion of the metal they were obtaining from
pestle and mortar tests of a hard white quartz, and were
much surprised to learn that which they thought was
silver or something equally valuable, was iron filings
broken away from the pestle and mortar in pounding
the quartz.
* • • *
“A Crippled Industry:” “Sydney Sunday
Times will next week initiate a campaign having for its
object the relief of the mining industry in respect of the
many injustices under which it labors. The attention of
persons interested in the industry is directed to this
matter, and information and expressions of opinion are
invited from them.” Writer suggests the publication in
the Sunday Times of an alphabetical list of all the mines
the purchase of shares in which has been frequently ad-
vised by its own Sabbath tipster, and which have since
passed into liquidation or oblivion without returning any
of the money wasted on them, to say nothing of the
amount of money lost in share-dealing. That is one of
the many injustices under which N.S.W. mining has
labored, and it has done as much to bring about a “crip-
pled industry” as anything this scribe knows of.
Croesus Proprietary, W.A.: “Latest assays
ranged between lOdwt. and 3oz. All the development
work is carried out by hand, so progress is slow. The
installation of air-drills would much hurry matters, and,
with very little trouble, a supply of air could be got from
the recently-erected compressor at Hannan’s Croesus
mine.” All the Croesus Proprietary wants is more gold.
Newspapers supply all the wind it needs.
* • » *
Croesus South: “ Extensive development work
is being done on the oxidised zone, with improved results.
Lode averaging 3 to Bft., and worth by battery treatment
BJdwt. per ton.” How does this correspond with the
official cable sent to London a few weeks ago“ 2250
tons milled for 3030 z. Expenses, £2OOO. Shutting down.”
The ore in the Croesus is so poor that the mine might be
more appropriately called the Lazarus.
Hannan’s Brownhill: “The diamond drill is
still operating from the True Blue lease, and another drill
is now at work on the Jubilee lease. Although no
particulars are obtainable the management slates that
recently the development has been very satisfactory and
there are no fears of supplies of ore running short. The
Diehl plant is running smoothly and is reported to be
securing a good extraction at a fairly low cost.” No par-
ticulars are available of the amount of drilling-work al-
ready done, probably some thousands of feet. If any-
thing had been struck it would have leaked out.
This mine has been getting 5000 oz. a month, worth
£lB,OOO, with a local cost of over £lO,OOO. Shares
have started to come down. A few weeks ago they were
over 905.; as this is written they are well under 80s., and
but for the number oversold they would be under 60s.
The days of the mine are numbered. Its ore is running
out. Kalgoorlie Sun devotes 3 cols, to an exposure of the
ways in which the B.H. shareholders have been hood-
winked.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia (W.A.): Producing over 5000 oz.
a month, and has been working a long time without div.
Said to be the best low-grade proposition in W.A., having
a formation that will probably live to any depth. Work-
ing two lodes, A and B, the latter probably a slice or
lense from the main lode, but both in the same channel,
each having an underlay as regular as a clock of about
45deg. The mine will probably be working when a lot of
others out there are forgotten. Has a fine battery and
cyanide plant, stacking the slimes ; a big lOdwt. show ;
development a little behind, but the mine will soon get
ahead with that.
• ■* * •
Cosmopolitan (W. A.): Main lode averages 7ft.
thick and worked 990 ft. long ; in the bottom level, 400 ft.
Not too much ore opened up considering the equipment
going on. There is said to be a fault in the south drive
which, if it continues, might upset calculations re the
deeper levels. Putting up a new 50-stamp mill.
* * * m
Lake View and Boulder Junction: “A recon-
struction of the co. is imminent, and until some satis-
factory arrangements are arrived at, no work will be car-
ried out on the property.” But why reconstruct a proved
duffer ? It was so named to lead people to think that the
Lake View and Boulder lodes would junction in it. They
never did, and never will.
• » * »
Berry Consols Extended (Vic.): For the half-year
42940 z., realising £18,387, out of which £2OOO paid in
divs. The reason why : board-fees, £32 10s. ; salaries,
£lO4 ; visiting-fees and travelling expenses, £26. If the
mine had a London office it would take more than £2OOO
to run it.
• * * *
Glenfine South : Adelaide Critic avers that
“ Glenfine South is not going to snuff out, in spite of the
directors and the wreckers.” Some people would be
thankful if the Critic had not boomed these shares by
saying they were going to double in value when they
were nearly four times present price, and followed them
all down the line, blaming everything and everybody but
the mine itself, when it had palpably gone wrong. Noth-
ing short of the marvellous can put the mine back into
the position it occupied last year. It has a magnificent
reef in one level, the run of gold in which has been mostly
gutted, but the reef does not go down in size or quality.
The public has gone down instead.
* * •
Clrillagoe Mines : Shares suffered a “knock-
out ” last week when they fell from 21s. to 16s. They
have recovered, say 25., since and it is given out that the
directors, to re-establish public confidence, and in “ con-
sequence of rumors ” are to personally examine the
property. Adelaide Critic, which has always been over-
fond of booming Chillagoes, says there is no reason for the
decline, that the first smelting will be a good one. The
Bulletin says there are many substantial reasons for the
knock-out, and would not be surprised to soon see the
shares down to 10s. Here are a few reasons. The de-
parture for China of the £5OOO-a-year manager Stewart so
soon after his return from England. The uncertainty of
his ever coming back. The fact that the railway has taken
nearly double the estimated time to build and that it has
probably cost far more than was expected. The delay in
the erection of the smelters, and, above all, the colorless
official reports dealing with mine-developments. The
formations are lateral, mostly in limestone, spread over
too large an area of country with no proved depth, and so
far removed from the sea-board as to make their working
expensive. An enormous amount of money has already
gone into the undertaking (ominous word !), and much
more must follow before it produces copper. The better
policy would have been to spend, say, £lOO,OOO in
prospecting and developing the property before building
a railway and erecting furnaces for mines', the permanency
of which has not been established.

LEROY
LIQUEUR
BRAMDY
Ihe one episode which, so far, has really
brightened up the Duke of York’s dreary path
hereabouts happened in serious-minded Maori-
-1 and, of all places in this world. Seddon’s two
islands laid themselves out for a stupendous grovel,
and were fully resolved to make such an exhibition
of themselves as Mr. and Mrs. York would never
forget. And at Wellington things broke down. A
big review was held in the mud of a wet and
bitter day, and after it was over the army got
mislaid. The officers were so busy following the
Duke back to Wellington and letting him see
their beautiful clothes, that they forgot they ever
had an army. It was left standing in a paddock,
up to its knees or thereabouts in mud, and
wondering what to do next. Nobody told
it what to do next. Night came on, and the lost
legion was still prowling about in a cold, boggy
camp, with no food for itself or for its joint and
several horse. In the end, a large section of the
indignant and neglected force girded up its empty
stomach and walked into town to see if it could
get a bun or something, and there also it found
nothing provided and nobody responsible.
Therefore it came to pass that the military
forces of Maoriland, 1)00 strong, proceshed the
streets of Wellington carrying lumps of bad meat
(presumably borrowed from friendly butchers) on
poles and explaining things’; and sang the “Dead
March outside Government House, where York
was holding a levee, and practically shook their
mouldy beef and their grievances in the face of
the Heir Apparent. Of course, the Duke became
aware that Maoriland’s mislaid and unfed army
was outsjde and going on in a quite unusual
fashion. There was a sudden waking up in
high military circles. The road out to the camp
was almost blocked by the forgotten provisions
rushing out in the dead of night. An apoplectic
commander and a whole staff of officers came after
the provisions, and when the commander had used
terrible language to his subordinates, he had the
men paraded and talked to them as if he was John
Norton’s Open Letter to the Apostle Paul. Then
he demanded the names of the ringleaders, and
the men, cheered-up by the provisions that had
arrived in the night, simply howled with laughter
and asked him, almost in so many words, where
he got that hat.
A large blot has thus fallen on Maori-
land’s li itional grovel. York will always
remember it as t:ie only place where he
was sung to by a mislaid military force carry-
ing decayed sausages and bullock’s liver on
poles. He can never again take quite seriously a
commander who fo'rgot his army, and found it
during the night wandering round in disorder
trying to borrow food. The rest of the Yorks’
visit will be one long struggle to look sei'ious at
military functions, and not to laugh at gorgeous
creatures in epaulettes. The tale of the troops
whose officers lost them in a mud-hole in a time of
profound peace, through their anxiety to show the
Duke their nice clothes, will go round the British
papers, and the Agent-General will probably lose
his head, and write denying it, and fall in. Taking
it all round, it is about the worst tiling that has
happened to people of intense dignity since the
last time a king fell over a forgotten coal-scuttle
on the stairs. •
If Maoriland was only a member of the Com-
monwealth, so that its military affairs came under
the notice of the Federal Parliament, what a
thrilling spectacle it would be to find King
O’Malley rising to ask something like this :
(1) Is there any other part of the Empire where the
present Heir Apparenthas been tumultuously approached
by his father’s troops carrying decayed meat ?
(2) Will the Government appoint a Commission to look
up British history and find out if decayed meat was ever
•previously brought under the notice of the Royal Family
in like manner ?
(3) Does the Government regard the incident as high
treason or merely as calculated to injure the trade in
frozen mutton ?
» * * *
Postmaster-General Ward and ex-Lands-
Minister M’Kenzie of Maoriland have been
knighted. Two more little baubles from George
of York’s small bagful of degradations! Hall-
Jones, Minister of Works, wasn’t knighted, and
isn’t likely to be. The hyphenated Jones, who
was commonly supposed to be a mild and humble
man, was requested to leave his rooms at a hotel
to make more space for the ducal party, and his
refusal made Seddon so angry that Jones was re-
quested either to leave the hotel or leave the
Ministry. It isn’t yet known which of the two
Jones left, but if he refused to leave either, and
had to be carried out, it would be a very interest-
ing situation. There was a time in N.9.W. when
a mild Premier named Jennings tried to eject a
long, bony and indignant subordinate named Dibbs
out of a Ministry. The result was that they both
got out, but Jennings was out first.
* » » *
The refusal of the Maoriland Minister for
Works to give up his bunk in a Wellington pub.
for the use of the Duljeanduchess has, despite its
inexpressible brutality, a somewhat refreshing
side. Every other Minister with whom their
Highnesses have come into contact would have
been passionately pleased to give up his little back
room to them and'go and roost in the ti-tree with
the hens.

On Nelson (M.L.) sea coast the water has
been covered with a curious slime that has quite
spoilt for food the local oysters and fish. It is
suspected to be the slime of the great god
Grovel, which is now hovering over M.L.

Sydn ’s Public Library has been falling
to pieces mo e or less for years, and
it is so crimped that when Mr.
Mitchell offered the authorities a
present of 15,000 valu-
able volua.es, with"
many more to f 1 ow,
tney could only accept
10,000, and even these
had to be stacked out of
sigl t in the librarian’s resi-
dence. The Public Library
question is a curious instance
of how a bg matter moulders
and petrifies, until the inten-
tion to do something about it
becomes a sort of heirloOm,
like an Egyptian pyramid.
In 1879 £150,000 was voted for
a new Sydney Public Library,
and plans were prepared, in
1880 a site was purchased on
which to build the new Library.
In 1881 Parliament selected a
new site. In 1882 Parkes
proposed another new site,
waere the Benevolent
Asylum now stands. In
1883 still another site
was chosen, and more
plans prepared. In
1884, part of the old
crowded establishment
was rebuilt to prevent
it falling down (cost

S.M. Herald cross heads as “an histori-
cal coincidence ” the reference made by a M.L
Mayor, in welcoming the Yorks, to the fact “ that
the Yorks landing at Wellington occurred on the
anniversary of Waterloo. ” These historical coin-
cidences are really remarkable. Writer landed
in Australia outlie anniversary of the Massacre of
Glencoe, and lie knows a friend whose fifth child
was born on the anniversary of the Battle of Sem-
pach. Come to think of it, almost anybody goes
somewhere or does something on the day that
something or other happened some time or other.

The Adelaide University has planted an
extensive lawn for the Dookoyork’s own benefit.
It is to be abolished when he has gone, because it
would otherwise necessitate the attention of a
gardener.
MUTINY.
When the Jook was being- entertained at Government
House, Wellington (M.L.), last week, 900 local troops,
mostly volunteers, were laagered outside the town in a
lagoon of liquid mud. They were without orders, with-
out officers (their officers were clinking their spurs in the
corridors of Government House), without forage for their
horses, and without fo.od for themselves—except some--
baleful bullock-meat, which they refused to touch. They
were left there to stand up all night or lie down in the
mud. They did neither; but organised a procession,
heralded by pieces of deceased bullock carried on poles,
and, stopping before Government House, played the
“ Dead March ” for the edification of the Jook. Next
morning the commandant, Colonel Pole-Penton, rode out
to the camp in a lather'of fury and called them “ infernal
curs and cowards, who had disgraced their country.”
Lo, this bold yeomanry, its country’s pride
its country s shame unless with food supplied.
Their kin heroic upon Orange veldt,
And Transvaal kopje made their valor felt,
And rode red-spurred into the fiercest fray.
Un half a biscuit to a man per day ;
And slept at night in beds of liquid mud,
And in the morning blithely bathed in blood ;
And broke their hearts to show, in squalid strife,
their loyalty was more to them than life.
T pon their land their deeds a glory shed
i hey crowned her with a halo bloody-red.
m? a^as " was their spilth of gore—
iheir kinsmen have disgraced her evermore.
Pole-Penton well might call them cur and hound
Who could not camp one night on sodden ground,
Ivor eat sick bullock’s corpse, once in a way,
1 heir love for dear old England to display.
But they must go with green meat on a hook,
And play the “Dead March” to the tremblings
Dook.
That Dead March direful—was it meant to be
Eor the dead bullock, or for Koyalty ?

anp Troubles.
Premier Peacock expected to be in hot
water in his new Leg. Assembly Chamber in
Melbourne Exhibition, but the hot water was not
turned on, and a wintry blast surged through the
room, giving to the setting a ghastly suggestive-
ness of the morgue, with all the legislators as sub-
jects lying on ice. It was the most frost-bitten,
chilblained, cold-blasted Parliament that ever sat
in Australia ; the only sound heard in the chamber
for five minutes at a stretch was the helpless chat-
tering of 50 sets of human teeth. Members
wrapped themselves in floor-rugs and table-cloths
and sat like galvanised mummies. A few unsuc-
cessfully tried to make fire-water a substitute for
the promised hot water, and the spectacle of one
refrigerated member struggling to repress the
chattering of a double-barrelled set of porcelain
teeth with spring fittings, and prevent them
dancing out on to the floor, whilst expressing his
disgust with a Cabinet that didn’t know enough to
come in out of the cold, was a most touching thing
to see.
* V * •
The migration of Vic. State Parliament to
Melb. Exhibition Building has thrown experienced
and habitual M’s.P. very much out of their rec-
koning. Most of them could find their way to the
old House even if blind, and occasionally one or
two of them did. But instinct is no guide now,
and a resident in Nicholson-st. furiously complains
that he has been knocked up twice by belated
gentlemen who want to (hie !) know if “thish ish
the Victoriannousher Parliament.” In the dark
and early hours of a.m. following the first sitting
of State Parliament this session, a watchman, dis-
turbed by a far-off, mysterious knocking, traced
the sounds to the great entrance facing Nicholson-
st., and found a bedraggled politician pounding
wearily at the big door with his knuckles. He
said he’d been looking for the House all night,
and refused to go away without a royal commission
or something. He was left knocking.
• • * •
Gov.-Gen. Hopetoun turned on a full head
of gush at the Parliamentary dinner in Melbourne
t’other day. Opening his arms, he took Australia
to his bosom, crying with an affecting little break
away down behind his larynx :
Gentlemen, I love Australia. (Oheers.) I have given
the best years of my life to one of the greatest of her
States. I also love the man in the street. (Hear, hear.)
I love his wonderful orderliness and his good nature,
especially when he is collected together in large crowds
such as we h ive seen him collected of late. I admire and
appreciate his sense of humour.
His Lordship appreciates Australia’s sense of
humour! Whatever sense of humour the Aus-
tralian has got is set going by the post-prandial
pappycock of elevated personages who take them-
selves and their little emotions with such dreadful
seriousness, and find wonderful portents of loya l ty
and love in the don’t-care-a-blankv-ness of the
average crowd in these parts. When His Ex.
talks largely and impressively of having given the
best years of his life to Australia, the Australian
sense of humour bursts loose, and you can hear
the kookaburra sing from Carlton to Dim Dis-
tance, which is Back o’ Beyond.
A Melbourne time-payment-picture man,
in default of a few shillings balance due, recently
essayed to seize on two oleographs. The two
house-ladies objected, and the pictures were
smashed in the course of some remarks that
fell from the various parties concerned,
also the picture-man was violently seized
by the leg when trying to depart through
the open window, and trailed all over the
furniture. He retaliated, but the ladies
stuck to their prey while the policeman
came, when they charged their
creditor with assault. At the
Court they told the magistrate
that they didn’t wish to be too
hard, and wouldn’t press the
charge if accused would pay costs.
He paid £2. Truly it is wonder-
ful what a woman will go through
to save her oleograph. A lioness
robbed of her whelps is nothing
to a suburban female deprived of
a shocking picture of a child
and a dog in a pine frame.

* * * *
Painful indeed is the cant mouthed by big
personages who have felt called upon to express
opinions in public concerning the recent terrible
misfortunes of Victorians at the war. In every
case, after a spasm of Chadbandish snuffle, the
comforters have wound up with a stodgy burst of
eloquence about the Glory reflected upon the
country and the lustre shed upon the names of
these gallant boys, who have asserted their
courage by dying heroic deaths for their country.
Passing over the fact that the unfortunate boys
have not died for their country, but whilst en-
deavouring to steal other men’s country for a third
party, one may question the heroism, glory, Ac.,
&c., attendant on being prosaically shot down like
rabbits in a vulgar scramble, all over before any
man in the crowd had time to exhibit any particu-
lar brand of moral grandeur. God !itis a terrible
thing to be slaughtered in a foreign land merely
to provide a pack of good, greasy, fat-headed
blatherskites at home opportunities of committing
oratorical nuisances in public.

The advertising-joke business flourishes
in Sydney, to the great profit of the lawyers. Last
week appeared in the dailies an advt. calling upon
the electioneering friends of a certain prim and
well-groomed ex-alderman to meet “at Mr. R.
Howard’s. Ben Buckler-road, Bondi, Thursday
evg., 8 o’clock. 1.0.G.T.” Now, Mr. R. Howard
is Sydney’s popular hangman.
♦ • m *
The failure of a certain lady, in the hotel
line of business, to get at Sydney Morning
Herald for damages, is a gratifying circumstance
from some points of view. The grievance of Mrs.
Public-house arose through a marriage-advertise-
ment. It set forth that a lady was to let for pur-
poses of honorable matrimony, and gave her
initials and address. As a result, she claimed to
have suffered much grief, trouble, and public dis-
respect, and to have been rushed by strange men
offering her their more-or-less soiled and undesir-
able hands in union. She said that she didn’t
insert the advt., and all that the Herald could
say was that it didn’t know whether she did or
not. It had offered to explain publicly that
the advt. was a sinful hoax, and, that offer
being refused, it asked what more could be
fairly expected. It might demand, in such
cases, that the advt. should be verified by the
signature of the alleged advertiser, but that
wouldn’t make matters any better, as it couldn’t
tell whether the signature was real or not. It
might go further and insist that it be verified by
the actual presence of the advertiser, but then it
couldn’t tell whether he or she was genuine or
bogus. If it demanded that the advertiser should
be identified it would then be necessary to identify
the identifier. So the aggrieved Mrs. Public
House kept on saying that she didn’t insert or
authorise the advt., and the paper kept on
ing that it didn’t know, and the jury probably
decided that, in any case, a good-looking woman
who kept a public-house might receive worse in-
sults than a dozen offers of marriage. Anyhow it
gave the paper a verdict.

The decision of the N.S.W. Attorney-
General to proceed no further in the charge of
murder against Mrs. Jane Smith meets with
public approval. She stood three trials before
three Judges and three juries, and three disagree-
ments followed. Of course, fourth trials have in
similar eases been insisted on, with the result, as
CAREFUL STUDY.
STAGE MANAGER: “ w ell, do you think you can
improve on that dying scene of yours?”
SICK TRAGEDIAN : “ I ought to, considering I
have been rehearsing all night!”
lately in Melbourne, the acquittal of the accused,
and, as some years ago in Sydney, the accused’s
conviction. Louisa Collins was tried four times
and, afcer three disagreements, was found guilty
and executed. New evidence was, however, in-
troduced at the fourth trial. The ordinary rule
should be, and it is generally so in England,
that unless new evidence has been adduced, con-
viction on a third tiial is not followed by the
death penalty. An affirmative verdict of the
third 12 men must be weighed for what it is
worth against the inability of the 24 preceding
men to find that verdict.

The acquittal of George Muir Houston,
late a clerk in Vic. Mines Department, led Judge
ON THE STAGE.
ROMEO (wtto voce): “ Will you marry me?”
JULIET: “Never, you brute!” (Hissing.) “ I'd
dance on your grave first!”
A’Beckett to make disparaging remarks about the
jury—seemingly with more justification than
usual. The trouble arose through the disappear-
ance of one Ryan’s money. Ryan was in arrear
with the rent of his timber lease, and Houston, in
his official capacity, urged him to pay up. So
Ryan came to Melbourne and paid Houston, at
the Mines Office, £l3l odd in settlement. Houston
was not the man authorised to receive money, and
he declined at first to take it; but Ryan, who ap-
parently reckoned it was sufficient if he paid
the money to any one in the office, and got a
receipt, and who was probably tired of
losing himself in the corridors of the public
buildings, pressed it on him. That was ap-
parently the last ahybody saw of the money,
and Houston was charged with larceny as
a bailee of Ryan’s hard-earned loot. It
was urged for the defence that Houston
wasn’t legally a bailee because Ryan didn’t tell
him to hand over the money to anybody
else; the confiding timber-getter merely dabbed
it down on the counter and took it for granted it
would reach its destination. The jury found
Houston guilty of misappropriating, but said it
wasn’t sure the money was Ryan’s at all, and as the
charge was one of appropriating Ryan’s money the
result, after much mental wandering, was acquittal.
The Crown, apparently, won’t charge Houston
with taking its money, because if it acknowledged
the coin as its money received from Ryan, then it
must admit that Ryan’s rent is paid. And the
jury won’t convict Houston of taking Ryan’s
money because it has a half-formed suspicion that
the money is the Crown’s money. So there is
very little satisfaction for Ryan either way.
* * * *
The amendment of the Vic. Marriage Act,
which enables a girl to force the alleged father of
her expected infant to plank down money to cover
the expenses of “the event,” is going to do away
with the favorite plan of identification by re-
semblance. It is always foolish to be like another
man’s child, but it is less dangerous now. Con-
sidering that so many Melbourne men have had to
pay for looking like certain babies, isn’t it fair
that the man who is compelled to pay before
arrival should get his money back if the expected
little stranger proves to be remarkably like the
other fellow ?
* * * *
The Victorian Weekly Post and Tele-
graph Guide notifies that the Postmaster-General
has directed that no communication addressed to
a certain person at Newtown, Sydney, shall be
transmitted or delivered through the post-office of
Victoria, and that no Victorian money-order shall
be issued in his favor. The person named is an
advertising astrologer. Now, why should there
be one law for the professional star-gazer and
another for the fortune-teller and palmist ?

Vic. Chief-Justice Madden recently dis-
missed the divorce petition of a lady who said
that her husband didn’t have a bath for five or six
years, and had latterly added desertion to his
other iniquities. Madden, it seems, didn’t abso-
lutely disbelieve the story £>f respondent’s want of
a wash, but he said, in his large, sententious
voice, that when a woman married she took her
husband “with all his imperfections on his head.”
Which showed a shocking want of logic in two
different ways: The lady took her husband with
all his imperfections, but the matter at issue was
that he later acquired new imperfections which
she didn’t take him with. Further he was charged
with having ieft off washing his body as well as
his head.
* * * *
The sum and substance of the platitudes
of Waxellency Madden in a dozen Melb. divorce
cases recently, seems to be that His Honor would
like to try solitary confinement as a way of making
a young husband continue to be passionately fond
of his wife after the first twelve months.

THE OLD CASHIERS.
“ Where are the snows of autumn?”
The Poet Villon cried.
In vain, in vain he sought ’em
On moorland and hillside.
But I, far more forlorn than he,
Cry out, across the years—
Where are the men that stood hy me-*
Where are the Old Cashiers ?
Maiyy a time I tasked them
For half-a-quid or crown,
But ev’ry time I asked them,
They planked the money down.
They did not make a fool of me ;
And so, I say with tears —
Where are the men who used to be,
Where are the Old Cashiers ?
Says one—“ What are you wishing ? ”
With pleasant silver guile—
“ I’m going to go fishing,”
I’d answer with a smile.
“ You’re just like what I used to be
Your lie my old age cheers.”
And he’d give seven-and-six to me—
Where are the Old Cashiers ?
Fine Old Cashiers, a-many,
In bygone days I knew ;
But now there’s hardly any
Who’ll cash my 1.0. U.
In vain, in vain I’ve sought ’em,
And so I ask with tears—
Where are the snows of autumn ?
Where are the Old Cashiers ?
C.R.

The Senate's positive and immovable stand
on the Supply Bill, when it kicked that necessary
document hack to the Govt, with an intimation
to introduce it properly, shows that that House is
determined to assert its rights. This was easily
foreseen when Chaffey Baker succeeded in gaining
the Presidential chair. Twice already Baker
has jumped on to P.M.G. Drake, who is in
charge of the aggressive chamber. Once when he
returned the Postal Bills to the Govt, printer to
alter all the italics which were supposed to
represent clauses beyond the Senate’s power of
revision, and again when he curtly informed
Drake, who had just recited a formal “message”
from the Governor-General to the House, that he
(the President) would in future deliver all such
communications to the Senate. It was to him
(the President) that the G.-G. addressed himself,
and he (the President) would hereafter acquaint
the House with any views or wishes that the
G. wished to convey.

“ Art ” : Quite agree with B. pars re beauty
of female form. Such pars may be advts. for P.D.
corsets, but they are true just the same. One sees
so many willowy waists gliding along the Melb. and
Sydney streets that one’s arm wishes to get to work
upon them. That’s human nature, anyway. The
mission of the P.D. is to present the female form
in its most inviting aspect to the male eye—and
arm. And nobly not to say scrumptiously —is
that mission fulfilled.*
The smoking public of Australasia will
probably be interested to learn that W. D. and
H. O. Wills. Ltd., of Bristol, England, in con-
junction with Heyde, Todman and Co., of Sydney,
have made arrangements to manufacture their
tobaccos intended for consumption in Federated
Australia in the latter city. Last year, Mr. G. F.
Todman entered into negotiations with Messrs.
W. D. and H. 0. Wills, Ltd., the outcome being
the amalgamation of the Australian interests of
the two firms. The joint Australian business will
be under the supervision of Mr. G. F. Todman
(late principal of Heyde, Todman and Co.) and
Mr. A. J. Warry (late of W. D. and H. O. Wills,
Ltd., Bristol), and the co. will trade under the
title of W. D. and H. O. Wills (Australia), Ltd.
It will manufacture both the proprietary lines of
the late Heyde, Todman and Co. and of Cameron
Bros, and Co., also the tobaccos hitherto manu-
factured in Bristol by W. D. and H. O. Wills,
Ltd., and will distribute throughout Australia all
those different lines under interstate Freetrade.

A WOMANS LETTER
_ . Sydney, Jane 25, 1901.
Mr Dear Arini,—
There s a large vacuum in Sydney consequent
on the flight of Fat Clara, the Gippsland lady who
used to exhibit her 39-stone self m George-street.
The other day she waddled down to the G.P.O.
Savings Bank, either to plant more wealth or to
draw it, followed by staring hundreds, and when
she came out again the police had to make a lane
for her through those who love a free show. Ciara
and the Duke of York met in London some years
ago, and he was quite pleased to see her again here
and shake her hand by sections, and chat awhile
on whatever Fat Ladies and Dukes talk of when
they foregather. Clara is still 80 inches round the
waist, and still protests that she does not tight-
lace. She cannot be ambitious or she would go
to Turkey and be a Sultana, jewel-laden, and
shake a marble palace with her tread, and get
high offices and titles for Gippslanders when the
rush set in Turkeyward.
Few men with a keen sense of humor, or of the
eternal fitness of things, bear acquired titles.
Just look at Way, Bart, intimating to the
Board of Adelaide Art Gallery that efforts were
being made to induce the Duke of York to un-
veil the painting “Love and Death” recently
bought by said gallery. Many of us know
of Watts famous picture. We all know why
Princess May is Duchess of Cornwall and York
instead of being Duchess of Clarence and Avon-
dale. She would be at George’s side when he un-
veiled a picture that must remind them both of—
well, .of Death and Love. What was that Laureate
Austin wrote about her “ forever doomed to wear
the mockery of widowhood around her maiden
hair.” No wonder officialism must use the words
“efforts’’and “induce.”
Do the Yorks keep diaries ? If so, and if they’ve
a sense of humor, what reading there will be for
father Ned ! Intelligence had come through
of the episode on board the yacht Shamrock, and
the Duke was holding a reception. An elderly
gentleman, swelling with a desire to say some-
thing sympathetic, was presented. Looking at
York steadily for a second he murmured, “Sad
noos this about yer Pa. ain’t it ? ”
Talk about funeral baked meats! In strict
harmony with the slump in York stocks is the
fact that those decorative Sydney street obelisks
were last week auctioned for'five shillings each!
Some of them cost over £lOO each.- And think of
the “ Government stroke ” stricken at their erec-
tion. Thirty shillings has bought that classic six
tint obstructed Granny’s neighbourhood, near
Hunter-street. Why, the timber in them was
worth more, to say nothing of their scroll work,
their acanthus leaves and censers and urns, and
above all that “loyalty” and “ iptense devotion
to that kingship” mixed thick with the canvas
plaster. The fate of these obelisks is indeed
suggestive of .the Australian’s attitude towards
his idols. He is sadly given to walloping his Joss.
The American eagle has Tooed considerably of
late in Sydney. Courtesies, in the form of
dinner, teas, at homes, and dances have brought
Columbia’s exiles on board the. Brooklyn, and
fetched the Brooklyn officers ashore. When
Admiral Remey was “At Home” last week the
Bird, although very wet, fairly screamed with
pride of the crowd (that turned up in spite
of the rain), and of the Brooklyn’s decora-
tions and music and choice things to
drink and nibble. Cards said dancing, but only
three or four couples attempted a waltz. Tall,
very tall and slender, Mrs Orlando Baker, who
lives a bit out of the world at Petersham, but will
receive by-and-bye at the Consulate, was present,
chief amongst the exiles. One of Mrs. Baker’s
daughters fills the chair of Greek at lowa Uni-
versity. When I tell you that this daughter is
only a couple of years younger-than Mrs. Baker
you will probably conclude that she should have a
step before her relationship. Miss Baker suc-
ceeded her father in the dead-language chair.
Seems as if Greek chairs were heirlooms in some
families.
Mrs. G. H. Reid, who knows how to wear her
clothes and handle her parasol and talk to gold-
laced men, was present with the fragile gentleman
who is supposed to spend his time keeping a dog
out of the wet, but who seems to make time to
frivol. He was protesting against a newspaper
item that stated he had taken a house called “ The
Wattles,” at St. Kilda. He never did—and won’t.
A wounded propeller was the first cause of the
“ At-Home ” on board the Armand Behie last
Monday. As the vessel had to stay longer than
usual in port, M. Brasier de Thuy, agent of the
Messageries Maritimes Co., and Commander Le
Coispellier asked 500 persons to come and rejoice
with them just for rejoicing’s sake. Once you
mounted the gangway you saw little of the ship,
it was so disguised with flowers and tricolor
ribbon and tiny electric lamps, and the tables
were covered with quaint French cookies. A lot
of visitors were badly “had” over what they
thought were huge bowls of strawberries, and said
they’d take strawberries and cream. Not until
they had bitten a berry did they know it was a
sugar-almond. The same people that you see at
every “ At Home ” were present, and they mostly
wore the same clothes.
.Went to a Sydney Quaker wedding the other
day, when I noticed two or three important
differences between the Quaker wedding ceremony
and that of other denominations. To begin with,
there is practically no wedding ceremony at all!
The people merely sit round and wait till the
spirit moves the bridegroom to offer himself to
the bride, and what would happen if the spirit
didn’t move that personage is a mystery. Then
the people wait some more till the bride likewise
, experiences divine prompting in the direction of
offering herself unto the bridegroom. The couple
do all the marrying themselves without the aid of
a middle man, and the bride doesn’t grovel in the
slightest, the obey doctrine being completely left
out. The Quaker bride and bridegroom take each
other on equal terms, the gist of their remarks on
the subject being that they will do their level best,
which seems a sensible way of arranging things
on the whole.
It is not often that a clergyman forbids people
to come to his own church. Something like that
occurred the other day when a minister issued
blue and white tickets to his congregation per-
mitting no one unticketed to witness the ceremony
of his marriage. Blue signified that you came to
the after feed, but the white pasteboard holders
merely saw the ceremony and then departed to
discuss it over their own tea and toast in their own
homes.
Lieut. Defrayer and his Scarf are a much-
photoed pair in this town. Sometimes the scarf
is pictured without the Lieut. Never the Lieut,
without the Scarf. It is a homely brown thing—
such as any old lady might knit—but it has the
merit of being entirely the late Queen’s work.
Some of her granddaughters and ladies-in-waiting,
the Duchess of York told Mr. Dufrayer, offered
to help her in the making of the scarves—but her
Majesty declined aid as she wished every stitch to
be her very own. By the way, Lieut. Dufrayer’s
mother (formerly a Miss McPherson) was the
lady who, when Morgan, the bushranger, stuck-
up Peechelba Station, Murray River (his last
crime, for he was shot there) was compelled to
play the piano half the night to amuse the robber,
who sat drowsing with his feet on the table and
two pistols at his hand.
At her farewell musical “At Home ” at the
Australia (it was a very diamond-dusted evening),
Mrs. Mackenzie Fairfax wore a gown of white
satin covered with silver sequins. Otherwise the
dress was quite plain. Her hair was squeezed
into a little coil at the nape of her neck, and just
above the forehead, fiat on the parting of the hair,
was a tiny diamond swallow. Women with small,
pretty heads often make their faces suffer in their
desire to keep the contour of the head un-
broken. She sang beautiful songs in far finer
voice than we heard her in at the Ophir Band
charity concert. Mrs. Van der Veer Green,
looking splendidly colossal—reminiscent of Bar-
tholdi’s statue at the entrance of N.Y. harbor
—also sang, and so set people worrying her to
appear in public again. Her “ Love, the Pedlar,”
was a gem of considerable sparkle and first water.
Miss M’Quade, all in white samite—or was it
satin ? —with black roses on her left shoulder,
looked and played well. There was a wonderful
dress—bright birds and flowers and trails of
leaves had been cut from silk cretonne and ap-
pliqued on to a very frail foundation, and the
train was like unto a pink cloud. The wearer
looked as if she’d got bushed in a section of Ceylon
forest. Mrs. B. R. Wise, Mrs. Pilcher, and Mrs.
Burdekin were among those present, becomingly
gowned.
Waitara Foundling Home had a field day last
week to present its annual report and give after-
noon tea. The Rev. Mother Alovsius came up
from the Mother House and Cardinal Moran from
town. Dr. Angel Money and Dr. Kelly left
their patients to have a look at the 94 babies, and
make little sympathetic speeches. And a fresh
number of ladies joined the committee. You see
the Home is dependent on private charity. The
visitors flocked for choice over to the cottage in
the orange orchard where the youngest babies are
rocked. Dozens of tiny creatures, from a week to
a few months old, lay in their swinging cots, and
sucked meditative thumbs or demanded nourish-
ment with strong wails. In another building
larger babies gathered around fires, for it was very
cold at Waitara, and in yet another, mature
creatures of four or five were at kindergarten.
Sixteen mothers are in the Home at present with
their very young offspring. And if you want to
crown a day with a good deed you will send the
Home a subscription.
An attempt to revive the shilling “ Pops ” was
made last Saturday at the Town Hall by the
Percy Bates Concert Co. A crowded house
greeted the programme, so the attempt ought to
become chronic. Mrs. Ward, wife of the nimble
Millionaire in “ Florodora,” was the chief attrac-
tion. She has a voice that suggests Trebelli-
Dolores, fresh and easy, and has evidently the
makings of a fine singer. Rivers Allpress and
tenor Bates were the next “draws.” Miss Kate
Hancock sang her two songs sweetly, and two
small children did a song and coon dance.

marriage is not to come off.
This relates to a matter of business.
