linery (real Paris models), coats and skirts, dress-
ing-gowns, little Prince Edward of York suits,
fancy haberdashery, ribbons and laces are, it
is officially announced, severely marked
down. Also, lot of ladies’ tweeds may be had
very cheaply; and you can buy a good dinner set
of 26 pieces for 14/6. And, if you want any of the
standard authors Dumas, Thackeray, Scott,
Dickens—you may have them for 4|d. each.
Fancy “The Newcomes,” in a nice, stiff, red-
brown cover, for four and a half tram fares !
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“At the opening of Parliament the most brand-
new looking male was ‘Billy’ Williams, the
masher member for St. Kilda, who lately married
the daughter of Mr. Pat. M‘Caughan. Said lady
was one of the smartest of feminines in grey, with
a bold splash of cerise in the doorway of her toque.
“A strong smell of vamish-cum-mackintosh
carried home the first impression of the Opening
Ceremony of Vic. State Parliament. After that
came‘Lieut.’ Guy Madden’s legs under curtain-
kilts. Stand the said Guy on a bag of Blank’s
Oatmeal and label it ‘ That’s wot 1 grow’d on,’
and you have set him in his native element and
given the meal a suitable advt. Nothing more
waxwork-like than Those Legs has ever been seen
in tartan. A third impression left by the wintry
function was the increased proportions of a gay
globe-trotter, who might have been, were it not
for a pair of sparkling eyes and a set of expressive
teeth (all her own), mistaken for Benedetto in
smart attire.
“ A gloomy mothers’-meeting tone prevailed at
the Opening Ceremony in the well-lighted Council
chamber. The mourning tones of the women’s
dress, the occasional whiff of violets, the hushed
whispers, suggested a whiskyless wake. Then the
mournful air of the Hon. Holder helped the idea.
The sedateness of Richard Baker did not
upset it. And there were the clerics—Bishop
Goe and Dr. Bevan—sandwiched in among the
black gowns; and George Jenkins, C.M.G., was
there, too, in a funereal suit and without a smile—
so mirthless that he didn’t even stroke his beard.
But Usher M‘Coil’s announcement in loud,
anxious, punctuated periods, that the Governor
was approaching the building, introduced a comic
element and dispelled the funereal illusion.
“One missed the late absented Zeal’s timid
hand-grip and Corypheus Jenkins’sly compliment
as the introductory to the tea-fight. Instead,
Speaker and Mrs. Mason offered greetings and
exchanged compliments. Mrs. Mason wore a
smart toilette of violet that had seen ducal ser-
vice. Senator Neihl allowed Mrs. Mason to in-
troduce him to a host of girls, and set him talking
to them all at once. Melb. girls are deeply im-
pressed by the brigandish air of the terrible devil
of a Colonel. They’ve never seen anything like it
before, nor met such an indefatigable talker.
They all stand round and stare in amazement at
the Champion Tireless Talker of the World.
“Lady Hopetoun in habit, top hat and high
boots looks bettor than in anything else. Cer-
tainly not more cheerful, for though she rode at
the heels of the hounds all Friday afternoon, when
the Findon Harriers hunted two lively foxes to
earth, she did so in sober earnest, and Harry
Brush, the much-damaged, got the nearest acci-
dent to a smile as lie helped her from her fine grey
steed, long after all the other habit-dashers were
dismounted and clearing the scone-plates. Return-
ing from the hunt, the cold Excellency read a
scarlet-covered novel in the State carriage, while
her lively partner recounted stories of the hunt
and chase (presumably) to his escorts. The
Countess wasn’t taking anything of the ladies on
board the train. She preferred to yawn over her
book.
“Dr. Maudsley, the Collins-st. medico of the
Soft Voice, shadowed the sprightly widow of
‘Cliveden’ in the field, while S’Rupert’s consort,
radiant in her hunting red costume and rustic hat,
drove the rest of the Dowager’s family in a pony-
cart. In one of the fours-in-hand rose up the long
figure of Phillip Fysh, while his diminutive wife
snuggled away in a cart with a party of seal-
coated matrons. The belle of the day—Miss
Muriel Williams—was a carelessly-garbed figure
in black. Her uncommonly well-dressed head
carried a jaunty hat of black and gold. She still
adheres to the light veil worn to the end of the
nose—a French fashion.
“The big, bustling, noisy contingent of sports
and swagger somebodies who journeyed up to
Sunbury at the invitation of the Rupert Clarkes
on Friday were welcomed by a scarlet-tailored
hostess, and entertained with a very sparkling
breakfast. Sister Hilda Cumming, blooming in
blue, got away from the table,of much Excellency
and had a jolly time with a Toorak party, having
for neighbor Majah Clarke, the best-looking
fellow on the premises. The dowager Clarke was
one of the doughtiest of Dianas, and almost
looked jaunty in her hard boxer and trim habit.
“ There was nothing like the wild heather or
the wild gaiety at ‘Rupertswood’ this year that
there was at the opening of the last meet, when a
parcel of hilarious girls drove off to a wayside inn,
and, while the billy boiled, sang, in a rallying
chorus, ‘ Why did I leave my little back-room ? ’
This year calmer weather and more correct con-
ditions reigned, and a well-stocked tent provided
the cup that cheers and the scone that racks the
chest after the hunt.
“What a pity Mrs. John Sandes (erstwhile
Miss Claire Berry) didn’t give to the part she
lately on the boards a tenth part of the
confidence she so' agreeably throws into her
appearance off the stage ! At the Hunt, the other
day, no one had the ‘airs’ of the budding stage
star, who carried off a not too fetching grey frock
with the stage dignity of an angry Lady Teazle.
“The Millers Albert and Son—ran the
‘Rupertswood’ shivoo in the absence of the
Baronet, whose arrival in England, by the way,
was cabled home on Thursday. S’Rupert’s trip
this time, many will regret to hear, was taken for
reasons of health, heart-trouble being the disturb-
ance.
“Everybody inwardly and a few outwardly
groaned when George Cruickshank, of the House
of Reps, and the ‘Cliveden’ fold, arose at the
Hunt breakfast, to drone out a return of thanks
on behalf of the hostess. He began in the true
parliamentary-bore fashion, but the shuffling of
feet moved him not to hurry on—and he only
pulled up after a ten minutes’ tongue-drag.
“ The other day the students of the University
Conservatorium rallied together to give their new
Prof, (the quiet Scot) and Mrs. Peterson a wel-
come to their new sphere. But the Prof, came
alone and thanked them for their good intentions,
and heard their music, and carried home a shower-
posy to his guid wife (who was too ill to attend)
from the regretful students. They appear to have
much respect for the staid Professor, and their
parents expect them to do great work on the
strength of it. Is much more progress made by
the students with ‘ temperament ’ who look to
catch the sparks scattered at the anvil of Orpheus
Hall? Some people begin to wonder whether the
creation of ‘ temperament ’ is an unmixed bless-
ing. For one brilliant pupil you get ninety-and-
nine prigs.
“I like ‘Ernani,’ it’s so laughable. At least
nowadays, when suitors are scarce and shy, it
amuses one to see stout Signora De Benedetto
wooed simultaneously and stormily by three
gallants, one poor and handsome, another old and
rich, the third Royal and ugly. Everything, you
see, beauty, wealth, and rank, piled at the plump
feet of Elvira, who, in trained pink-satin, looks
much more attractive than as Ethiopian Aida,
but who is-do you remember that GUbertian
description ?—‘ not a girl of a thousand, but a
girl of forty-nine.’ The footwear of Elvira s three
admirers, as they bark and bite vocally in the
poor girl’s pi'ivate apartments, is a study m con-
trasts. Bandit Ernani (Larizza) sports bold,
defiant jack-boots. Don Silva (Travaglim), a
nice Auld-Robin-Grey ancient (the flower or trie
suitor flock), patters round in tame-cat black-
velvet shoes, fastened with little straps and gold
buttons. Don Carlos (Cattadori), alias the King
of Spain, affects ankle-boots. They have the mien
of prunellas, but I guess they’re black-velvet, and
they turn over at the top in small pale-blue tabs.
Very chic and extra-special, like the varnish of the
York beard and the build of King Ned’s frock-coats.
Like King Ned and Yorkie, Don Carlos is other-
wise not chic. His shaggy red hair and beard
smack of Gog and Magog. His long, thin legs are
shown up by meagre black hose, while his short
top-trunks are padded out a la Hottentot. Quite
an embarrassing back-view, and such a contrast
with the Royal flirt’s calf-contour.
“Life will be more expensive than ever, now
that Royalty has reintroduced the system of ‘ gifts
for good boys.’ The Duke gave our Lieut. -
Governor a silver salver. The recipient promptly
took a wrinkle, and presented the leader of his
escort during the celebrations with an expensive
bauble. Now, chairmen of various committees,
having worked a local Dives into Federal power,
each receives gold medal from theirman. Nothing
in the nature of bribery, mind you. The echoes of
the past battle had almost died away, when the
grateful legislator unexpectedly started to weep
thankfully on the waistcoats of his supporters-in-
chief. Next time they looked at their watch-
chains, they espied a golden token of remembrance
dangling thereon.
“ The Victorian Dog Show is to be held at the
Exhibition Building, when the State Parliament
also sits. The walls of the building are so tiiin
that the slightest outside sound is heard within.
When Madden, C.J. was reading the opening
speech he could scarcely be heard above the sound
of the band outside. How nice it will be when
some poor old wheezy-voiced M.L.C. is trying to
talk above the sound of 500 pups all barking at
once, not to speak of the crowing of cocks, the
quacking of ducks, and the gobble-gobble of prize
turkeys! ‘Mr. President’ —bow! wow! —‘I
maintain ’ —quack-quack—‘that this ’Ouse’ —cock-
a-doodle-doo —‘ is the ’—bow-wow-wow-wow
‘saviour of the country ’—quack—wow—cock-a—
wow-wow—doodle—quack—doo.’ That is the sort
of thing that will go on. Not that it matters.
Any dog could bark more sense than is talked by
the average Vic. Leg. Councillor.
“R.C. circles are interested in the coming mar-
riage of Dr. Eustace Keogh, of South Yarra, and
Miss Winifred Rowe, of the same district. Both
families are gilded pillars of Holy Church.
“Brighton is a suburb that leans towards
Toorakian snobbery, and tries'all it can to be ex-
clusive. The latest effort in this direction is
turned towards making an approaching Bread-
and-Scrape dance as ‘nice’ as possible, so, though
the tickets are only half-a-crown, a ‘selection’ (?)
committee has been formed. Only a two-and-six-
penny selection committee.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Another Letter from a Nurse. "
8 Carlton-street,
Prahran, Yic.,
12th March, 1901.
Dear Sirs,
I am pleased to report the benefit
I derived from the use of Warner’s Safe Cure.
After many years of pain and suffering from
kidney and liver complaint, I, who was accustomed
to minister to others, was brought to a bed of sick-
ness myself. The doctors could give me no hope
of relief or recovery, and said that I had but a
short time to live. I tried various advertised
remedies, but all failed to give me relief until I
tried Warner’s Safe Cure. From the first bottle I
began to improve in health. The pains gradually
left me. I continued to take the medicine for
some time, until I was perfectly cured, and sound
in body and mind. I may state that the doctors
were more than surprised to see me up and about
again.
I have heard of many other cases during my
professional career, which were in like manner
cured by the use of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Yours obediently
EMMA DARYELL,
Ladies’ Nurse.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd.
Warner’s Safe Cure is the result of scientific re-
search. It cures all diseases of the kidneys and
liver. As a consequence, all disorders arising
from a deranged condition of these vital organa
are overcome. Indigestion, rheumatism, anaemia,
blood disorders, biliousness, gout, gravel, general
debility, neuralgia and Bright’s disease, are
amongst the many complaints due to defective
action of the kidneys and liver.*

Curious facts : The first man hanged in
Mudgee (N.S.W.) gaol was named Rope ; and the
man who, on that occasion, provided the timber
for the gallows, and helped to erect same, was
himself the next man hanged in that gaol.
The health of big Jock M‘Kenzie, of
Maoriland, just K.C.M.G.’d, still flatters his
backers. The sturdy Scotchman fights manfully,
and after many weary months’ close confinement
to his home is now able to walk in his garden.
J. O. N., SENATOR.
He’s tall and shallow, loose of jaw,
In language ever fiercely harsh,
And yet whilst speaking makes his points,
You’ll note them —in his large moustache !
George Leake, Westralia’s new Premier,
is one of the most popular men in that State,
though a Groper by birth and belonging to one
of the Six Families. Born at Perth in 1856,
Leake was educated in that town and at
St.. Peter’s College, Adelaide. He was ad-
mitted to the W. A. Bar in ’63, and was made
acting-Crown-Solicitor the following year.
At the advent of Responsible Government,
in 1890, he was returned for Roebourne,
but declined a portfolio in the Forrest
Ministry, then just starting on its
decade of power. In ’94, he resigned
the Crown-Solicitorship, was returned
for Albany and elected leader of the
Opposition. Leake has been a con-
sistent Democrat, though he has the
reputation of being somewhat
lazy. He has always been a great
thorn in the side of Bigjohn,
whom he nightly goaded to fury
from the front Opposition
bench. Not that there was
anything to get furious about,
but Oom John could not brook
opposition. Leake, in thecir-
cumstances, regarded For-
rest as legitimate sport, and
a few well-timed interjec-
tions would set the bull-
voiced autocrat snorting
violently. Leake fought
manfully for Federation
and the rights of the people,
and, if a referendum were
taken to-day, his choice as
Premier would be confirm-
ed by a two-to-one major-
ity. Yet the seats were so
ounningly and shamefully
distributed by the mail who
sent a contingent to
S’Africa to insist on the full
franchise being granted to
the Uitlanders, that it is
just possible Leake will find
himself in a minority. Indeed, it irf
said that he can only rely on 22 votes
in a House of 50.

register trade.

r.S.W. Leg. Assembly (both wear wigs).

Cooper and Bailey’s Circus reads all right, but let
me tell you that Jenkins did not arrive in Aus-
tralia until after Cooper and Bailey’s Circus had
loft. I arrived about the same time, and I know
that Mr. Jenkins had his time fully occupied
looking after the business of a very large publish-
ing house, whose interests were in his hands.—C.
Like Senator Playford, whose seat he
takes in S.A. Assembly, W. Jamieson is a very
practical agriculturist. He was the champion
ploughman the last year in which the S.A. Agri-
cultural Society held a championship ploughmg
match.
Mr. Justice M. H. Stephen, of N.S.W.,
was the first native of Australia admitted to
N.S.W. Bar. He was born in Tasmania in 1828,
and was called to the Bar in 1850. All the men
at the Bar when Mr. Stephen presented himself
were foreign-born.
Two of the oldest ecclesiastics in the Aus-
tralian Anglican Church are Dean Cowper, of
Sydney, who will be 91 on Tuesday, July 2, and
Archdeacon Farr, of Adelaide, who will be 82 on
the previous day. Apropos, the late Dean of Mel-
bourne lived to be 96.
Payne, one of the Melbourne candidates
for the Vic. Leg. Council, is a son of the enor-
mously wealthy Payne, whose estate turned in a
fat probate not so very long ago. He is unheard-
of as a politician, and a particularly painful type
of the Rich Landlord.
David JSyme has dropped his Queen’s
Memorial collection scheme out of the Age with-
out comment. There' was beginning to be felt
need of a footnote to explain who this particular
Queen was, and where she lived and what she did,
and so Syme abandoned hope.
Bryan O’Loghlen, who was once Austra-
lia’s only baronet, completed his 73rd year on
Thursday of this week. He was once quite an
important political personage in Victoria, but
now he is as much a dead letter as either Gavan
Duffy or Townsend M'Dermott.
Jasprizza, lately murdered at Young
(N.S.W.), arrived from Austria nearly 40 years ago.
started a small vegetable garden, added to it a
vineyard, grew wealthy, purchased property in
many directions, and after a hard-working life of
70 years was shot dead in his bed by an unknown
assassin. .

to present addresses, will not forget two things—
(l) The fussy little soldier with the bald head who
kept repeating “ Silence ! silence ! You must not

The thick German accent of George when lie got

A. E. Morgans is regarded in some quar-
ters as the coming Premier of Westralia.
He admitted to a reporter the other day that
he intended remaining in the State for a
long period, and would be found taking
a deep interest in politics in the future,
but thought that the new Ministry
should be. given a fair trial before it is
thrown out. Morgans is about the
only boomster remaining in Wes-
tralia whose reputation has not
been scratched by his own wild
cats. Believed that he is
quietly waiting to see how
things shape before he decides
to go for the Premiership as
leader of the Opposition, or to
get there by coalition with
the present crowd. He would
be well-advised to hurry up,
for as the bottoms fall out of
some of his mines his bubble
reputation will suffer, and
mines, all the world over have
a way of coming down crash,
especially in Westralia. His
best mine is undoubtedly that
named after himself, Mt. Mor-
gans. It is worthy of his name,
but not of that of the mine which
its name so closely resembles. On
the other hand, the Millionaire,
another of his flotations in the same
district, appears to be by no means
so promising. He has floated other
concerns in Westralia, the outlook of
which is not too dazzling. There was a
time when George Gray was looked upon
as the coming Premier, What chance
would he have now ?
E. H. Coombe, who takes Senator John
Downer’s place in S.A. Assembly, was one of the
official shorthand-writers at the first sittings of
the Federal Convention in Adelaide. Like ex-
Premier Holder, he gravitated into country jour-
nalism midway through life, and thence passes
into politics.
Langdon Bonython, of S.A., spent £760
on his candidature for the House of Representa-
tives, while Labor-member Batchelor got there
just the same with an expenditure of £33. For
the Senate, Chaffey Baker, the Tory, spent £605,
while Labor-member M‘Gregor arrived for a
trifle of £33.
Mr. “Vesey” Browne has bought Dul-
calmah property, near Singleton, N.S.W. The
residence on the estate, which contains 11,250
acres, cost £30,000, and the price paid by Mr.
Browne for the whole lot was £45,000, or at the
rate of £4 per acre. The new owner intends
residing there.
A Federal Minister had a dog, and it had
a habit of biting people. Once, when he was
absent, it rushed out and frightened a passer-by
so that proceedings were taken in the matter.
Then he sent quite a long telegram of instructions
on the subject, and the message was sent “on
service ” arid paid for by Government.
Northern Territory, the biggest electoral
district in the world, is now represented by the
smallest member of S.A. Assembly. Solomon’s
successor is S. J. Mitchell, an almost unknown
Adelaide solicitor. Mitchell is president of the
Telegraph Operators’ Association, and thus had
hundreds of eager canvassers all along the over-
land line.
Philoprogenitive Ireland ! Six brothers,
Faheys, left Ireland in 1840, and settled in N.S.
Wales. The last of them died the other day,
aged 82, and the family computation is that the
six have left behind them a thousand descendants.
James Anthony Froude used to blame productive-
ness of this sort on what he called the philopro-
genitive potato.
In re the jibes levelled at Mayor Ware, of
Adelaide, for kissing the Duke’s hand on being
made a C.M.G., it is maintained by Ware’s
friends that he acted as correctly as was possible
under the circumstances. He is very lame,
through a railway accident of years ago, and, being
unable to kneel like the rest, he acted as he did
by official advice.
King O’Malley, of the hyacinthine locks
and the portentous spectacles, will be 43 years old
on Tuesday next. It is his constant complaint
that his mother would not cross the St. Lawrence
(his native home was just on the Canadian side),
so that he might be born in the United States.
“I can never be President now,” he says with a
wail; “that is why I left that great country.”
The late James M‘Kean put up one of the
long-talking records in Vic. Leg. Assembly. On
quite a minor question—whether Stawell stone
snould be used in the building of Parliament
House—he spoke for nearly five hours. Deceased,
who had nearly 40 years’ experience of the Vic-
torian Bar, always maintained that the greatest
forensic effort made during that time was Daw-
son’s summing-up for petitioner in the Molesworth
divorce-case.
Dear Bulletin.— Before the York flare
dies quite out—what price me for a procession, or.
a K. C.M.G., or to have my picture in The
Bulletin, or, better than all, to get a fat
billet. Listen! I am the eldest son and
eldest child, of the eldest son and eldest
child, of the eldest son and eldest child of
the first white female born in N.S.W. (Rebecca
Small, b. 1788; afterwards “ Granny ” Oakes,
d. 1883). If the whole family came along behind
me, we should make no slouch of a procession in
ourselves. But it’s the billet I’m after. — Leslie
Oakes.
THE REVERSE OF RESERVED.
Forceful Wallace Nelson, five years editor
of People’s Newspapee, at Rockhampton (Q.) t
has left the paper, and talks of going to England.
Wallace’s brains and honesty were appreciated by
many readers, but not by Rockhampton adver-
tisers. It is the business of crawling for advts. —
selling your soul for pottage —that makes Aus-
tralian country journalism the low-down tiling it
often is.
A N.S.W. candidate . lately opened his
campaign in a wayback township. Three minutes’
speaking used up his ideas, and he proceeded to
state that someone had hocussed the water on the
table, and had turned his mind into a dreary
blank; he intended to forward a sample of the
fluid to Sydney for analysis. Whereupon the
chairman rose indignantly, and swallowed two
glasses of the suspected liquid. Then a
voice advised the candidate—“ Send yer brains
down ter Sydney, ole man, and get them aner-
lysed,” and the meeting enthusiastically left the
premises.
“Crow-Eater” : The Commonwealth Par-
liament talks of fixing the compulsory-retirement
age in its soon-to-be-ereated Civil Service at 05.
Now Charles Todd, S.A. Postmaster-General,
will be 75 next month, and he looks quite capable
of carrying out his duties for years to come. Re-
tiring allowances were abolished in S.A. in 1881
and it is not very fair to throw the old man on a
cold world without any consideration, seeing that
if the P. and T. departments had not been taken
over by the Federation he would have remained
undisturbed indefinitely. An Adelaide suburban
postmaster, the other day, voluntarily resigned at
the age of 80.
Alexander Forrest (brother of Westralia’s
ex-Premier), who died of Bright’s disease last week,
aged 50, was a bushman of the old type, and an
explorer of the days when men trusted more to
their knowledge of bushcraft to pull them through
than they did to the stomach of a camel. The
successful trip of the Forrests through central
Australia from west to east, after so many
attempts to negotiate the same country from east
to west had failed, was as much due to Alexander
as to John, who got all the kudos. The younger
Forrest did most of the scouting ahead with either
one blackboy or single-handed, and many a time
pulled the party out of a tight place. On the ’79
trip, when he was in charge—from the De Grey
River to the overland line—he somewhat marred
proceedings by an ill-considered attempt to climb
over the steepest part of the King Leopold Range,
and thereby knocked up his horses for no sufficient
reason ; otherwise he showed good judgment and
discretion, and always volunteered for the lion’s
share of the work. Like the late Ernest Giles,
Alex. Forrest was rather given to bestowing
names of titled people on his discoveries, but, at
any rate, Leopold of Belgium has a better claim
to be geographically considered than had Prince
Amadeus. But Australian explorers have ever
been weak in nomenclature. They always hover
between Sandy Creek and Victoria.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., exact date and' exact
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
The Hindoo in a new role. At a sports-
meeting at Garvoc (Vic.), on the York birthday, a
Hindoo entered for the buckjumping competi-
tion, and, by clinging to the saddle like a
monkey, managed to sit out the first round.
Afterwards a £1 collection induced him
to re-mount; but this time the horse
was fairly on the job, and sent the
darkey flying, his many-folded turban
going first, while his long hair
streamed out in a dozen different
directions. When liam Chutnee
recovered his breath he explained
excitedly in six languages at
once, with a copious supply of
the international “ blanky,” that
he’d have stuck till doomsday had
the moke not adopted the unfair
dodge of bucking sideways.
New-cliuni visitors at any
of the Australian city railway
stations are struck by the fact that,
even in the coldest weather, very few
men walk up and down the plat-
form whilst waiting for their
train. But it isn’t only on rail-
way platforms that the average
Australian stands still whereas
the fidgetty Britisher is kept
moving by force of habit. The
same difference is seen in their
manner of waiting for a girl at a
street corner or killing time on a pier. The
one man walks as though he were condemned
to “sentry go,” and the other seems bent on
giving himself as little trouble as possible,
and takes leaning exercise.
“Yes,” said the imaginative Maorilander,
as the coach rolled through the Waiinaramaropotu
Valley, “ last season I came through here, and
the whole of that flat was covered. The Maoris
were flooded out of their whares, and you could
see the pigs swimming about and eating the pota-
toes floating on the water.” There was a short
silence. “Wall, stranger,” said the Yankee
tourist, “don’t mind giving you half-a-dollar for
every potato .you bring me that’ll float in fresh
water.” Then there was a long silence.
“ Boozuru ” waa lying alongside the road,
with liia hand closed tightly over the bottom of his
pocket. Some bank-notes protruded from the top,
and when a deadbeat came along presently, he gave
a gentle, but ineffectual, pull at them. He passed
on a few paces, and returned, giving the notes
another pull en passant. Then he passed to-and-
fro several times, making a more determined
effort each time, but the notes wouldn’t shift. At
last tug, “ 800/.um ” began to rouse, and, to
make things took right, the deadbeat shook him
and cried, “ Hey ! wake up ! Someone will be
goin’ through you for that money directly. It’s
nearly out of yer pocket now.” “Not much,”
said “ Boozum,” “I had a pretty tight grip*of it
all the time you were pullin’ at it.”

A curious case was recently before Narra-
bri (N.S.W.) Land Court. A selector had ren-
dered his compliance with the provisions of the
Crown Lands Acts difficult by marrying the
owner of the adjoining selection; and the ques-
tion was whether the parties had each, to fulfil the
statutory conditions by residing separately on his
and her selection. It was forcibly argued that
the policy of the law was not only to stimulate
land-settlement, but also to encourage morality ;
and that it was neither intended nor desirable
that married couples should be separated by a six-
wire The court, however, held that the
moral consideration was not weighty enough to
override plain statute law, and that therefore the
conditions of residence had not been performed.
Waiver of forfeiture, however, was recommended,
provided the husband performed his wife’s con-
ditions of residence in addition to carrying out
the improvements on his own land.

“ J. 8.” : Reflection induced by “ Deeby ”
(8., 6/4/’01): Cruelty to animals—dumb or other-
wise—is largely governed by economics. In Aus-
tralia animals are cheap and men dear. In Eng-
land, where an animal is worth three times and a
man one-half their respective Australian values,
the animal has often the better billet. In time
conditions may more closely approximate here,
but till then the dumb animal will continue to
suffer. Meanwhile, there are quite a lot of men
in this country who deserve shooting.

“ W.J.” : The same piteous brute men-
tioned by “ Aga” in B. 30/3/T is to be seen with-
out inconvenience above-ground, and on almost
any low-down Wiininera (Vic.) farm. Where
writer was working a few winters ago, no less
than three plough-horses were regularly slung-up
every night, and next day belted from end to end
of the cultivation. If you forgot the sling your
horse would be down in the morning for certain.
And if you didn’t hoist him bodily to his feet he
would stay there. Also, have seen good honest
horses so blistered and raw under the collar that
they positively refused, when hitched-on at. 7 a. m.,
to stagger two yards. But the inspector, like the
early-closing God send, doesn't get to the back-
blocks.

“Vril De-
gilbo Station
(Burnett district,
Q.) has its own
little natural curi-
osity, facetiously
termed the Lady’s
Mile. The “Mile”
is a belt of country
a mile or there-
abouts wide, and
running approximately
east and west for a dis-
tance of some few miles.
The formation, which
begins abruptly and
ends ditto, consists of
loose spherical volcanic
stones average size
about that of a football.
You kind of walk on
ball-bearings when cross-
ing, and are supplied
directly by nature with a
hall-marked incentive for cursing.
“ Bungarra ” : Entered aN. YV. Westralian
township, a few weeks ago, and met the local
policeman pacing up and down outside his camp,
as if something was on his mind. Asked him what
had happened. He said, “ It’s like this : I chased
and caught a nigger the other day. He was wanted
for camp-stekling ; and I put him securely on the
log, and turned in, happy. When I woke up in
the morning, I found that the chains, cuffs, and
nigger were missing. Two. days after, the chains
and cuffs were returned to my camp in broad day-
light, with a note saying, “ I thought your chains
were a bit rusty, so I took the liberty of cleaning
them for you. Will send in my bill on a spear-
head the first time I meet you away from the
station.” This nigger was trained at a mission,
and is not caught yet.

“H.S.” on certain aboriginal
burial customs
On the Lower Murray, from Huston
to the Wellington crossing (embracing
the country where the baked gypsum
“ widows’ caps ” were most generally
used) the dead were prepared for inter-
ment by being bound up with bark-cord
in their opossum-cloaks. The grave
was made 4 to sft. deep, and always
lying cast and west, and upon a sheet
of bark in the bottom was placed the
corpse. Frequently the dead man's
weapons were buried with him. Then
the body was fenced-in, by cross-sticks
pressed into the earth on each side of
the grave ; longer sticks being after-
wards placed lengthwise, and across
the others. Upon this framework was
set a thick layer of long grass; so that
none of the earth would come in direct
contact with the remains. Then the
earth was tilled in, the mourners or re-
latives slashing themselves with sharp
shells, and casting them down upon
the newly-made mound. Occasionally
—like the Edwards River natives—they
erected over the grave a bark hut, oval
in shape, and with a small aperture
facing the east. Often a rude brush
fence was placed round the hut. the
enclosed ground being cleared as bare
as a clay-pan. This enclosed space was
regularly tended and swept- sometimes
even for years ; but I never could dis-
cover that there was any fixed duration
for this practice.
The YY est (j. aborigines are
dying off at a terrific rate. Drink
and disease were for many years
the great decimators of the Q.
blacks, but recently opium hits
been the especial scourge of the
poor niggers. Station managers
m search of cheap labor have been,
to some extent, responsible, but the
backblox Chinese are the principal
sources of the mischief. Seldom
does Warrigal .touch genuine
opium. He usually has to content
himself with opium charcoal.
During recent Q. Federal elections a
“ backbloeks ” man walked 45 miles to Barcaldine
to record his vote—only to find that his name was
not on the roll!
* * * *
“Spin” Governor Broome’s visit to
New Norcia mission statioji. He was not answered
by the darkie in Latin, but in polite and correct
English. New Norcia is about tne most successful
of all Australian aboriginal mission stations.
Many of the black boys serve at Mass, and there
is a good choir. The blacks are well cared for,
and the absence of the usual mission station half-
caste kid is remarkable.

“C.E.H.” : “ Pelorus Jack,” theso-called
whale that is supposed to pilot steamers through
the French Pass, M.L., really Comes along to rub
the barnacles off his back against the vessel’s side.
He is of dirty-grey colour, about 12ft., long and
looks like a cross between shark and porpoise.
My own opinion is that he is the re incarnated
spirit of a canny Scot who once lost a threepenny-
bit overboard and is still looking for it.

you all over the place for a small piece of tobacco,

dently enjoys the morsel immensely. Is this
common ? [Many goats and some sheep like
tobacco.]
* * • •
When three men (drunk) were leav ng a
N.S.W. town on horseback recently one dropped
his pipe, and, in di mounting to get it, twisted
his ankle and broke his leg below the knee. He
told his mates, but they laughed at him, and then
two men coming along in a cart asked him what
was the matter. He told them, and, putting him
into the cart, they drove to the hospital, dump; d
him inside the gate, and drove off without letting
.the hospital people know. After the man had
been there a couple of hours he was discover* 1
and taken inside, where it was found that th
broken shin-bone was protruding from the flesh.

He was dry as Georgie’s poodle and was quickly growing
worse,
But he didn’t hold the boodle—’twaa a fact that made
him curse—
But he was a man of mettle and was not exactly dumb,
So he walked into M’Pherson’s bar and
Called
For
Rum.
So they handed him the bottle and he made that rum
look queer,
Swigged it off and asked politely : “ Is Jim Smith a-staying
here ?
A peculiar sort of feller but a decent, Jimmy is,
And he’s got a gammy left leg and he
Walks
Like
This.”
Then he limped (as his friend Smith did) to the door and
did a git,
And M’Pherson, realising that he hadn't paid his bit,
Cursed until the glasses rattled and the beer-machine got
jammed,
Then he wound up with the homely phrase of:
“ Well,
I’m
Damned 1 "
Now, the chap who “had” M’Pherson thought the joke
too good to lose,
So he told a friend of his’n, who was dying for a booze,
How he’d scored his cheap refreshment—though he didn’t
tell him where —
And that friend he struck M’Pherson’s and he
Called
For
Beer.
And they drew a quart of swanky—it was in a pewter
pot—
And he downed it with a single gulp, and said : “ It’s
mighty hot !
Is Tommy Brown a-stayin’ ’ere ? ’E’s whiskers ou ’is phiz,
And ’e’s kind er wooden-leggckl and he
Walks
Like
This.”
Then M’Pherson wax6d wrathy, there was murder in his
gloat
As he leapt him o’er the counter, seized that joker by the

And he shouted : “ Brown’s not staying here, I don’t
know who he is,
But M’Pherson’s pretty handy, and he

“Jimmy”: Be horse-sense (“Colt,”
9/3/’01), I know a wheeler in a N.S.W. coabmine
who owes his life to the gumption of an old pit-
horse. Three full skips, being insufficiently
spragged, broke away and collided with the man s
set, jamming him between two buffers, whereupon
the horse promptly braced himself against the
skips and held them. Ten minutes after, two
men passing that way found the wheeler uncon-
scious, and the horse still braced against the skips
holding on like grim death. As the road where
the accident happened was very steep, a moment s
delay on the horse’s part would have brought the
string of skips over the wheeler’s body ; as it was,
he escaped with a broken leg.
* » • *
At Bellinger (N.S.W.), recently, a farrier
successfully shod a bullock all round. The ox
kicked viciously while its hind feet were being
treated, but brute force went down before the
smith’s intelligence.
• * * •
In the course of a recent stealing case at
Darlinghurst (Sydney) Quarter Sessions, evidence,
though not material to the issue, was given as
follows:
Male witness :“ I live with my brother’s wife. I have
children by her. We all live close together. I often
meet my brother at night after tea, and jwe and
smoke together. There is no animosity between us.
Another male witness : “lam living with my sister-in-
law. We agree well.” , T
A female witness : “lam living with a man to whom l
am not married. He is willing to marry, but my father
objects because I am not of age, and he will not give his
consent.” , . .
Another female witness in same case explained that
“ when I saw the police I was on my way to meet a
married man by appointment.”
Another male witness had been charged with attempt-
ing to rape his own illegitimate child, and another had
served three years for horse-stealing. ” .
“ And,” gasped the Judge, “ we send missionaries to
China." . . ,
“ A Woman ” : Be camels and Afghans in
N.Q.:
Camels in N.Q. have come to stay—and Afghans too, if
the Commonwealth Parliament doesn’t pass the Bill to
exclude aliens. The poverty-stricken carriers and packers
that are left after all the railway construction were
making a mere living wage. The Afghan has now come
to take the very bread out of men who were the bold
pioneers of the country; who in face of incredible
hardships opened up tracks to the gold and tin rushes.
In those days a white man had some heart to work.
No aliens then ; but as soon as the country is opened the
Government allows these colored curses to invade the
country. The Afghans are now building stores and hotels
.at Mt. Garnet, so that our member for Woothakata, who
is supposed to be among the main causes of the irruption,
together with Mr. John Moffat, Copper King of the north,
will have more to compete with than the carriage of ore
to the terminus. Our local member ought not to be so
greedy considering he was working in a road-party before
he got his stroke of luck in Herberton. My husband,
who is bordering on his 60th year, has been carrying for
the last 30 years and has eight children to work for; the
camels’ coming has utterly ruined us. P.S.—There are 80
camels at Mt. Garnet and 14 died coming over. Twenty
Afghans passed through Mareeba to Mt. Garnet about
three weeks ago. People were wondering if they wanted
them all for driving camels ; but they want them to
build hotels and stores. [The camel is wanted ; the
Afghan isn’t. White men can manage camels just as well
as black. —Ed.]

“ J.s.” : A friend of mine who has a jib-
bing horse told me he was going to try the hoof-
tapping plan. “You see,” he said, a horses
brain can only contain one idea at a time, there-
fore if you tap his shoe, it takes, his mind away
from the jibbing idea, and he’ll go like blazes.”-A
few davs later I met the experimenter, with his
head bandaged and his features badly damaged.
“ Hullo ! ” said I, “ didn’t it take that idea out of
his head ? ” “ Oh, yes,” said he, “ but he got hold
of another idea—to knock my skull m.” If five
stitches counted for anything, the neddy had
carried out his “ idea ” remarkably well, too.
“ Dave ” : Mot a traveller the other day
bare-foot—his boots round his neck. “Don’t
know,” he said, “but th’ dashed country sruined ;
seems ter be no dashed money in er. Worked er
a cove back there fer th’ lars’ three munee fer three
bob a week, an’ when I give notice he sez :
» Well, I can’t pay y’ in coin Bill, but if yer likes
y’ ken take it out in water-melons ! 1 here were
nothin’ else fer it so I begun : but how many
melons could a cove eat! ”
Printed herewith is a picture of a portion
of a woolly-butt (E. longifolia) long known in
Oberon district, N.S.W., as the Handcuff-Tree.
The handcuffs seen protruding from the
wood are said to have been placed on a twig
of the tree when it was a sapling (about 70 years
ago) by one Paddy , who died in aforesaid
district five years ago at the great age of 104.
Paddy was a convict, who, after obtaining a
ticket-of-leave, again got into trouble while work-
ing on a Hawkesbury River farm. He absconded,
but was soon afterwards captured by two troopers,
who camped with him for the night. Next morn-
ing, one of the troopers having gone after the
horses, which had strayed some distance, Paddy
managed to get hold of a shovel, blinded his re-
maining guardian with a shovelful of hot ashes
from the fire, knocked the man senseless with the
implement, and made off. How he got the
“cuffs” off is not known, but it was on this occa-
sion that he concealed them in the sapling. The
section of the limb is now temporarily in the pos-
session of Mr. C. Foulkes, of Watson s Ray,
Sydney, having been recently cut-out by him, by
permission of the owner of the run on which the
tree stands.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pea,rls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is h, sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited. -

A Grocer’s Complaint.
Mr. F. H. Thomas, who carried on a grocery
store in Palace-street, Petersham, for several
years, has been long enough in the business to
know what he is talking about. Upon hearing
that he desired to ventilate a certain grievance,
a reporter called upon him, with the following
“We believe you have been complaining of
some hardship in the trade,” said the pressmen.
“Yes,” said Mr. Thomas, “I have found the
work rather heavy, for in these days of ‘ cut’
prices, great economy is necessary; this is
generally so when one is looking after their own
business. What I chiefly complained of was that
after serving behind the counter most of the day,
T would have to leave the shop and go out
delivering with my cart, no matter what the
weather was like. The sudden change from a
warm shop to the driving rain was neither pleasant
nor healthy. Lung, troubles resulted, and 1. began
to suffer from shortness of breath, particularly at
night. I became weak and bloodless, and unnatur-
ally despondent. Sometimes T woke at night
gasping for breath, and my ill-health and decline
was a subject of remark by my friends. Despite
the use of different medicines, I continued to get
worse, so I consulted a leading Sydney doctor. I
was horrified to learn that I was suffering from
asthma and consumption. For twelve months I
was under medical treatment, but received little
benefit.”
“No doubt you took further action,” suggested
the reporter.
“Yes,” replied Mr. Thomas, “ a relative who
had been cured by Dr. Williams’ pink pills for
pale people recommended me to try them. After
some hesitation I did so. Two boxes enriched my
blood and strengthened my lungs, and I was so
pleased with the medicine that I continued taking
it until cured. 1 can honestly say that Dr.
Williams’ pink pills freed me from every symptom
of asthma and consumption when the best medical
treatment seemed powerless.”
Since the above interview, Mr. Thomas has dis-
posed of his Petersham business, and is now
engaged in the storekeeping trade in Quean bey an.
Weak, bloodless, nerveless people are especially
liable to be attacked by winter ailments, such as
asthma, bronchitis, consumption, rheumatism,
neuralgia, sciatica, coughs and colds, etc. Dr.
Williams’ pink pills make weak people strong, and
thus enable them to resist or throw off the above
diseases. They also cure St. Vitus’ dance, rickets,
skin disorders, paralysis, locomotor ataxia, etc.
Sold ,by chemists and storekeepers, and by the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place,
Sydney, two and nine per box, six boxes fifteen
and three, post free. Substitutes are dangerous.

Cabled, on the 15th June, that there had
been 1202 cases of plague at Hongkong, 113 i of
which were fatal. For week ended Bth May . 161
cases, 155 deaths.
London Daily Chronicle says there exists
a packet of Queen Elizabeth’s letters winch has
never been opened. They are supposed to be
love-letters.. To open the packet there must be
the consent of the King, Archbishop of Canter-
bury, and Lord Chancellor.
“Wernher, Beit and Co., co-engineers of
the Jamieson Raid,” writes The Bulletin’s Lon-
don correspondent, “are blank-blanketying their
solicitor, Hawkesley, for taking legal notice of
Markham’s charges against them m tne
House of Commons. The M.P., to show he
wanted no shield of Privilege,Repeated his re-
marks to his constituents, and W.B. and Co. have
been forced to issue a writ —and they 11 wish they
never ‘ wrct.’ Should the case go through m its
entirety, I have the best authority for saying we
shall have the most splendid expose of modern
times, either concerning much-tainted S Africa or
any other country. Markham is only 34, ana a
born fighter.”

Mrs. Lowe, the President of the Woman’s
Suffrage Union, is an enthusiastic believer in Co-
Operative Coupons, and her speech at the public
deputation in favor of the system was very clear
with regard to the benefits which the system had
conferred on the wives of the workers. The
members of the deputation, numbering several
hundred, cheered her remarks so vigorously that
Mr. Peacock had to remind them that it was not a
public meeting. Fully 300 people who attended
the deputation could not get into the room and
had to remain in the corridors.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
For the very first time, Broken Hill miners,
hitherto politically the solidest of workers, are
allowing sectarianism to set them at one another’s
throats.
Nemesis ? J. J. Moloney opposes Dan
O’Connor as candidate for the Phillip Division
m the coming N.S.W. election. “ Appint
Moloney ! ”
The gradual encroachment of the sleepless
■Jingo : The Federal Senate has altered the Con-
stitution phrase “Parliament” in the enacting
words of bills to “King’s Most Excellent
Majesty and Senate and House of Representa-
tives.”
The ami able-Beaueham p only- last month
ceased to draw Governor’s pay. He spent 18
months in Australia (a good deal of the time
travelling outside N.S.W.) but on retiring received
six months’ leave, .“not on full pay,” but half.
He left on Nov. Ist, and his pay thus ceased on
May Ist last.
Senator M‘Gregor badly wants to know
why Britain should be so anxious not to offend the
sensibilities of the foreign Japanese by excluding
them from Australia, when it doesn’t mind offend-
ing about 51 millions of the people of the United
Kingdom by using unpleasant expressions re
their religion in the coronation oath.
Sydney Mayor Graham is standing for
N.S.W. Parliament. An interjector at one of his
meetings cried, “Too many billets altogether!”
Right! The Mayor’s job is surely big enough for
any one man (especially if he happens to be a
practising doctor !). Dr. Graham’s craving for the
other job is mere greed of Importance.
It looks very much as though Warrior
Sleath, Labor-M.L.A., will go underat Wilcannia
in the State election. The opal-gougers at White
Cliffs, who boss this electorate, were willing to
forgive him much, but not the military craze ;and
so they are running a candidate of their own
choosing—a brother of Labor M.L.C. Buzacott.
THE N.S.W. GENERAL ELECTIONS.—
WHAT IT AMOUNTS TO.
SATAN (to St. Progressive and St. Liberal):
“Now you two, keep your eye on the vote,
and don't forget that the biggest liar gets it."
Both on the goldfields and about Perth,
NVestralian Laborites are just now in a state of
deplorable disruption. Each lot is about equally
divided ; and one half is busy calling the other
half “blackleg” and “scab.” The row in the
Perth Trades and Labor Council resulted lately in
both president and vice, throwing up their thank-
less jobs.
The faces of Sargood, Zeal, and Fraser are
wonderful sights to see when radical Senators
-tribe in the old Vic. Leg. Council Chamber, and
breathe brutally Republican sentiments with un-
blushing freedom. All three shrink visibly in
anticipation of the falling roof. By the way, the
daily press seems to have entered into a “con-
soiracy of silence ” re the anti-imperialism of
several Senators.
Though there are scores of needy old
fellows at Broken Hill only 25 applications for
old-age pensions have been lodged there. The
rest of the ancients haven’t been the necessary
quarter-century in the State j for B.H. consists
mainly of people from the nearer centres of South
Australia and Victoria. Wanted, a Federal
pensi on-scli erne.
Queensland Labor-men are battlers, and
110 mistake ! They growled and grumbled the
other day at Gympie because of 3142 on the roll
only 2254—72 per cent.—voted. Yet compare
that with the 35 per cent, of votes cast the week
before in S.A., when Labor-leader Batchelor’s and
other seats had to be filled. But it paid—the
Northerners won and the Southerners lost.
Lord Beauchamp, speaking at his welcoine-
’Ome :
In conclusion, his lordship said that so long as the
people of New South Wales, and Australia generally,
showed their sympathy with the British Empire, and so
long as they realised that not selfishness, or their own
interests, but the good of others was the thing to be
sought after, they need not fear. (Applause.)
In a word, Australia is in business “for the good
of others.”
Gr. H. Reid, the other night, publicly
alluded to N.S.W. Works Minister O’Sullivan
(apropos of the latter’s attack on the daily press)
as “an inkslinger himself for many years on an
obscure journal in whose religious beliefs we do
not all agree.” The Bulletin never thought so
badly of G. H. Reid (aspirant to the Common-
wealth Premiership) as to believe that he could
gratuitously make such a remark as that in this
sectary-cursed country.
“In concluding, he (the Duke of York)
said he would carry away lasting impressions of
Maoriland and its people.”—S.M. Herald.
The Duke has sanctified the name
That once the Herald banned ;
Good loyalists will use the same,
And local poets, wild or tame,
In nimble-footed lines or lame,
Will call their country grand—
“ My own—my Maoriland.”
A recent Bulletin par. reported the
deplorable total absence of anything in the shape
of a trade or labor union at Pt. Pirie, South Aus-
tralia’s second town. Adelaide Laborites, who
are terribly tired, would do nothing; so the
Broken Hill unions sent missionaries over the
border, and already close upon 500 men have been
enrolled in a couple of societies. The fact that
half-and-half Labor-M.P. Roberts (who has been
away lately Boer-ohasing) hasn’t helped at all has
been pasted in several hats.
“Pukaki”: Not only is the Jingo fever
taking longer to die in Maoriland than elsewhere,
but it is strongest among the workers. The local
Fatman, indeed, is getting visibly sick of the
whole thing. In Maoriland Fatman has to bear
his fair share of taxation ; wherefore his enthusi-
asm in the cause of Hempire is already a thing of
the past. The worker, on the other hand, knows
he hasn’t to pay more than his fair and honorable
whack, and being more ready to part than the
Bulgy One, he has still a little whoop left.
The Federal Government made a big mis-
take when it put seven of its members into the
House of Representatives and only two (one of
them an honorary) into the Senate, It evidently
couldn’t get over the idea that the Senate was a
formal perfunctory place something like a State
Upper House. The Senate is as representative of
the people, and almost as powerful and as much
alive, as the House of Reps., and Minister Drake,
who plays a lone hand there most of the time,
grows tired and hoarse trying to be a whole Minis-
try by himself.
It becomes clear despite the cableman’s
efforts to put a good face on things, that the
Chamberlain crowd are growing most uneasy.
London Times finds it necessary to screw up their
resolution by telling them they “should continue
the war in S’Africa until the Boers own that they
are finally defeated. The nation does not want a
patehed-up peace.” Just so ; but every nation
has to put up with what it can get; and, judg-
ing by present appearances, that will be a very
poor return for over £160,000,000 in money, and
perhaps 60,000 men dead or crippled.
One half the Federal Senators are to re-
tire every three years, and the question of which
half retires first is making much needless trouble.
The idea of settling the question by lot— i.e., by
gamble—is not only crude, but it is utterly illogi-
cal on the part of a Parliament which is so much
shocked at Tattersall. In each State the three
Senators who got the smallest vote should go out
first. They are obviously the three that the
people value least, and that the people selected
with the greatest amount of hesitation, and these
are good enough grounds to go upon.
M'Gowen, M.L.A. : “ There are only two
factors in all production—land and labor.” Sheer
foolishness. There is a third factor, and the name
of it is Intelligence. A man without any land,
and born without hands and feet, may, if he has a
great thinking capacity, add more to a nation’s
wealth than 20,000 misdirected hand-toilers will
do in a century. Out in Western N.S.W. there
are regions where much land and 10 years of labor
have produced less than nothing the product
hasn’t made up for the loss of the original capital,
to say nothing of loot lives and lost hopes and lost
years. That was land and labor, without the
intelligence to make good use of them.
One can get a pretty good idea of the
land-law reforms that they want most in the
droughty west (N.S.W.) by noting the points on
which all the party candidates are practically
agreed. For the squatter even the Labor candi-
dates are demanding extension of leases to 42
years at least (it is 28 at present) ; for the selee-
TO BE OR NOT TO BE.
MR. NEEDY: “ How can I live without you?”
MISS PLENTY: “I'm sure I don’t know —unless
you work."
tor, extension of area (present max., 10,240ae.).
A popular “ plank ” is the taking over bodily of
squattages most suitable for selection, on payment
of compensation and appraised value for improve-
ments, and an accompanying provision allowing
the selector to pay rent for these improvements
instead of capital value. The latter generally
oripples him at the start.
PATRONAGE.
The Dook has cabled home to his august
Dad to the effect that “good is likely to result to
the Empire from his (the Book’s) visit.”
The greatest Empire time has ever seen
On Earth’s green lap,
Is better now than it has ever been,
Since he its outer boundaries has seen—
That little chap.
He makes us smile, the little Dook, yet he
Is not alone
In thinking thus, for (whisper) so do we !
But what is for the Empire good may be
Bad for the Throne.
S.M. Herald, commenting on the recent
casualty to Victorians in S’Africa :
Australians will be now more than ever convinced that
the only feasible reward for their patriotic participation
in the war is the emphatic crushing of the existing
opposition.
Which is pretty “ pure,” even for Granny. The
war is to be prosecuted with extra ferocity because
a party of Australian swaddies allowed themselves
to be surprised and shot down. Will Granny
advise the Commonwealth Government to under-
take the conquest of S’Africa at a cost of £1,500,000
per week ? Or does she really think that Cham-
berlain, who won’t consent to the exclusion of the
Jap, will persist in a fruitless war because a few
Australian Jingoes ask him to ?
Senator M‘Gregor’s straight out advocacy
of a State lottery, such as brings in £2,500,000 a
year to the Italian treasury, and materially helps
the revenues of Denmark, Austria, Spain, &c., has
much to recommend it, both commercially and
morally. While Sydney is simply full of betting-
shops which the police are quite unable to find
though the owner’s well-known name is glaring at
them from the signboard, and the illegal tote
flourishes in some places and the legal one in
others, and the betting odds are quoted publicly
in the daily papers, and the country has either to
choose between seeing its money go to Tattersall
at Hobart, or to Tattersall at Noumea when he
is turned out of Hobart, it is mere snuffle to say
that an honestly-conducted State lottery can
make Australia any worse than it is.
The June bye-elections in Victoria gave a
nasty knock to the Trades Hall Laborites.
Lemmon, at Footscray, failed to hold Hancock’s
old seat, lately vacated by Samuel Mauger. A local
salesman Jacob Fotheringham beat him by
45 votes in a very heavy poll. At Fitzrdy,
O’Connor —a Richmond tailor and Board o’ Works
Commissioner, posing as a Laborite—got the
nomination of the Fitzroy League, but was
rejected by the Political Labour League (for
having opposed Tudor for Yarra) in favor of
M'Grath, ex-tram conductor, and sec. of Bread-
Carters’ Union. The local workers resented this
dictation, and O’Connor came out on top with 1187
votes ; then followed three other candidates with
1021, 942, and 548, and M'Grath was absolute last
with 478. So O’Connor goes in pledged to the
Labor platform, .and is responsible to the local
Labor League—same as his colleague Billson.
Yet the Labor party in Parliament denounces
and repudiates him. The Political Labor League
is making a beautiful mess of things.
If Peacock and Co. get through the session
it will be purely on their luck. They are a talking-
rather than a doing
crowd. Already they
have spent more hours
in Cabinet than there are
days in the session. It
is rarely a Minister is to
be found in his depart-
ment, but he can always
be got at a Cabinet meet-
ing. So far, Peacock’s
luck is carrying all before
it. In the first place, he
was lucky to miss being
branded by the Dooko-
york. Then that dull
equity lawyer, Irvine,
was appointed Opposi-
tion leader, and now he
has resigned in such a
way thatthe Opposition
is scattered to the forty-
four winds of the vast Exhi-
bition building. Unless the
Tory party can induce Irvine •
to withdraw his resignation
it will meet the House with-
out a leader at all. The fact
that Outtrim, Kirton, and
Graham, who were Pea-
cock’s bitterest enemies-
after the Turner Govt,
was defeated, have crept
quietly round to the
Opposition cross benches
is a sure sign that they
want "to get back to
the old fold. Looking at
things as they stand now, it would seem that the
Tory party is practically extinct in Victoria.
Ex-Premier Reid in his late Sydney
speech : .
We got the expenditure (of loan money) down to some-
thing like £2,000,000 a year, which was a safe amount. . .
I was exposed to torrents of abuse because I kept the
public expenditure firmly within limits, and would not
allow these cursed deficiencies to grow under my ad-
ministration.
Both bosh, as usual ! Dibbs got the loan expendi-
ture down to £1,330,000, and Reid, after following
the same economical policy for 18 months, gob
tired and put it up—not down—to over £2,000,000.
Also Reid was a good deal abused for his deficit#
(according to the Auditor-General, £573,127 in
1896, £213,470 in 1897, and other sums), and used
to moan considerably about the injustice of it.
The present bright and frisky statement that he
was abused for not having deficits is something
quite new, even for Reid. .
Almost every week a grieved member of
the (N.S.W.) See Government rises to state that
owing to the tangled condition of the land titles
in Sydney’s resumed area it has been quite im-
possible so far to settle up the claims. This
may be so—or it may be that after borrowing
about £3,500,000 in 12 months the See Govern-
ment hardly knows where to raise another million
or so. But the interesting point is, that no
Minister rises to explain why the See crowd treats
the rents of the resumed area as ordinary revenue,
and spends them as such, well knowing that they
will be wanted to pay arrears of interest on the
purchase money. N.S.W. is just screaming for
information on that point and can’t get it, while
the question that N.S.W. didn’t ask is being
answered till N.S.W. is tired of hearing the
a nswer.

“ Myrtle ” : As I write, in front of my
Mt. Morgan (Q.) boarding-house, is an Indian
hawker, selling scented soap. At the boarding-
house behind, a Chinaman, selling fruit. To the
left, Cingalese quack-doctor ; to the right, hotel
with Malay cook. At rear of hotel, two Chinese
squabbling ; still further to the rear, three drunken
whites (if the dirt were washed off). Unless the
Commonwealth hurries, we’ll soon be past saving.


Griffith, M.L.A. (N.S.W.): —
He refused to defend the Government for not having a
final session of Parliament as requested by the Labor
Party, and he assured his hearers that if when Parliament
did meet it was found that the finances were not being
honestly administered, that £150,000 of trust money and
a quarter of a million of loan moneys had been used as
revenue, as had been alleged, and that the colony was
drifting towards a big deficit, the Labor Party could be
relied upon to do its duty to the electors by removing the
Government from office.
In other words, if, when Parliament meets, the
Labor Party finds that Queen Anne is dead, it
will take any action that may be necessary.
Statistician COGHLAN has been showing for
Heaven knows how many years how the loan-
moneys are misapplied, and the latest misappro-
priation of the trust funds is clear to anyone who
takes the trouble to read the receipts and expendi-
ture accounts in the Government Gazette.
Also, the long, ghastly procession of deficits glares
forth in the Auditor-General’s reports regularly.
If the Labor Party is only beginning to find out
these things it is time N.S.W. got a new Labor
Party —one that is a trifle swifter on its feet.

The plague rat will yet be the salvation of
Brisbane and Townsville. These cities are about
the most unsavoury and insanitary conglomera-
tions of houses in Australia. The state of such
quarters as Petrie Terrace (Brisbane), where
plague is just now prevalent, would disgrace a
backyard in Canton. But now the authorities
have to clean-up, and the ultimate result will be
an unmixed blessing. By the way, there are still
plenty of rats in Sydney. A trap set on George-
street premises, near Hunter-street, last week,
caught 40 in a single night.

Consumption can be Cured!
READ WHAT MR. FRED. STEVENSON SAYS
VITADATIO
HAS DONE FOR HIM.
Dungog, July 17, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —For over two years I suffered from what the
doctors here told me was consumption, and during that
time I suffered considerably and could not get any relief.
Seven times I broke a blood-vessel, and gave up all hope
of recovery. The doctors told me they could not do me
any good, and gave me up. It was then that I heard of
VITADATIO, and I determined to try it. The first bottle
did me a deal of good, and after taking twelve bottles I
can now say I am quite cured. Twelve months ago I
weighed under nine stone, and to-day I was weighed at
your representative’s request, and was just ten stone
eight pounds, and am now working on the roads as
maintenance man, and well able to do a hard day’s work
as well as I could years ago. lam well known here and
will do all in my power to persuade others who are suffer-
ing to try your medicine.
(Signed) FREDERICK STEVENSON,
Government Maintenance Man.
Dungog, N.S.W.
I have read the above carefully, and know it to be
correct, as I have known Frederick Stevenson during his
illness, and have watched his wonderful recovery ; he is
now quite well. I can also add that I have tried VITA-
DATIO for indigestion, and it has done me a wonderful
lot of good.
(Signed) EDWARD PIPER, J.P.

S. A. PALMER,

The Talune Mystery—the Last.
As usual, the third trial ended on a Friday morn-
ing, with a crowded court and an even larger-than-
usual proportion of morbid-minded females, who
had flocked into the audience in the hope of seeing
how a woman would bear herself in the supreme
moment of being told that a jury of men had
decided she should be choked to death.
The hand of the clock trembled a few minutes
past ten. There was something ominous about
the court. The two detectives who had “ worked
up ” the case against accused vvere absent, and
the regular habitues said it indicated that they
knew the jury would return a verdict of guilty,
and did not care to be present to see their
victim sentenced to death. Apparently the
hardened detective is superior to the female
criminal-court lounger. .
A man in a frock-coat appeared carrying a staff
surmounted with a small gilded crown. Behind
him walked one in a black kniekerbocker suit and
white cravat. Last, and most impressive, stalked
Judge Owen —he looks very grim in a wig in the
red robe of his office. These officials, for whom
the Law has usurped the prerogative of Divinity
and arrogated the lordship of life and death,
swept up into the canopied seat, took their places
with an impressive dignity and commenced to
write. The man in black was the Sheriff the
real hangman, though he employs Nosey Bob to
break necks for him.
MRS. JANE SMITH, three times tried at Sydney
on a charge of poisoning Patrick Conway on
board s.s. Talune, and now discharged from
custody.
Photo, by Kerry, Sydney.
Through a door in the northern wall came a
straggling line of men to the jury box. When
they were set, the Judge looked up from his
writing and asked had they agreed upon a
verdict. “No!” said the foreman rising to his
feet. “ Let him be sworn ! ” snapped His
Honor. Down in the dock the woman sat like
a statue. A smile played round the mouth of
solicitor Moss, the underground engineer of the
defence. In a few seconds the jury were dis-
charged and the Grown Prosecutor asked that Mrs.
Smith be bound over on her own recognisances to
appear when called upon. She stepped out of
the spiked dock with alacrity, and, walking
round, stood impassively before the Associate,
who, after some delay, gabbled off something
about appearing when called upon, and not leaving
the State without the permission of the Court
under pain of forfeiting £4). Mrs. Smith bowed
her head, and the Associate fell back into his
seat. Then she walked out of the door without
the slightest sign of emotion, closely followed by
her solicitor.
I stepped into the office of Solicitor Moss. A
quietly-dressed woman looked at me with a pair
of hazel eyes—eyes so soft that it would be hard
to Delieve their owner could possibly screw up her
destructive faculties sufficiently to destroy even a
fly in cold blood. She shook hands, and I noticed
that her brown hair was wavy, and her com-
plexion, in spite of 16 weeks imprisonment, pink
and white. She is intensely feminine, and speak'-
with a low, pleasant voice.
“Yea, thank you ; I’m beginning to feel better
now I had a good sleep yesterday and last night,
and when I woke this morning there was some-
thing pleasant in the air. I feel as though 1 am
just getting over the effects of a heavy blow ; a
little dazed, you know ; I felt that way all through
the trials after I really grasped the fact that all
those men in uniforms, and wigs, and gowns were
deciding whether poor me should be allowed to
live or taken out and murdered by a hangman.
“It takes some time to realise! that your hie is
really in danger. All those men looked pleasant
and human, and that they were mostly there try-
ing to hang me seemed quite impossible gradu-
ally I began to know it was so, and, at first, 1
felt that I must laugh or cry myself mad, and
then I just felt crushed. The feeling has not all
gone yet, and I am afraid sometimes that I shall
wake up and find lam dreaming in the dock. i
suppose it will go away in time.
“ It was dreadful sitting there all those days
with hundreds of eyes fixed upon me I knew
they were all looking at me. I could feel their
eyes from every part of the court. I m very
grateful to some of the papers for their impar-
tiality-impartiality is a great boon nowadays.
Strange that one of the daily papers the one
paper that appears to have all the smart men of
the continent on its staff —didn’t know enough, or
hadn’t instincts decent enough, to give a woman a
fair trial for her life. , . -
“ I’m not going away ; I m going to stay herein
Sydney. We haven’t the money to go back to
Maoriland. The costs of the trial have abso-
lutely ruined us. My husband had to give up his
position to come over here to be with me all
through this horrible time. We had to part with
our little property to save my life, and now we
are down to our last shilling, and are both trying
to get work immediately.
“I suppose it will be very hard to get a start.
Judging by the conversations I have heard in the
trams, and' some of the press remarks, there is a
strong prejudice against me. When I was going
out to Stanmore, after leaving the court, some
women were talking about it, and agreed that I
ought to be burnt alive, and, not knowing who I
was, appealed to me for concurrence. I said some-
thing about the fearful strain of three trials, and
got out into the street. That makes me think it
will be hard to get a start. . . , ,
“ The gaol authorities were very land indeed.
I had everything I could wish. The food was
good and the bed was from the hospital and very
comfortable. All that I complain of is a rule that
a woman while on her trial has to be accompanied
everywhere by a male warder. It s monstrous j
there are female warders, but it was only by the
repeated applications of Mr. Moss that I managed
to have the male replaced by a woman official.
HERMES.
There are two bands in Emmaville (N.S.
Wales), both of which claim the ownership of a
certain drum. Tom Chandler, J.P. and tin-
buyer, has to settle the ownership, and as he is
running for the State Parliament as a candidate
for Glen Innes electorate, each band threatens, if
it doesn’t get the drum, to vote against him. Also
as Chandler is away electioneering, and will pro-
bably not be able to settle the thing in time, pro-
bably both bands will vote against him, so as to
make sure of revenge in case of losing. The
Bulletin advises T.C. to kick holes in the drum
in question and present a new drum each to the
two bands before election-day.

CANADIAN PACIFIC RAILWAY.
This is the Imperial Route Home, as well as the most at-
tractive. It avoids alike the heat of the Red Sea and the
cold of Cape Horn. The railroad journey between the
Pacific and Atlantic Oceans (Vancouver to Montreal)
occupies about 4 days, and is made
WITHOUT CHANGE OE CARRIAGES,
through the grandest scenery in the world—over the
Selkirk and Rocky Mountains, across the Prairies, and
along the North Shore of Lake Superior.
Passengers have the choice of a number of overland
routes, enabling them to visit the principal cities of
Canada and the United States.

Dan Mulcahy, who just squeezed in as
Labor M.P. for Gympie, in place of Fisher,
M H R is a local sharebroker. It was a tough
fight— the Govt, sent up Leahy, and Matt. Reid
dfd most of the Labor-party s battling. But

The worst thing about royalty is the
poetry that is made concerning it. For instance,
the Singapore paper threw this remarkable pro-
duction after the Yorks when they exited from
that black-and-tan seaport■
Welcome ! thrice welcome ! our future King .
Singapore hails you and the would-be Queen.
While we deplore, our “ Mother Queen s death,
Providence so willed it, amidst universal reg e .
Accept our hospitality, sincere and true,
In point of loyalty, can excel us a few.
In the midst of bereavement, too sad to forget,
Duty before everything, the wisest of precept.
England’s honor, uphold at any cost,
Long live Britons, their fame ne er lost
Our volunteers ready at our noble King s cal ,
Our lives, our blood, our all in all ,
Command our services, our gallantry behold,
His enemies shall fly, in numbers untold.
Liberty of thoughts, action and speech.
Even-handed justice to both poor and the rich.
“ Defender of the faith,” religion and creed,
Independent of the caste, color and breed.
Mav this voyage be productive of good,
Mentally, bodily and politically too.
Seeing with one’s eyes, hearing with one s ears,
Is better than book-study for years and years.
All England’s eyes, your movements do watch,
They measure your words—they scan your thoug ts.
Noble son of a noble sire— _
Almighty grant your noble desire.
Singapore bids you most hearty farewell.
With sincere blessings, no tongue can tell.
Long live the King, his shadow ne er grow less,
The “ Queenly Queen ” may God ever bless.
One more request and we will have done
“TELL PAPA WE AKE HAPPY” on your return.

“ The Echuca Miracle.'
In the town of Echuca a case has recently
occurred which is the talk of the town and district.
People marvel and cannot believe what their eyes
have seen. Mr. Michael O’Brien is a respected
citizen, and well known in the community, and
the public can vouch for this remarkable restora-
tion to health and happiness. He relates his ex-
perience, sufferings and cure:—“l have been a
great sufferer dur-
ing the last three
years, and have
tried most patent
medicines which are
being advertised ;
also three doctors
of this town and two
in Melbourne, and
received but little
benefit from any of
the same. I was in
a very bad state, and
had lost all hope of
recovery, when,
luckily for me, a
small book was left
at my house, and
this (upon reading the cases that had been cured
by your Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills), at once
put new hope in my heart, and I at once pur-
chased a bottle from Mr. Cunningham, the local
chemist, and upon using this bottle of your pills,
I found they had made a great improvement in
my health ; so I again purchased in all eight
bottles, and am now in the best of health, which I
owe to your Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. My
case was one of severe indigestion, being informed
by the doctor to that effect. You may publish
this as you think fit, as it may be the means
of helping those who may suffer from a similar
complaint; and being well known in this town, I
am sure there are numbers of my friends who will
confirm all I have said in this letter.” Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood
purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness, indi-
digestion, constipation, headaches, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
boils and blotches, and female ailments. Sold by
chemists and storekeepers, Is. per bottle, or
six bottles 6s. 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H.
Comstock Co., Ltd. (Australasian Dep6t), 58Pitt-
street, Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the
full name blown thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
At Caulfield, last Saturday, a very possible
winner of the forthcoming V.R.C. G.N. Steeple was re-
vealed in old Lacebv, a cast-off from the Millers’ stable.
Laoeb.v bad run away with a stake at Sandown earlier in
the week, and on Saturday he took the Wanda Steeple-
chase just as easily. That is to say, he won by three
lengths after clouting the last fence. His legs are notun-
impeachable, and trouble may come to one of them
before the big Steeple falls due, but as long as he keeps
going satisfactorily the light-weighted Laceby will look
very dangerous. Among the hurdlers that ran at Caul-
field and showed Grand National pretensions Rackstraw
looked fairly promising. Rackstraw was the runner-up
to Aquarius last year. He seems to be getting back to
form. He is in clever hands, and with lCst. 51b. Yeoman’s
unlucky horse should run well.

Principal supporter of 1902 Champagne Stakes
(entries closed last week) is Mr. H. Richards (N.S.W.),
who has eight in—the get of Beauchamp, Simmer, Prince
Chester, Dalmeny, Yardley and Fucile. Mr. H. Ramsay
has five by Mostyn, and Mr. “ Bill ” Forrester a like num-
ber by Gaulus and Niagara. Other owners sending five
each are Messrs. W. H. Mate and “ Jimmy ” Wilson, jun.
The list also embraces brothers to Bobadil, Commander,
The Faithful, Malster, Reel and Hautvillers, half-brothers
to Portsea, Ibex, Johansen and The Palmer, sisters to
Haulette and Finland, and half-sisters to The Chevalier,
Hautain, Amberite, La Carabine, Hindoo and Australia,
as well as near relatives of Diffidence, Merriwee and
Wigelmar.
* * * *
Scobie’s elect should this year be a good murky
prophecy for Melb. Cup. He has entered 11, and last
year with only three he managed to dodge most of the
public.
* • * *
Bonnie Boy (winner of the big event at Canter-
bury Park, Saturday) once belonged to “ Bill ” Forrester,
and subsequently ran among the galloways during the
pony boom. He got back with the “ registered ” when
the embargo placed upon the little ’uns was lifted by the
A.J.C. Bonnie Boy raced about the Northern (N.S.W.)
rivers for some time, but since his return had done little
beyond getting just licked. Nothing had a hope against
him on Saturday. He was bred by Mr. E. Oatley by
Niagara from Bonnie Doon, the Tasmanian-bred mare, by
Manuka from Media by Peter Wilkins.
* * * *
Megaphone’s recent starting at Coonamble
(N.S.W.) races brings to mind that the great old wonder
has now seen 13 summers, and hadn’t been heard from
for some time till the recent announcement that he was
sold to someone somewhere for 21gs. Megaphone has
lived and been owned in Q., M.L., N.S.W. and Vic. at
different times. Many judges rated him Carbine’s equal.
At 3 years (carrying 8.4) he ran second to that flier
Bungebah (7.10) in A.J.C. Epsom, won the Squatters’
Handicap with 8.12, and on the third day, met Carbine,
Cuirassier and Cardigan at w.f.a. in the Craven Plate—
1J miles. Carbine lined up 7 to 4 “ on,” and Megaphone
same odds against. A tough tussle all the journey saw
Carbine win by less than a length. Stated that Mr. W.
T. Jones wanted to buy Megaphone before the race, but
the price didn’t suit him, and he gave the then owner
(Mr. O. E. Brown) £5OO for the call of Megaphone, after
the race, for £3300. Mr. Jones, however, dropped the
matter, and subsequently the late Mr. Donald Wallace
secured the nag. Megaphone proved a failure at the
stud.
* * • *
Died at Kelso (N.S.W.) t’other day Mr. W. M.
Kite, who, years ago, scored big successes at racing as well
as in breeding. Normanby (winner of Sydney Cup) and
Woolstone, who annexed two Tatts. Cups, were Mr. Kite’s,
also Dockairne, Dockwood, and others who left their
marks. It was he who brought out Maltese Cross in foal
tb Wild Oats, and Gozo, the famous Tucka Tuclta stallion,
was the foal. Gozo was Mr. Kite’s when he won A.J.C.
Maiden Plate in ’B6. His present owner is Mr. J. R.
Smith.

Little Joe, winner of Towers J. C. Handicap,
brought off a nicely-planned coup by a narrow margin.
Silva, the Towers Leviathan, bought this Gossoon 3-year-
old from W. Kelso specially' for the race and “ kept ” bim
in his big double book on the Towers and Milchester Han-
dicaps, thus utterly confounding backers. Silva has all
the luck of the Chosen ; he owned Coralie when she won
the Villiers and Carrington Stakes, and has now a
“ boshter” balance to his credit after taking the “ knock ”
in Brisbane 10 years ago.
