Reported that one-time great performer (in late
“ Bill ” Kelso’s stable) Loch Marie died, t’other day, soon
after slipping a Projectile foal, which is bad luck for the
Widdin stud proprietor, Mr. Joseph Thompson. Loch
Marie was a beauty by Lochiel from Barbara, by Bar-
barian (son of Sir Hercules) from Adeline by Kingston.
Big things were expected of her at the stud. L.M.’s best
season was as a five-year-old, when she annexed Moore-
field Flying Handicap (Bst. up), Moonee Valley Turkeith
Handicap (8.9), Williamstown Prince of Wales’ Plate (8.5),
Truganiua Stakes (10.9), A.J.C. Trial (8.5), and Villiers
Stakes. Two and a half years ago, humping 8.11, she
6quelched 37 others in Carrington Stakes, and, same
afternoon, was pulled out for the last race (the Pace
Welter) with 11.9 in the saddle, and won in a canter from
a big field, putting up 1.15 for six fur.

Cavaliero, by Cuirassier (son of Musket) from
Clio, is Maoriland’s premier small-stick horse; the
weight of 13.13 carried by him to the front in
Remuera Hurdles (Auckland) was the heaviest ever borne
locally. There were eight behind him ; minimum weight,
9st.
* * * *
An innovation adopted by Tahuna Park (M.L.)
Trotting Club, paying out on first and second horses,
proved a great success, tote total being £1865 over
amount machined at corresponding meeting last year.
The betting public thinks it can pick a winner in twice—
hence the increased speculation. A few good divs. were
gathered in, and only on one occasion did the second
horse pay more than the first.
* * • *
Someone has gone into the thing and ascertained
that since Paris showed the road five years back 30
Australian nags have gone ’Ome and all but about a fifth
have scored. Among those who didn’t do anything were
Aurum (the best of the lot) who went amiss, Amiable (a
flier here), Survivor, Stoccado (late Cranberry), Sailor Boy,
and a couple from Bananaland. Paris, Form, Acmena,
Calra, Old Clo, Newhaven, Australian Star, Merman, The
Grafter, Battalion and Georgic, have performed extra well,
while Achray, Ravensplume, Marrazonna, His Grace,
Tornado, Oban, Essex and S3'erla have succeeded in
getting away with minor events. Since ’97, the spoils
gathered in by the first-named batch embrace the
Cesarewitch, Cambridgeshire, Prince Edward Handicap
(twice), Lewes (five times), City and Suburban (three),
Northamptonshire Stakes, and Ascot and Goodwood
Cups. All this in face of the fact that not during any one
season have there been more than a score of Australian
nags at work.

* * * *
The other night, at Melb. Democratic Club,
dusky Peter Felix met old-timer Mick Dooley in pre-
sence of a £2OO-house. Betting at ringside, 4 to 1 on
Felix, with few takers. The black stripped lithe and
shiny, but one glance at Mick showed that condition and
he are henceforth strangers. Latter received a great
ovation from old sports present, and smiled his thanks in
his old, simple, childlike fashion. First sparring round
left him already laboring and Felix confident, and the
end was already plainly in sight. He W'as a mere punch-
ing-ball in the second bout, and was sent to the boards
for three periods of 9sec. in succession, without attempt-
ing to return a blow. A sigh of regret and relief w’ent
up when his seconds, in pity, caved in. It w'as a pathetic
sight to see old Mick with cut and bleeding nose, sitting
in his corner with downcast head, comforted like a child
by sorrowful followers. His days for the game have gone,
and should he tackle it again, all that is left of him should
be arrested for attempted suicide.

Shades of the mighty ! What is the bruising biz coming
to when the halt and the lame are champions.
• » * *
That whilom Australian 11.4 top-noteher, Dan
Creedon’s, apparent return to old time form was mis-
leading, as last month he met Marvin Hart, and, after the
most torrid thing in fist fights seen for years, was clean
knocked out in the sixth round. Operations were savage
from the jump. Dan’s left “peeper” was closed in the
second round, but he took heaps of stoush (half of which
would have shattered most ordinary bruisers) in the hope
of finding the fatal point. The fourth term found the
audience crazed with excitement, so vicious and stiff was
the punching. The gong’s sound saved Dan in the fifth
round, but he was too far gone to survive another.
Creedon is gray as the proverbial badger, and looks years
older than he really is.

Second round of Sydney senior grade Rugby
commenced on Saturday, and South Sydney is now top,
with ’Varsity and Eastern Suburbs bracketed, one point
less, second, and Glebe (10).fourth. Glebe accounted for
’Varsity—6 to 3 ; 6000 looked on. North Sydney and
Newtown played a draw —3 each. South Sydney beat
Balmain —7 to 6 (their second win in succession by 1
point), and Eastern Suburbs settled Western Suburbs—l 7
to 3.: Ultimate winners still seem very much hidden.
There are only 2 points difference in four clubs, which
means that half the original entrants are still well in the
running.

* * * «
Australian cricketer Albert Trott recently had
a brace of eggs opposite his name in one match for the
first time. Playing for M.C.C. against Sussex he was
clean bowled by Bland in the initial effort, and next time
had his wicket shattered per medium of a brilliant throw-
in by Bland from mid-off. Albert got even though, for
he bowled Bland in the first innings and Bland was
caught off Trott’s work in the second. Trott’s average in
that match was 9 for 63.
* » ♦ *
Hampden Cup and attendant amateur golf
supremacy of N.S.W. was won, Saturday, on Aus-
tralian G.O. links, at Botany, by Mr. E. P. Simp-
son. Talk about a good all-round man 1 Competi-
tion extended over two days, and began on Bondi links
previous week, when ultimate winner did best by finish-
ing “ all square.” At Botany, he also led with 9 down.
Bogey was stiffened considerably this time, being brought
from 83 to 78 at Bondi, and 87 to 83 at Botany. Mr.
Simpson returned the best gross result in Saturday’s play
as well as the best individual round at each course. The
Cup was instituted in ’9B, and presented by Governor
Hampden. Mr. H. M‘Neil won on the first two occasions,
and Mr. E. J. B. Macarthur on the third, Mr. E. P. Simp-
son being runner-up each time.
* * • *
Final heat of N.S.W. bowling “Champion of
Champions ” tourney (meaning champion of all defined
club champions, as distinct from the individual cham-
pionship for which any and every bowler may compete)
between “ Jim ” Hogue, M.P., and “Joe” Bradbury,
last year’s winner of the title, was decided last Saturday.
Bradbury led from the start, and at times was many
points to the good, but Hogue plodded along so effec-
tively that they tied thrice. Hogue opened the last
“ head ” two to the bad and drew a fine shot, but Brad-
bury “ rested ’’ it with a better, and won by 31 to 28. In
all 41 ends were contested. Bradbury now not only lords
it over the different club champions, but also over the in-
dividual champion of the State.
» * * m
“Bias” : Bowlers’ methods of doing things are
as ancient as their game To-day we have two champions
of N.S.W. One (the “ individual champion ”) earned his
distinction by winning from all “ associated ” comers, the
other (the Champion of Champions) through beating pre-
viously singled Out chib top-nocchers. Among those who
went under in the competition won by the former was
the holder of the last-named title, who now turns round
and beats the individual premier. Truly matters are
very much mixed. Which is really the champion or the
better of the two ? Again, who comes better accredited
—the man who beats all club champions or the man who
accounts for all comers —champions or otherwise. To win
his particular title each had to compete in the event won
by the other.

» * * •
Australian premier pigeon shot, Arthur Eales,
is making things hum “ down under.” Early last month
he whacked £33 stakes with another ; later he tied at the
fifth round with the English crack, Mr. Southby Hewitt,
and shared £67. That afternoon 16 kills out of 18 shot at
were credited to him. A day or two afterwards Mr.
Eales won a handicap, beating six opponents, and subse-
quently competed in a £3 sweep with £2O added, to
which 14 shooters subscribed. An English shot (Mr. Col-
lier) and Mr. Eales divided the £62 at the close of the
fourth round. T’other day Mr W. J. L. Eales’ brother
won the London Challenge Cup, shot for under the
auspices of the London Gun Club —an exclusive organisa-
tion. The excellence of Mr. Eales’ form and work just
now will be better understood when ’tis known that
they’ve got him at the almost shutting-out distance of
33Jyds. This is a yard further back than the recognised
British limit.
• * * •
Burly Fred Cavill —channel and ocean swimmer,
and father of the world-famous family of natationists
owning his name —has thought out a new system of sight-
ing rifles —an automatic back-sight, which he claims ismore
simple, stauncher and more accurate than the present
method. Experts say the idea is all right. The Admiral
has sent a sample ’Ome in charge of'an officer of H.M.S.
Blake, who will explain the modus. Cavill pere is an old
soldier, and has smelt powder with a vengeance. Half a
century back he was one of the crew of the Royal yacht
Fairy, and was on that vessel when the late Queen led
Sir Chas. Napier’s Baltic fleet to sea from Spithead.
Later lie saw service in the Russian war, and was aboard
the Penelope frigate when she ran aground under the
Russian forts at Bomarsund in the Baltic, and remained
hard and fast for 3A hours, while the Muscovites poured
in red-hot shot and shell. The Penelope ultimately got
off after losing all her guns and a big percentage of the
crew killed and wounded. Cavill afterwards served in
the same vessel during the Mutiny, and since then 40
years of his life have been devoted to the Volunteer
cause. He is now on the retired list, with the rank of
staff-paymaster of the N.A.V’s.

T’other day Vic. amateur ped. G. A. Moir, at
Melbourne Harriers Sports, won a 300yds. handicap from
scratch in 32 4-ssec., thus establishing a fresh Australian
best, which displaces Charlie Rush’s 33J and Arthur
Macarthur’s 33 1-5. Both were N.S.VV. athletes. Rush,
now' postmaster at Cobar, has been sick for a year or
more.

Photo, by Eden, Sydney.
A certain Parliamentary candidate is the hero
of the following. He attended a picnic of the local trades
a couple of years ago ; and condescended to join in the
contest for the “ blue ribbon” of the day—the old buffers’
race. He not only competed for but won the event, and
was duly invested with the “ ribbon ” —a pair of vases.
This year he was elected president of the trades associa-
tion, and in gratitude for the honor promised a handsome
prize for next sports meeting. The race for the presi-
dent’s prize created great and many envious
glances were thrown at.the winner as he went up to re-
ceive—the identical pair of vases which the president had
won two years before. To make it more bitter, the
winner of the president’s prize was the original donor of
the vases when won by that prudent gentleman. What
are that candidate’s chances of receiving the local Labor
vote?

’tothersider, or Pettitt, the champion.
» * • *
Belford Buller, a St. Bernard, shown at Sydney
the other day, is 37in. high at the shoulder ; and there-
fore is placarded “ the tallest dog in the world.” Any-
way, a phenomenal height, seeing that anything over
30in. in a male and 27in. for the other sort is up to
standard—and “ the taller the better, provided the sym-
metry is maintained.” Barry and Simplon, the tallest
among famous modern St. Bernards, were only 32Jin.;
the former weighed 1501 b., the latter 1701 b. The Sydney
dog, though only 17 months old, is said to weigh 1751 b.

“ The total value of presents given to the
Royalties, by the Maoris at Rotorua, is estimated
at £2000.” What a mean, cadging business this
monarchy is !
On the 31st March the American Tobacco Co.,
of New South Wales, placed a contract with Messrs.
Stuart Bros, for the erection of a new factory and offices
adjoining the Masonic Hall in Castlereagh-street, Sydney.
The time-limit of the contract is seven-and-a-half months,
as from the Ist June last. The building will be replete
with all the most modern appliances, machinery and con-
veniences, and it is the intention of the company to have
the star factory in Australasia.
Concerning the Royal visit to Maoriland .
King Richard went very near losing his mana over an
incident in connection with the ducal visit. He was
determined that the Royalties should see in M.L. some-
thing that they could not see anywhere else, and that it
should be something the recollection of which would be
likely ever to remain with them, as a testimony to his
genius as a host. He arranged for an old-time Maori
gathering at Rotorua, and at Rotorua there were as-
sembled some 5000 splendid specimens of the Maori
people, from the furthermost parts of the land
with one notable exception—and thereby hangs an
interesting story. The Auckland people had made up
their minds that the royalties on arriving in Auckland
harbor should have more than an ordinary wel-
come, and they set about arrangin': for a grand dis-
play by Maori war-canoes. It was proposed that Mahuta,
the Maori “ King,” should be asked to assemble his
people at Auckland, with their war-canoes, and go forth
at the right moment to welcome the son of the King in a
manner that for once in a way would dispel “ that tired
feeling” from his face. King Mahuta consented, and it
was arranged that he should present the Duke and
Duchess with an address of welcome on behalf of the
Maori people. King Richard, however, said “No,” and
his word, as usual, was law, but it was the most un-
popular thing he ever did, and it will not be
forgotten for a very long time in Auckland. King
Richard said that it would never do to recognise
Mahuta in this way ; that he was no more a king than a
dozen other Maori chiefs, and that to give him promin-
ence on such an occasion would offend the whole of the
other tribes who had agreed to assemble at Rotorua to
meet the Duke. The Aucklanders declined to take King
Dick seriously; they said they didn’t specially want
Mahuta, but they did want an old-time war-canoe display,
and they would have it. A lot of money was collected
and arrangements were made to get several hundred
natives and a number of canoes down from the King
Country, but somehow or other—nobody seems to know
exactly why—the affair never came off. The Premier has
had to bear the brunt of it, and at the reception he was
actually hooted for his opposition to the scheme. This
was anything but pleasing to Seddon, who is
really very popular with the crowd, and it went hard with
him that it should appear otherwise in the presence of
the Duke. He has been at pains to explain that neither
himself nor the Govt, nor any Govt, department put
difficulties in the way of the canoes being carried to
Auckland per rail, but nothing will remove the unpalat-
able fact that he was acually hooted in the presence of
Royalty.
The Duke, or whoever was responsible for the pro-
gramme of the ceremony in connection with the landing
at Auckland, made one unfortunate blunder for which
the mothers of the Northern City will never forgive him.
For weeks previous to the arrival of 11.R.H. some
thousands of children and teachers belonging to the
public schools had been assiduously practising a display
known as “The Living Union Jack.” Much time and
pains had been taken in rehearsing, and a lot of money
spent in decking so many little ones out in dresses of
appropriate color and design. An incident of the display
was to be the singing of “God Save the King.” Two hours
before the time appointed for Royalty to pass that particu-
lar spot the young people and their instructors assembled,
and they went through that weary wait with exemplary
patience, believing that eventually they would be re-
warded by a sight of their future King amd Queen.
Judge then of the surprise of the children and the multi-
tude that had assembled there when the Royalties drove
past without stopping, and without indicating in any way
that they were pleased with the exhibition. There was
keen heartburning over the incident, and the slight was
felt so severely that the latter part of the Children’s
programme was not carried out. The thing rankles
deeply in the breasts of the mothers of Auckland.
The Duke of Cornwall was never so near “ the infernal
regions ” as when he visited Tikitere, a few miles’ drive
from Rotorua, the Maori wonderland. The sights of Tiki-
tere must be approached on foot, and for a considerable
distance as one wanders around, with crumbling pumice
underfoot, boiling mud and water, and hissing steam all
around, great care has to be exercised to avoid getting
scalded or worse. The Duke escaped scatheless,
and without the least indication of uneasiness saw
all there was to be seen. Capt. Pont, of the Govt,
steamer Tutanekai, who was with the party, had, how-
ever, less good fortune, for he put one foot through the
sulphur crust, getting his foot scalded ; and Minister
Ward had a narrow escape of a similar adventure. It
would have been quite too utterly sensational an advt.
for the Maoriland Wonderland had we lost the Heir
Apparent in one of its boiling mud-holes, or if he had
suddenly “ gone under ” through the palpitating sulphur
crust over which one has to walk to view the “ sights” of
Ohinemutu.

Who Samuel Byrnes is.
The writer is glad to take the hand of Mr.
Samuel Byrnes and give it a hearty squeeze.
That we are parted for the moment by ten
thousand miles of seawater doesn’t count. May
you live right along and prosper, Mr. Byrnes.
In this grumbling old world, more full of aclies
and pains than an American water-melon
black seeds, it is jolly to hear a man sing out, “ /
am first-class every way ; and, as for my health, it
couldn't be better.”
This is great, especially when we understand
what went before it. For several years Mr.
Byrnes was in bad form. Dyspepsia it was, and
a very nasty variety of that abominable complaint.
He got butlittle sleep—so he writes—and was in
pain most of the time. He called in the doctors,
one after another, and asked them what he was to
do.
They agreed on the main point, and they were
right. Indigestion, liver disorder, and the nerve
troubles which are thrown in as make-weights
the doctors said these things once got rid of, our
friend would be all right.
And they did their best to bring it to pass—
these worthy men. They gave him drugs the
same, no doubt, that have been so often and so
vainly given.
“After the doctors gave me up,” says Mr.
Byrnes, “ I tried everything I could think of, or
others recommended to me. At first, I felt sure I
would come upon something helpful, but I never
did, until somebody told me about Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. Even after reading what was printed in
books and papers, as to the merits of this prepara-
tion, I still shook my head.
“ ‘ Not likely to be any better than the rest, I
said; ‘the chances are all against it.’ For, you
see, my heart was, as you may say, down in my
shoes, and I was not in the mood to take hope
from any testimony that could be produced.
“All the same, I began taking the Syrup; I
don’t know why. The good effect was almost im-
mediate. I stopped casting up my food, and com-
menced to feel stronger and better. Without
troubling you with the story of how I got on step
by step, I will merely say that the medicine
seemed to build me up, and put me together bit
by bit, until I was sound and well as any man
wants to be.
“ I have lived here sixty-one years, and many
people in this neighbourhood know what I have
said to be true, and were as much astonished at
my recovery as I was myself. lam now seventy-
one years of age, and hale and hearty. For this
wonderful blessing I thank God and Mother
Seigel’s Syrup.
“ As the reader looks at my signature and says,
‘Who is Samuel Byrnes?’ I present him my
compliments, and reply that, if we ever meet, I
shall be glad to tell him by word of mouth much
more than I have written, and to testify all day
long for the remedy that made me the man I am.”
Samuel Byrnes, Lemon Grove, Penrith, N.S.W.,
September Ist, 189 ff
The prediction that Ellen Terry would never take
kindly to elderly-woman parts has been falsified. In the
beautiful performance of “ Coriolanus,” staged from Alma
Tadema’s designs, Ellen Terry appears as Irving’s mother,
and is said to be very strong.
At Poverty Point.
The approaching marriage of clever Dion Boucicault
and charming Irene Vanbrugh will not be the immediate
result of love at first sight, anyhow. They met in Aus-
tralia eleven years ago, when Miss Vanbrugh was the in-
teresting young heroine of J. L. Toole’s co. Perhaps
there was something prophetic in the fact that the lady
made her first appearance at the Princess’s on the same
night (April sth, 1890) that Brough and Boucicault opened
their new Melb. Bijou Theatre with “ Money.” Dion B.
didn’t act in the luckless comedy, but he wa# on the spot
as stage-manager. Miss Vanbrugh was then a pale, slim,
bright-looking charmer of, say, something older than 22.
Anyone could have been excused for falling in love with
her. Lord Hopetoun opened his first Parliament at that
time, and old Johnny Toole and Miss Vanbrugh were
distinguished Visitors at the ceremony. Present scribe,
gazing at Johnny’s fair companion from the opposite side
of the Vic. Legislative Council, wrote her name upon his
heart, and now lives to tell the tale.
At Melb. Salon, last Thursday night, when, as it hap-
pened, none of the local puff journals had their recording
angels present, Miss Eunice (Unie) Russell gave her
dramatic powers a severe test. Her Princess Katherine
was a very French and pretty piece of work, Mrs. G. B.
Lewis (in skirts, not as a profane caricature of George
Rignold) taking the part of Henry the Fifth, a,nd playing
up to the brilliant amateurs. As mad Ophelia, the fair
Miss Russell carried her audience away from themselves,
and caused many tears to trickle down their innocent
noses. Mrs. Lewis may well be proud of having provided
the dramatic instruction which this gifted girl has taken
so earnestly to heart.
The liveliest turn seen at a Melb. house for a long
time happened at the Opera House t’other night, when a
box of “ Johnnies,” irritated by Austin Rudd’s nerve-
twisting “ sez-z-z,” started heaving bath buns at his
head. Rudd bore about a shilling’s worth of confection-
ary ; then he burst all bounds, and swooping on the wads
of dough that littered the stage, threw them with much
vehemence in the teeth of his enemies. Having followed
up the buns with a big stick and several tough, portable
objects, the artist hurled himself into the thick of the
Johnnies, and “foremost fighting fell.” He routed the
enemy, and turned up for his next ditty wearing a lusty
black eye, but blown with victory. After all, a bun is a
comparatively innocuous thing to throw at a comic
singer, but some people are never satisfied.
Hichens (in his New book, “ The Slave,”) on Melba :
“ A world-renowned prima-donna, in a garden hat and a
white muslin gown spotted with mauve orchids, was
eating pineapple and talking French with a vivid colonial
accent. Her companion was a big boy who hated music.
She loved big boys, and as she hated music too, they
naturally had a great deal in common. They were talking
about the Grand Prix, and the boy was giving her a good
tip for it. She cared more for a good tip than for all the
operas ever composed. Yet she could sing with consum-
mate art when she chose. She had been born with an
iron will and with an inordinate ambition, and God had
given her one of the loveliest voices in the world.”
There’s a ton of solid, accurate information in that para-
graph. Melba is generally believed to make quite as much
money by getting “the straight griffin” (whether re
copper-mining or racing) as by singing.
“ Jim ” : Went to hear the Imperial Concert Co. and
w'as astonished to find that the vocalists, including
Madame Belle Cole, came upon the platform with a piece
of music or a note book in hand, and sang from it.
Surely no artist nowadays ought to make what is prac-
tically a show of being not note-perfect and word-perfect
in the commonplace ballad which he or she expects to
impress the audience with. You don’t find a Dolores
referring to a piece of music or a bit of paper as she goes
along. A properly-educated Australian novice doesn’t
make such a mistake. The recent debutante, Miss Olive
Kingston, had empt\ - hands when she trilled her long and
difficult operatic passages.
Signora Benedetto, of the Italian Opera 00., didn’t want
to bang the Melb. Agb critic for remarking that her
figure was a heavy handicap to her performance as Aida,
the slave girl, but she sent the rude man, with her com-
pliments, a photo of herself in less unsuitable garments.
The Benedetto as she appears in “ Ernani ” is quite a
stately prima-donna. The long train gives her more
height, and her deportment is a work of art.
Italian opera, here, as in its native land, is disfigured
by the hideous arrangement (which looks like half of a
baby’s cradle, or an Esquimaux canoe turned upside-
down), placed right in front of the conductor, in the
stage foreground. Under cover of this eyesore, the
prompter follows the opera, with the score lying open on
the stage, beating the time with his finger, if necessary,
and giving the singers their cues. Apparently he stands
below the stage, and has only his head, shoulders a.nd
arms protruding through the hole in the floor. Italian
companies seem unable to get along without the assist-
ance of the man under the canoe.
America’s most popular dramatist (writes “The
Stroller” to Tub Bulletin from New York) is Clyde Fitch,
whose “ Lovers’ Lane ” will shortly be presented in Aus-
tralia. Fitch has a much stronger work in “The
Climbers,” a play of American social life —with a morally-
rotten, embezzling husband, and a self-sacrificing lover
that tries, in vain, to make him go straight. In one
striking scene the lover discovers the intended flight of
the husband, and makes him confess before his wife and
relatives. He will only do so in the dark. The lights are
turned black out, the confession is heard in the darkness,
and the lights are suddenly turned up just in time for
the lover to catch the husband bolting from the room.
The strong part of the wife is played by Amelia Bingham,
a large woman of the Nance O’Neil type, with many of
Nance’s vocal mannerisms. She, too, started her career
under M’Kee Rankin. The “ Lovers’ Lane ”is a “ pretty
play ” of a liberal-minded parson who wants to keep a
woman separated from her husband because she is useful
as his choir-leader. The grocery, the church, and the
schoolroom dominate the play and give it a way-back
simplicity. This season Fitch has had four plays running
simultaneously in New York alone. He wrote “ The
Cowboy and the Lady," which introduced Nat Goodwin
to London.
Amusing to read Wilson Barrett’s skite about his
readiness to produce new plays by any dramatists, known
or unknown, whose work will pass an examination by six
chosen experts. Most of the plays produced by Barrett,
of late years, have professed to be written wholly or
partly by himself. He’s the only fellow, seemingly, who
can “ satisfy ” his own six experts.

Nominations for N.S.W. State Election
closed with 350 candidates for 112 seats, and out
of the 250 new aspirants there is not a single
name which inspires any particular confidence.
Thirteen members win their seats unopposed, and
so far the See Ministry has more than held its
own. McFarlane of the Clarence wins his
seat without a fight for the second time running,
while D. McDonnell has the unique experience of
securing his first election unopposed. He is the
Labor nominee for Cobar, vice Spence, transferred
to the Federal Parliament. Other members
returned are Waddell and Bennett, two of the
Ministry, John Norton and Opposition Leader
Lee.
“ Adam’s Diary,” translated by Mark
Twain, is reminiscent of Mark’s old style.. He
thinks Adam “has now become sufficiently
important as a public character to justify this
publication ”: —
Monday.— The new creature with the long hair is a
good deal in the way. It is always hanging around and
following me about. I don’t like this ; lam not used to
company. I wish it would stay with the other animals.
Wednesday. —Built me a shelter against the rain, but
could not have it in peace to myself. The new creature
intruded. ... I wish it would not talk; it is always
talking. , , .
Sunday.— This morning found the new creature trying
to clod apples out of that forbidden tree. ... It calls
itself Eve. . .. ....
Monday. —She has been climbing that tree again.
Clodded her out of it.
Tuesday.— She has taken up with the snake now. .. .
I am glad, because the snake talks, and this enables me to
get a rest. . ,
Friday. —She says the snake advises her to try the fruit
of that tree. . . . ..
Next year.—We have named it Cain. She caught it
while I was up country trapping on the north shore of the
Erie ; caught it in the timber, a couple of miles from our
dug-out—or it might have been four, she isn’t certain
which. . . . She thinks more of it than she does of any of
the other animals. Her mind is disordered—everything
shows it. . . . It isn’t one of us, for it does not walk ; it
is not a bird, for it does not fly; it is not a frog, for it
does not hop; . . . I feel sure it is not a fish, though I
cannot get a chance to find out whether it can swim or
not. It merely lies around, and mostly on its back with
its feet up. I have not seen any other animal do that
before.
A good many months afterwards, Adam goes out
hunting, with the intention of catching another
animal of the same sort. He returns empty-
handed ; only to find that, while he was away,
Eve—“ without stirring from the home estate—
has caught another one ! ” He ‘ ‘ never saw such
luck.”
Next day.—l have been comparing the new one with
the old one, and it is perfectly plain they are the same
breed. I was going to stuff one of them for my collection,
but she i& prejudiced against it, lor some reason or othei.
. . . She calls it Abel.


Before Justice Connolly, of Maoriland, a
Hogging Judge, a man was the other day acquitted
by the jury on a charge of assaulting a Maori
woman, whereupon the Judge opened-out upon
the jury. He did not wonder at the frequency of
tins class of offence when juries returned such
verdicts. Ihen, to the accused, who asserted his
innocence; “You are discharged ; consider your-
self very lucky.” In North Queensland, 20 years
ago, a Judge on circuit acted somewhat similarly
whereupon the foreman of the jury: “With all
respect to the Supreme Court, I’ll just ask your
Honor to mind your own business. Your re-
marks are a gross insult to the jury, which is
sworn to do its duty, and will not tolerate your
Honor s insolence.” And what did the Judge say ’
He said nothing-. He only glared.

Court this ruling, from Knox Wigram’s “Jus-
tices Notebook,” a recent and high authority was
quoted : J
, Alad whose 21st birthday happens on a Wednesday is
held to be of age during the whole of that day, no matter
at what hour he may have been born. Consequently, his
non-age must expire on the last instant on Tuesday
Consequently, as he is not a minor on the last instant on
aj (Tuesday), and as the law does not recognise
10n a day. he is not a minor at any portion of
age ' BO 12 o’clock on Monday night he is of
Whereby Good Friday falls on the previous
Wednesday, and many other curious things hap-
pen. 4or instance, if a man spends portion of a
day pounding his wife, the law can’t recognise
the incident, for it takes no account of portion of
+1 that happened at a period
that had no legal existence didn’t legally happen

Rev. Osborne Lilley (Brisbane) re Poly-
nesians (in Australian Christian World) :
They are well fed and suitably housed. They have
wages enough to clothe themselves in broad cloth and
fine attire, some smoke cigars and ride their bicycles.
The Rev. Lilley has evidently come across some
of the “ walkabout” pimps and panders, who, in
conjunction with the Chinese, haunt the low quar-
ters of Bundaberg and Mackay. The rev. gentle-
man surely hasn’t the front to maintain that the
average “boy” (on his £6 and keep per annum)
trots round in broadcloth smoking cigars !
The Maoris are th.e only South Sea Island
race which is holding its own against white civi-
lisation. The Maori population of M.L. is now
42,000 against the 39,000 of five years ago; but
the proportion of half-castes and quarter-castes is
steadily increasing. A Young Maori Party has
recently been formed, having for its platform the
discouragement and abolition of objectionable and
pernicious customs in connection with Maori
meetings of all kinds, the suppression of the drink
traffic, the adoption of the mo3t suitable clothes
and the most suitable buildings, the higher educa-
tion of the Maori, and the extension of systematic
agricultural and pastoral pursuits. A sum of
£lOOO has already been collected towards the
erection of a Maori school for native girls. Gradu-
ally the party will drift in the direction of politics
and may ultimately be a very live organisation.
“ S.F.” : Be that lad and Jupiter’s moons
(B. 6/4/’01). The eye, in such cases, possesses
accidental perfection of all its parts with unusual
receptivity of retina and nervous-mechanism.
Such an eye—as the light-rays from the Pleiades,
or from Jupiter’s moons, are practically parallel—
would be able to distinguish the smaller members
of the constellation, or the moons aforesaid. Of
course the chance of an individual possessing o ie
perfect sight-organ is infinitely greater than that
of his having two. And as we all, who don’t
squint, use binocular vision, the discovery by its
owner of a single perfect eye ,would become yet
another chance.

time it arrived. I had sent for my own doctor, but had
not had one night’s rest for a week. I started taking the
Bronchitis Cure three times a day as directed, and was
very much eased at once. At the end of a week I only
took it twice a day, and then only every night for a
week, as I was so much better when, thanks to the Lord
for adding His blessing, I was quite well, and walked into
town and back without feeling any fatigue. I had not
done that, previously for 12 months—always went in the
omnibus—as walking caused me such pain and distress in
the chest. I always walk now, and never feel it, and I
am stronger than I have been for years. I thank my son
for his great kindness in sending the medicine, and am,
dear Sir,
“Yours very truly,
“M. MORTIMER.’
Extract from a letter, since written by the same lady to
her son, Mr. John S. Mortimer, Llenwellyn, Katunga,

The extract runs as follows: —“ As for myself, thank
the Lord, lam feeling stronger than I have for years. I
had an attack of Bronchitis in November, but Hearne’s
Bronchitis Cure was again successful. I feel quite well,
and walk into town feeling quite strong.
“ I must ask you to send me six bottles more of the
medicine, as I wish to hgve a supply in the house. I
have tried to get it made up here, and let my chemist
have a bottle to see what he could do. He tells me this
week he can make nothing out of it, he never saw any-
thing like it before, so there is only one thing for me to
do—send for more. I have never kept in bed one day
since 1 commenced to take it. I used to be in bed a fort-
night at a time always, and after that for months I was
as weak as I could possibly be, and was always taking
cod liver oil, so you will see at once it is quite worth
while sending for it such a long distance.
“ Something more I must tell you. Charlotte has been
very ill since I wrote you. Her cough was so bad she
never had a night’s rest, and was spitting up blood
very much. The doctor told her husband that there was
nothing more he could do for her. So on the Sunday I
sent her half a bottle of the Bronchitis Cure, and told
her to try it, and if she did not use it, not to waste it,
but send it back again. She had such confidence in her
doctor that I thought she would not try it. On the
Wednesday I sent over again, and she was much better,
the night’s rest was very good, and cough and bleeding
from the lungs better. She sent for another half-bottle,
and on the following Sundaj' sent over to say that she
was quite cured and did not require any more medicine.
So you see what great good it has done, and she wishes
to have some of my next supply.”

A Seven Years’ Case.
EXPECTORATING BLOOD AND MATTER.
COMPLETELY CURED.
“ Mr. W. G. Hearne.
“Dear Sir,—Your medicine has cured me of bronchitis
and asthma, from which I suffered for upwards of seven
years, during which period I was scarcelv ever free from
coughs, and frequently the difficulty of breathing was so
distressing that for nights in succession I had to sit up.
I write you this acknowledgment from a sense of duty, as
in my case every other treatment had failed. For a year
previous I bad been getting very much worse, and at the
time I obtained your medicine I was confined to bed
suffering from a most violent cough, expectorating blood
and matter, and apparently beyond hope of recovery.
The first dose of the medicine gave me welcome relief,
and I steadily improved as I continued the treatment,
until I became, as I am now, quite well.—Yourssincerely,
H. WALKER, Balmain, Sydney.’’
Asthma.
PREVIOUS TREATMENT FAILED.
A SEVENTEEN YEARS’ CASE.
CURED BY THREE BOTTLES.
Mr. Alex. J. Anderson, of Oak Park, Charleville,
Queensland, writes :—“After suffering from Asthma for
17 years, and having been under a great many different
treatments without benefit, I was induced to try Hearne’s
medicine for Asthma. After taking three bottles of this
medicine I quite got rid of the Asthma, and since then,
which was in (he beginning of 1883— 15years ago—l have
not had the slightest return of it. The medicine quite
cured me, and I have much pleasure in recommending it.”
Writing again on the 4th April, 1899, he states: “1
am keeping very well now. Never have the slightest
return of the Asthma.”

idea of the extent and importance of their profess.ona

dividual like the writer.
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of par lieu l
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for ey eing
and examining the polished steel articles "nth which s
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. Th? collection in this room is in every way up I
date. Whenever a new instrument conies to the tronr,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added tc
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbm in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Lmltc
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any artie.e or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary m main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable cve-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger s admission
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
dass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving th it
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Aus ml . a
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they w re
present in personal consultation at the institution.
1 The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a

sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages

requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field m any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast”for the thorough comprehension of the most pei-
feotly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
meii Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult oi
all professions-that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases oi mankind-we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any knotv-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippant y dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessnessof
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “ guilibles ” are not so plentiful as they used to
he” the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and wor t out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
W hat better and more striking illustratioh of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, bner
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. v\ hy .
Because he dwarfs other men in this-branch of sci nee.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at nice
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
sonic one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows. . , . , .
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
thev'can have but a hazy notion, and-superficial know-
edge To use a liaekneied but expressive phrase, they
are “Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
sieve the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,

TO THE tobacco trade
W. D. & H. 0. WILLS (AUSTRALIA), LIMITED.
CAPITAL - - - £250,000.
BOARD OP DIRECTORS:
Sib W. H. WILLS, Bart., Chairman.
Sib E. P. WILLS, K.C.8., Yice-Chairman.
Sir FREDERICK WILLS, Bart., M.P.
GEORGE ALFRED WILLS, Esq.
HENRY HERBERT WILLS, Esq. (Managing Director in England).
G. E. TODMAN and A. J. WARRY, Managing Directors in Australia.
The Tobacco Trade of Federated Australia is hereby informed that
W. D. & H. O. WILLS, LIMITED, BRISTOL, ENGLAND,
in conjunction with
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO., of SYDNEY,
Have made arrangements whereby all the well-known brands of their Tobaccos intended for consumption in Eederated Australia
shall be manufactured in Sydney, N.S.W.
For this purpose a New Company has been formed, under the title of
W. D. & H. 0. WILLS (AUSTRALIA), LIMITED,
Eor the purpose of taking over the English Company’s business in New South Wales, Victoria, Queensland, South Australia and
Tasmania, together with Messrs. HEYDE, TODMAN & CO’S business, and Messrs. CAMERON BROS. & CO’S Virginia
Tobacco Eactory, in Sydney.
The New Company will start under the most favorable auspices, and its productions, made from the same materials as at
present employed, will be distinguished by the same care and excellence of manufacture which have made the English Company’s
Brands so popular in all parts of the world.
W. D. & H. O. WILLS (AUSTRALIA), LIMITED, confidently looks to the trade generally for the support which it has
hitherto extended to the English Company, and feels sure that it will be enabled to give every satisfaction to its customers
in all parts of Eederated Australia.
The Head Office of the New Company will be situated at the premises of the late Firm—
Hedye, Todman & Co., 39 York Street, Sydney.

COMMONWCALTH.


A Paddington Tribute.
Another Convincing Narr&tiv
The Case of MR. THQMAB BRADY.
(by our reporter.)
A short distance from Oxford-street there
is a comfortable little bootmaking business
carried on by Mr. Thomas Brady, at No.
,47 Glenmore-ruad, Paddington, where our
representative found that gentleman en-
gaged in his usual vocation.
y “I have called to ask about your long ill-
ness, Mr. Brady.” _
“It was a long illness, you’re right. For
about seven years I did not know what it
was to be entirely well. I was always
either bilious or else suffering from indiges-
tion, and in that time I had consulted four
doctors, and between them they gave me no
end of instructions regarding food, and how
I should take more out-door exercise ; all of
which advice I willingly carried out, for I
had been ailing too long. Their prescrip-
tions —consisting of multifarious pills
and medicines —proved altogether useless,
and nothing they gave me prevented
my digestion from going wrong. Ihe
small quantities of food I did eat caused
great irritability of the stomach, and sick-
ness and vomiting were quite common.
After each meal, however light, I felt nasty
colicky pains, and the least pressure on the
stomach produced tenderness in a very
marked degree. I was often troubled with
heart-burn, and also pains in the right
side, near the edge of the ribs, and under
the shoulder blade. My bow*ls became in-
active, very likely through my sedentary
occupation. I used to be a particularly
healthy man, but so much confinement and
sitting down commenced to show their evil
oonaequences. Oppressive, throbbing pains
became apparent, and they were always in-
creased by moving about, or by strange
noises. If I took a walk along the street I
got awfully giddy, and my eyes were not
strong enough to stand the light of day.
Sometimes my headaches were so violent,
and my eyes so bad, that I had to knock off
work, and then lie down in a fit of despair.
I,was in such a state of excessive exhaustion
that I was always ready to lie down and go
to sleep, although I slept badly. Many a
night have I turned and tossed without
closing my eyes, and in the mornings I
arose feeling bad-tempered and fretful, and
with a disinclination for any exertion, or
even my ordinary work. A sour taste in
the mouth made everything the same, and
a'bitter fluid used to arise from the stomach,
especially on getting up from bed. I was
also aware of the presence of a yellowish
fur upon th# tongue. Month after month
passed away, and I was losing flesh and
strength all the time. I had got so weak at
last that I would sometimes faint whilst
sitting down, without any exertion or
excitement. My nervous system had got
into such a morbid condition that I even
felt inclined to shrink from the light.
All the colour had gone from my face, and
my skin generally lost its natural appear-
ance. The doctors could not make my case
out at all, as there was no doubt that my
condition was very serious. I could not
bear the thought of living on much longer
in that way, because all my faculties were
much affected by my sufferings. ”
“ You were very despondent, perhaps?”
“ Well, I think I liad every reason to be,
afte; laking pills and medicines galore,
Witno.it reaping any b nefit from them. I
was prevailed upon by my si-ter to throw
the doctor’s treatment aside, and go in for
& course of. Clements Tonic. ‘ I know so
many who have been cured by it that I am
sure it will do you good,’ she said. W ell, i
thought she might be right, and such after-
wards proved to be case. You can hardly
imagine how quickly it picked me up, for
in a week or so I could eat anything with-
out fear of vomiting it up again. My
tongue became healthy and clean, and the
sourness soon departed fiom my mouth.
My food no longer provoked those agonis-
ing paroxysms of pain in my stomach, as
Clements Tonic seemed to set my internal
organs properly to work. How differently I
looked at life when I could rise in the morn-
ing feeling refreshed after a peaceful sleep.
The medicine I was now taking drove away
all the sluggishness from my liver, and it
had the effect of making my bowels more
regular in their action. With my strength
coming back, I could go about my work
with a feeling of cheerfulness, instead of
the distastefulness which had for seven
years attended my exertions. The pains in
the head, which used to prostrate me with
their violence, were cured, and my eyesight
was as good as before my sickness came on.
The plainest of food was a luxury, and my
ravenous appetite soon made me get stout
again. When I had taken the fourth
bottle of Clements Tonic you would not
have thought I was the same man. All my
nervousness had been thrown off, and the
pallid face I had worn so many years now
looked a healthy, bright colour. I could
•take a walk without fearing those attacks
of dizziness which had previously ac-
companied me when out for exercise, and
the fainting fits never came on again. 1
have to thank my sister for her advice, and
Clements Tonic for its matchless capabilities
as a health restorer. Without it lam sure
I should have dragged along the road of
life without enough strength or spirit to
make my existence endurable ; but now I
jog along quite happy and contented, feel-
ing myself relieved from my burden of
suffering. If ever I feel a little out of sorts
through too long continued labour, or
through the close confinement and lack of
exercise which my business involves, I
always have Clements Tonic at hand, and it
has never failed to do me good. You can,
therefore, quite understand how much con-
fidence I have in it, and that is the reason I
do not begrudge the time taken up in this
interview.
“ And may we draw the attention of the
public to your illness and cure?”
“I shall be glad if you will do so. be-
cause somebody may read it with advantage,
and you can publish what I say in any way
you like.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Thomas Bradt, of 47 Gleamore-road, Padding-
ton, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of three folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to three, and
that it conttins and is a I rue and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiv-
ing any payment; and I make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intituled ‘‘An Act for the more effectual abolition
of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the
various Departments of the Government of New South
Wales, and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof,
and for the suppression of voluntary and Extra-
judicial Oaths and Affidavits."
Declared at Paddington, this 9th day of October,
one thousand nine hundred, before me,
OHAS CAMPBELL. J.P.

Bruising the Serpent’s Head.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her hero arrived on horseback, as heroes and
bushmen are wont to do. And the manly young
fellow had earned her undying gratitude, trust,
and love, by offering her a home a rough busb
home, certainly, but still a home.
To the weary Eve it seemed probable that a
vigorous young Adam could transform even a
forest selection into some sort of a Paradise. With
a sweet woman to help him, Adam had no doubt
whatever on the subject; he was sure of it. In
any case, it was clear to Eve that almost anything
was to be preferred to the stuffy pleasures of the
ribbon-and-lace department of Greytown s general
and galvanised-iron store.
“ It’ll be a bit lonely for you, Eve,” said Adam.
“ But your mother could come and stay while I m
away shearing.”
And so the Paradise was planted. To the
casual passer-by it was just an ordinary bush hut,
stuck anyhow in the middle of a partially-cleared
patch, surrounded by a rough-and-ready fork-
and-spar fence, and fringed by “rung” timber;
but casual passers-by thereabouts were very casual
and very unpoetic. To Adam and Eve it was full
of rose-coloured blossoms of hope, presaging a fine
fruitage, regardless of seasons or rain or drought.
They were young and loved each other worship-
fully.
It was nearly two years before the serpent crept
in. It came in the guise of Adam’s curiosity.
Surfeited with the sweets of the present, he must
needs gamble with the future by probing the
past. And one night, out of the fulness of her
love and trust, she, being closely questioned,
admitted something that no woman ever freely
forgives herself for admitting. .
A great terror filled her soul as she felt him
shrink from her side and lie very still; but it was
too late for all but silent tears. Her pillow was
wet when daylight came ; but his was cold, bho
had heard him get up and dress and take his
bridle from the wall. She knew as though she
could see, just where lie had yarded the horses.
As he came in later for his saddle and a hurriedly-
collected pack she strove to speak, but the plead-
ing whisper refused to leave her lips. vVhen in
the dark she heard him go to the cot and kiss their
baby boy it was in her mind to cry out to him in
her agony, but her heart told her it would be use-
less. The rattle of the slip-rails being let down
in the grey, damp dawn smote on her ears like a
knell. She wondered vaguely if he would look
back as he rode away down the rough track, but
put the liodo from her as an unholy thought.
Yet, with his foot irresolutely in the stirrup, lie
did look back. Had she shown herself at the
door with the boy in her arms, he had, perhaps,
coms back, and ? .
Her mother arrived before evening. The store-
keeper’s cart fetched her. The man told Eve he
had been instructed to take her orders for what-
ever was required on the selection.
“Adam’s gone shearin’ early this year, he re-
marked. “ Must be goin’ a good long ways ? ”
“Yes,” she answered, slowly, “he’s going a
long way this year, I expect.”
Adam had told the mother nothing; had just
called and asked her to go out to the selection,
and stay awhile, and had ridden off with his usual
grave, “ So-long ! ” So there was no need for Eve
to make explanations; it was not very extraordi-
nary that a shearer-seleotor should start north wards
a month earlier, or later, than usual; for, in the
land of long distances, shearers go out to meet the
springtime, and may travel far with the season
that suits. , . ~ ~ ,
No breath of scandal, therefore, was wafted
down to the township from the lonely home up
under the Sawpit Ranges, and no ill was sus-
pected by the distant neighbors. Adam counted
all this as good fortune, for, as yet, he was by no
means certain that he wished the world to know
that he had left his wife and child for ever. In
fact, he hardly cared to admit any such thing,
even to himself. Procrastination thrives with
most Australians, and Adam was no exception.
He was urged onwards in his solitary ride by a
notion that he would be less a man had he stayed
with Eve after her confession, but, as lie rode, a
thousand tender memories tugged at his heart-
strings, day-long and day-long, and made the
stodgy plains seem unutterably wide and dreary.
At night, by the lonely camp-fire, something that
was not rain often glistened on his sun-tanned
cheek. More than once, when he fancied he
heard his baby boy cry out in the night, lie had
suddenly started up from sleep, and had impul-
sively set out to catch the horses and saddle-up for
a return journey. Sometimes, in broad day, when
he imagined he could hear Eve saying, pleadingly :
“ It was such a long time ago, Adam, such a long
time ago,” he had surprised his horses by turning
their heads for home, then reining-up, undecided
whether to spur home to his loved ones, or con-
tinue his journey northwards to the early sheds.
He went on ; each day’s ride taking him further
from winter and nearer to spring. His first shed
had “ cut-out,” and a second was half-through ;
yet he was still undecided. Sooner or later he
must do something definite —that he knew; but
what? ,
It had become gradually clear to him that he
should not live with Eve again ; but what about
the child? Should he for ever cut himself off
from his fine baby boy, the little Adam, with his
father’s blue eyes, and his mother’s brown curls?
Could he part them ; had ho the right or the
heart to do it? A great pity for them and a
greater for himself surged up in his heart when he
thought of it all; they had been so happy
together.
He would write to Eve one of these days, telling
her he was not coming back to her any more, and
advising her to go and live with her mother in
Grey town, and he would get an agent to sell the
selection and pay her the money. He would tell
her she could keep the boy for the present, until
such time as another home could be found for
him, “somewhere a long way off, so that he might
grow up without knowing what sort of girl his
mother had been.” In his mind he drafted the
letter often ; thinking it out, painfully, word by
word, and sentence by sentence, many times
changing the tone and the wording, but always
winding up with a reference to the child and “the
sort of a girl his mother had been.” And it was
just that particular phrase which caused most of
the delay. Adam was not satisfied with it; it
seemed rather a cruel thing to say to the girl he so
loved, and who had mothered his child ; and yet —
and yet, it was surely necessary. No other words
he could think of so plumbed the depths of his
self-pity, and at the same time conveyed the
merited rebuke to her in whom he had been so
sadly deceived. At shearing sheep Adam was far
more expert than in the making of phrases and
the writing of letters; so he kept on shearing
sheep and postponed the letter-writing.
But a day came when there could be mo longer
delay. The season had brought the shearers
southward to within twenty miles of Greytown,
and but a short day’s ride from Adam’s own selec-
tion.
“ S’pose you’ll be making for home safternoon,
Adam?” casually remarked his pen-mate, during
the first Saturday forenoon they were at Brenda.
Adam hesitated ; his mate’s remark came as
somewhat of a shock. He bent low over his work
to hide his confusion, eventually growling some-
thing to the effect that he “supposed he’d take a
ride home one of these Saturdays to see if the
selection was in the same place.”
“I’m blanky sure about you,” chaffed the
“ hard-case ”of the shed. “I’ll bet the big brown
won’t be so fresh this time to-morrow. None of
yous married blokes ought to be let own a decent
prad.”
“ It’ll feel like gettin’ married all over again,
won’t it, Adam ? ” chipped another, slyly.
So the chaff went on, and Adam realised that,
to avoid pointed remark, he would have to at
once arrange his domestic affairs on a basis that
would at least appear satisfactory.
At “ smoke-ho ” ho slipped down to the hut and
wrote the oft-considered letter. After a brief but
frantic cudgelling of his brains for a more suitable
phrase, he concluded with —“You may keep the
boy until I can take him where he will never learn
the sort of girl his mother has been.” He left the
letter with the cook and returned to his work.
As the shearers filed irregularly from the shed
at mid-day, their work over for the week, Adam
saw the mailman disappearing in a thin cloud of
dust far across the plain. He felt that he had
never known real sorrow in his life until then.
In the early afternoon he sat on the wood-heap
before the hut door and gloomily watched the
other shearers go off, saddles on arms, to the
stockyard. They were off on a twenty-mile ride
for such Saturday-night excitement as Greytown
afforded. *
“ Ain’t yer cornin’, Adam?” queried one, care-
lessly. Adam shook his head, and muttered
something about having sent a letter, and that lie
would be going home “ perhaps next Saturday.
“You can leave the brown horse in the yard,
though,” he added. “I’ll go over and have a
look at him directly.”
“ Righto ! ” And the noisy crowd were soon
racing off towards the township, as light-hearted
as though the twenty miles were but as many
Adam watched them out of sight, and the lump
in his throat grew big as he remembered that
he. too, might have been galloping with them,
each stride of his big brown horse taking lnm
nearer to all that he had ever really loved.
But it was better as it was; he had done the
manly thing and would have been a fool and a
weakling to have done otherwise. He would not
think any more about it. . ,
Full of this laudable resolve, he strolled into the
hut and casually scanned a fortnight-old news-
paper which the mailman had left for the cook.
Under the heading “Greytown; from our own
correspondent,” he came across an account of a
severe storm that had disturbed the serenity of the
district recently, and had been particularly
devastating out towards the Sawpit Ranges.
Trees had been uprooted and smashed to pieces
by lightning, roofs blown off, and many settlers
homes totally destroyed. “So far as is yet
known,” concluded the correspondent, there
has been no loss of life, though some of the far-
back settlers had most terrible experiences.
Adam dropped the paper as though it burnt
him, stood a second as in doubt, then swiftly but
methodically taking his saddle from the tie-beam,
stalked over to the stockyard. In five minutes
the brown horse danced out of the yard on his
hind feet, and in five more was plunging out past
the homestead gates, bearing a white-faced man
with burning eyes, along the straightest track to
Greytown.
A few miles had been cast behind ere the horse,
fresh and fiery and eager for home, began to steady
down to his work. The dust raised by the
roystering horsemen of the early afternoon still
hung like a soft haze in the sunset and caked in
white sweat-lines along the big brown’s neck and
flanks. Then Adam recollected that twenty
miles at racing speed was rather much to expect
from even the best grass-fed horse in the district.
And where was the hurry ? He only wished to
incjuire at the township how the storm had treated
his bush home, and if his wife and child had
escaped unhurt. Still he checked the horse but
little, and the township lights began to twinkle
ahead with the first of the summer stars. _
As he rode into the place it seemed to him that
he had been away five years ; but the hilarious
voices of his Brenda shod-mates, raised in tuneless
chorus as he passed the first of Greytown s
“jpubs,” reminded him that it was shearing-time,
and but five months had passed since he had said
“ So-long ! ” to his wife’s mother at the cottage
gate yonder.
The cottage was in darkness now, so he would
learn no news there. He would casually inquire
at the general store and post-office.
“ Hello, Adam !” shouted the cheery old store-
keeper, as the young man with his arm linked
through the bridle-rein looked in at the open door.
“ Some o’ the Brenda chaps was here ’safternoon,
and told me you was out there, and wouldn’t be
going home till next Saturday. I gave em a
message for you. S’pose you haven’t heard about
your wife and kiddy ? My word, they had a
narrow squeak for it! ” .
Adam staggered against the door-post, but the
old storekeeper failed to notice his visitor s agita-
tion in the murky light, and rattled on volubly :
“ The night of the big storm the kiddy took real
bad, and the missus started for here by the short
track across the spur, carryin’ the youngster. It
was a terrible night, and she lost her way in the
storm. But she battled on somehow, and, more
dead’ll alive, got to the doctor’s door, down the
street, at daybreak. Only just in time, though :
Doc says the kiddy would have been past help m
another hour ; diphtheria or sumthin’. Anyway,
they’re both pretty right now, and the doctor took
’em ’ome in his dog-cart only yesterday.”
Before the storekeeper had finished his story
Adam’s foot was in the stirrup.
He looked over his shoulder to ask: VVliat
time did the Gully letters go out?” „
“ Let me see, now ; Splitting Jack took em,
said the other. “It must have been about—-.
I noticed there was one for your folks; can tbe
more’n an hour ago .” .
Adam waited to hear no more. Flinging bim-
self to the saddle, he touched the horse sharply
with the spurs. The gravel thrown from the four
startled hoofs rattled on the iron front of the store
like a sharp volley of .hailstones, and . the next
moment the big brown was thundering down the
half-lighted street into the darkness of the bush
beyond. Just clear of the township, Adam swung
the horse round on to a faint track, which was
locally known as the “ short-cut ” over the spur—
the same short-cut that his wife, tattered and
wind-swept and despairing, had fought her way

through to save her ohild, and his, on that terrible
night.
Never was so risky a ride ridden at such a pace;
never had the good brown horse and his deter-
mined rider had such a task ; never would either
have faced it had daylight disclosed the dreadful
dangers. The storm-king had sent the forest into
wreckage. Splintered timber and loose rocks lay
piled disorderly in every direction, and yawning
chasms were torn by the flood-waters across the
narrow siding track.
One idea possessed the horseman, one idea urged
him on. Several times had the horse stumbled to
his knees, but, trembling with terror and blown
with exertion, had recovered, and stood reeking in
the darkness, while his master rushed hither and
thither afoot to find a way round the temporary
obstruction of fallen timbers. Whether tearing
aside tangled branches with bleeding hands, or
clinging with thighs a-stride to the saddle while
the brown horse plunged onwards along the moun-
tain side, Adam kept repeating to himself : “My
God ! and she battled through this ! She battled
through this in the storm for the sake of my kid !”
It was his fixed intention to get to the selection
before the fatal letter was delivered, or die in the
attempt. There seemed no other alternative.
At last, the crest of the last ridge was reached,
and Adam raced the big brown down the now
comparatively clear slope at top speed. Horse
and man had been bred in the bush ; that gave
them their only chance for life. And they won
through, striking the track within a few chains of
the selection slip-rails.
The rails were down. He was, then, too late,
for surely Splitter Jack had passed in with the
letter, and Eve had already received the cruel
blow. A bend in the track hid the hut from
sight, but as Adam leaped from the back of the
now utterly-foundered horse, and ran stumblingly
along, a band of light shone from the open door,
revealing the old man in the very act of handing
Eve the fatal missive. He saw her turn quickly
and hold the face of the envelope to the light,
then swiftly press it to her lips. Then, with
trembling hands, she tore it open, but, bethinking
her of the messenger, turned to thank him. But
the old man was staring with startled eyes down
the track, and his horse was snorting in alarm at
an apparition advancing swiftly towards them.
The next moment the horse was tugging his
swearing owner half-way across the paddock by
the bridie-reins, and Eve was hysterically sobbing
in her husband’s arms. Presently she raised her
tearful eyes questioningly to his.
“Where’s the boy ?” he asked, huskily.
For answer she led him to the cot, where the
little fellow lay fast asleep. Adam stooped and
kissed him. As he straightened himself, Eve
again questioned him with her eyes.
“ You saved our boy,” he said, simply. “ That’s
good enough for me.”
Then, as he took her in his arms, his hand
closed firmly over the letter she still held. And
she never got a chance to read it.
♦ m * »
Tt was three days before Adam, on foot, but
looking five years younger, got back to Brenda
woolshed. He cheerfully explained to all hands
that ho had lost the big brown horse.
“Tt don’t seem to trouble you much, anyway,”
remarked Hardcase.
“ No,” replied Adam, Perhaps I’d grieve a lot
more at losing a worse horse.”
“ I'm blanky sure ! ” said Hardcase, ironically.
But Adam only laughed.

JOHN DRAYMAN.
The Interpreter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had reached that stage when a man regards
the woman he has once loved as an episode of his
past.
She, feeling he was slipping away from her,
suddenly realised she loved him. So, with the
cussedness of woman, who only values what she
can’t get, she longed once more to subdue him.
That was why she encouraged him to talk of
Miss Thwaites, though jealousy gnawed at her
heart all the time.
Yet it pleased her to feel that although she was
married to an old fossil (as she celled Jem) she
was attractive enough to make a good-looking
man walk two miles every night to see her.
But Morton wanted a confidante, and force of
habit led his footsteps to the Bungalow. Jem
was always at the club, but Mrs. Jem was always
at home in the evening—to him. She was such a
good listener, too, and it was jolly out there on
the verandah watching the moon rise, and smoking
Jem’s excellent cigars, sometimes he said more
than he intended—at least, Mis. Jem thought so.
Women seldom give men credit for subtlety, yet
the men who are loved the most passionately are
all slightly feline.
To-night Morton spoke first.
“ Miss Thwaites has gone for a trip to Sydney.
I went to see her off this afternoon, and give her
some flowers.
Mrs. Jem writhed inwardly, but answered
calmly “Yes; why not?”
“Well, you see, the girl at the shop where I
ordered them was a bit of a fool, and must have
given me someone else’s bouquet; there was a little
bunch of forget-me-nots in the centre. I never
noticed them till it was too late, and I’ve been
wondering ever since what Miss Thwaites would
think ? ”
Like a woman, Mrs. Jem jumped to a conclu-
sion ; it was all right; he was still hers. The
darkness hid her smiles, but she said reproach-
fully: “Oh, Jack, you shouldn’t have done it;
the poor girl will think you meant something.
She’ll be expecting a proposal next.”
“Eh? damn it all!” said Morton ; but Mrs. Jem
smiled.
* « * *
It was Mrs. Jem’s At-Home Day. Only one
visitor remained —Dora Desmond, an old school-
fellow.
“ Did you hear what they were saying about
Jack Morton ? ” she asked.
“Yes, that he and Miss Thwaites were going to
make a match of it; but we know better than
that, don’t we, Dora dear ? ”
Dora smiled. “ Why, I thought you were tired
of him ? ”
It was a polite way of saying just the reverse,
but women are sometimes polite to each other;
also, Mrs. Jem kimw all about Dora’s love affairs.
“Aren’t you?” i orap rsisted with raised eye-
brows.
“No, not quite. Listen then ’’—she told Dora
of the “parting-bouquet episode,” adding triumph-
antly—“ So you see there’s nothing in it.”
Dora looked grave. “ Don’t you be too sure—
shall I tell you why he asked you what the girl
would think ? ”
“Yes; why?”
“ Because he did mean something and he
wanted one woman to tell him what another would
think ! ”
* • • *
Six weeks after Miss Thwaites said “ Yes.”
PLYMOUTH SOUND.
North-West.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are stars in the seas and skies-
Red jewels aloft and under—
And waves on the brown cliffs rise
A-swing to a song of thunder;
Off-shore ’neath a cold, clear moon
The surf on the reef is sprawling,
And ever a-through its tune
I dream there are voices calling—
The voice of the gaunt gums calling,
The song of the desert calling ;
From river and still lagoon
The heart of Australia calling.
The croon of a drowsy creek
That kisses a careworn river;
The bush and the west wind speak,
A-ripple with sun they quiver
The sunbeams sown in my blood,
And call to my heart to follow
The staggering seas that flood
The west with their long backs hollow.
Australia’s noon sighs “Follow,”
The song of her dawn shouts “ Follow
The wash of her tides a-flood
Sings low to my heart to follow.
Her sunsets burning the sands
And dyeing the white surf crimson,
Are dragging with passionate hands
A heart that their hot tide swims on.
There are stars in the seas afar,
But down in the west is glowing
A fairer and jsurer star
Than those in the dream-seas showing.
It burns where the seas are going—
Where often I dream I’m going—
North-west where the gray gums are
Like ghosts in the pale dawn showing.
M.L.
QUILP N.
His Reason.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man had been sitting on the pavement out-
side his house for three hours. It was two o’clock
in the morning and the usual sleepy guardian of
the law was approaching.
“What are you doing there?” asked the man
with the large feet, drawing near cautiously.
“Sitting-down,” said the man.
“Yes, I am aware of that fact.”
“Well, was it necessary to inquire?”
“Now then, young man, if you get insult-
ing you must move-on.”
“Oh ! I was under the impression this was my
own home, but perhaps I’m mistaken. Anyhow,
if it is, I’ve a right to sit here.”
“Oh, I beg pardon!” apologised the police-
man, drawing closer. “I didn’t recognise you.
What’s the matter —lost your key ?”
“ No.”
“ Left it in your other clothes, maybe ? ”
“No,” very curtly.
“ Too early to wake your mother up ? ”
“No.”
“Waiting for the milkman, p’raps?” ventured
the officer.
“ No.”
“ Expecting a letter from your best girl, eh ? ”
“No.”
“Trying to catch the person who steals your
morning paper ? ”
“ No,” more sharply than ever.
“Want to get an appetite for breakfast, pos-
sibly?”
“No.”
“ P’raps, maybe, want to get cool before going
to bed ? ” chuckled the policeman. (The thermo-
meter registered one point below zero.)
“No ! What the blanky——”
“ Are you,” began the policeman, but he was
interrupted.
“Look here,” said the Problem. “Have I to
reveal to you the sacred secrets of my household?”
“No,” said the policeman uneasily.
“Must I inform you of my domestic infelici-
ties ? ”
“No.”
“Do I—must I tell you my loving wife is
standing on the stairs with a poker in one hand
and a broom in the other, waiting for my appear-
ance?”
“ No.”
“ And that she has declared war ? ”
“ Eh !—what ? ” stammered the policeman.
“Now then,” said the man in a commanding
tone, “depart and attend to your own business.
I’ll sit here for a month if I like.”
And the policeman, actually abashed and dis-
concerted, apologised and went on his _ way,
eagerly surveying each door-step, in his en-
deavor to secure a door-mat, that lie might be able
to refresh himself with a comfortable sleep in
the most convenient place.
JONATHAN R.
That recent decision of U.S. Supreme
Court (by five Judges to four) that the Consti-
tution does not follow the flag, marks a big
departure in American policy. It simply signi-
fies that the hitherto free Republic is weary of
the r6le she has played since the Civil War, and is
prepared to again step forward as a slave-peopled
Empire. The decision was, of course, aimed at
the Filippinos.
The Riverine Herald (Echuca, Vic.)
thus lays around with its moral cowhide :
Some of the Melbourne young women can drink. I
mean the bare necked, bare-armed, bare-breasted and
pasted young women, who were going the rounds of the
Government festivities during the week—-the Parliament-
opening, concerts, conversaziones, at homes, Flemington
review luncheon, and such like. Some of them, many of
them, can put away champagne with any bookmaker
celebrating a Cup. Some of these half-dressed and three-
quarter dressed charmers were to be seen by whoever
liked to look at them scoffing the Government champagne
at the rate of two or three glasses per 10 or 15 minutes.
“N.” : Illustrated London News pic-
tures King Edward distributing the Maundy
Thursday bounty, or rather the procession filing
up to King Edward. Surely an enemy hath done
this! Maudlin Thursday! The good monarch, with
holy benevolence exuding from every pore of his
face, is supposed to stand where we do in looking
at picture. There is a lane of pauper men And
women, venerables with beaming loyalty on their
gnarled phizzes. They represent—what percen-
tage of London’s pauperdom ? Archbishop and
bishop march in procession. A gorgeous beef-
eater carries the Maundy Thursday dole on a gold
alver. Faugh ! Likewise woosh !
